<start> HUMAN , ALL TOO HUMANPART I . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> I . <end>
<start> To the Disillusioned in Philosophy . <end>
<start> If you hitherto believed in the highest value of life and now find yourselves disillusioned , must you immediately get rid of life at the lowest possible price ? <end>
<start> venice . <end>
<start> One can even become venice as regards the clearness of concepts . <end>
<start> How disgusted one is then at having truck with the half clear , the hazy , the aspiring , the doubting ! <end>
<start> How ridiculous and yet not mirth provoking is their eternal fluttering and straining without ever being able to fly or to grasp ! <end>
<start> The workers of Reality . <end>
<start> He who realises at last how long and how thoroughly he has been fooled , embraces out of spite even the ugliest reality . <end>
<start> So that in the long run of the world s history the best men have always been workers of reality , for the best have always been longest and most thoroughly deceived . <end>
<start> Advance of free-thinking . <end>
<start> The difference between past and present free-thinking cannon better be characterised than by that aphorism for the recognition and expression of which all the fearlessness of the eighteenth century was needed , and which even then , if measured by our modern view , sinks into an unconscious naivete . <end>
<start> I mean Voltaire s aphorism , crowe moi , mon ami , l error aussi a son merit . <end>
<start> A Hereditary Sin of Philosophers . <end>
<start> Philosophers have at all times appropriated arid corrupted the maxims of censors of men moralists , by taking them over without qualification and trying to prove as necessary what the moralists only meant as a rough indication or as a truth suited to their fellow-countrymen or fellow townsmen for a single decade . <end>
<start> Moreover , the philosophers thought that they were thereby raising themselves above the moralists ! <end>
<start> Thus it will be found that the celebrated teachings of Schopenhauer as to the supremacy of the will over the intellect , of the immutability of character , the negativity of pleasure all errors , in the sense in which he understands them rest upon principles of popular wisdom enunciated by the moralists . <end>
<start> Take the very word will , which Schopenhauer twisted so as to become a common denotation of several human conditions and with which he filled a gap in the language to his own great advantage , in so far as he was a moralist , for he became free to speak of the will as Pascal had spoken of it . <end>
<start> In the hands of its creator , Schopenhauer s will , through the philosophic craze for generalisation , already turned out to be a bane to knowledge . <end>
<start> For this will was made into a poetic metaphor , when it was held that all things in nature possess will . <end>
<start> Finally , that it might be applied to all kinds of disordered mysticism , the word was misused by a fraudulent convention . <end>
<start> So now all our fashionable philosophers repeat it and seem to be perfectly certain that all things have a will and are in fact One Will . <end>
<start> According to the description generally given of this All One Will , this is much as if one should positively try to have the stupid Devil for one s God . <end>
<start> Against Visionaries . <end>
<start> The visionary denies the truth to himself , the liar only to others . <end>
<start> Enmity to LiGHT . <end>
<start> If we make it clear to any one that , strictly , he can never speak of truth , but only of probability and of its degrees , we generally discover , from the undisguised joy of our pupil , how greatly men prefer the uncertainty of their intellectual horizon , and how in their heart of hearts they hate truth because of its definiteness . <end>
<start> Is this due to a secret fear felt by all that the light of truth may at some time be turned too brightly upon themselves ? <end>
<start> To their wish to be of some consequence , and accordingly their concealment from the world of what they are ? <end>
<start> Or is it to be traced to their horror of the ail too brilliant light , to which their crepuscular , easily dazzled , bat like souls are not accustomed , so that hate it they must ? <end>
<start> Christian Scepticism . <end>
<start> Pilate , with his question , What is Truth ? <end>
<start> is now gleefully brought on the scene as an advocate of Christ , in order to cast suspicion on all that is known or knowable as being mere appearance , and to erect the Cross on the appalling background of the Impossibility of Knowledge . <end>
<start> Natural Law , a Phrase of Superstition . <end>
<start> When you talk so delightedly of Nature acting according to law , you must either assume that all things in Nature follow their law from a voluntary obedience imposed by themselves in which case you admire the morality of Nature : or you are enchanted with the idea of a creative mechanical , who has made a most cunning watch with human beings as accessory ornaments . <end>
<start> Necessity , through the expression , conformity to law , then becomes more human and a coin of refuge in the last instance for mythological reveries . <end>
<start> Fallen Forfeit to History . <end>
<start> All misty philosophers and obscures of the world , in other words all metaphysician of coarse or refined textureMISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> I ? <end>
<start> are seized with earache , earache , and toothache when they begin to suspect that there is truth in the saying : All philosophy has from now fallen forfeit to history . <end>
<start> In view of their aches and pains we may pardon them for throwing stones and filth at him who talks like this , but this teaching may itself thereby become dirty and disreputable for a time and lose in effect . <end>
<start> II . <end>
<start> The Pessimist of the Intellect . <end>
<start> He whose intellect is really free will think freely about the intellect itself , and will not shut his eyes to certain terrible aspects of its source and tendency . <end>
<start> For this reason others will perhaps designate him the bitterest opponent of free thought and give him that dreadful , abusive name of pessimist of the intellect : accustomed as they are to typify a man not by his strong point , his pre eminent virtue , but by the quality that is most foreign to his nature . <end>
<start> The metaphysician Knapsack . <end>
<start> To all who talk so boastful of the scientific basis of their metaphysics it is best to make no reply . <end>
<start> It is enough to tug at the bundle that they rather shyly keep hidden behind their backs . <end>
<start> If one succeeds in lifting it , the results of that scientific basis come to light , to their great confusion : a dear little God , a genteel immortality , perhaps a little spiritualism , and in any case complicated mass of poor sinners misery and pharisee arrogance . <end>
<start> Occasional carefulness of Knowledge . <end>
<start> The utility involved in the unchecked investigation of knowledge is so constantly proved in a hundred different ways that one must remember to include in the bargain the subtler and rarer damage which individuals must suffer on that account . <end>
<start> Thechemist cannon avoid occasionally being poisoned or burnt at his experiments . <end>
<start> What applies to the chemist , is true of the whole of our culture . <end>
<start> This , it may be added , clearly shows that knowledge should provide itself with healing balsam against burns and should always have antidotes ready against poisons . <end>
<start> The Craving of the Philistine . <end>
<start> The Philistine thinks that his most urgent need is a purple patch or turban of metaphysics , nor will he let it slip . <end>
<start> Yet he would look less ridiculous without this adornment . <end>
<start> ENTHUSIASTS . <end>
<start> With all that enthusiasts say in favour of their gospel or their master they are defending themselves , however much they comfort themselves as the judges and not the accused : because they are involuntarily reminded almost at every moment that they are exceptions and have to assert their legitimacy . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> The Good Seduces to Life . <end>
<start> All good things , even all good books that are written against life , are strong means of attraction to life . <end>
<start> The Happiness of the Historian . <end>
<start> When we hear the hair splitting metaphysician and prophets of the after world speak , we others feel indeed that we are the poor in spirit , but that ours is the heavenly kingdom of change , with spring and autumn , summer and winter , and theirs the afterward , with its grey , everlasting frosts and shadows . <end>
<start> Thus soliloquies a man as he walked in the morning sunshine , a man who in his pursuit of history has constantly changed not only his mind but his heart . <end>
<start> In contrast to the metaphysician , he is happy to harbour in himself not an immortal soul but many mortal souls . <end>
<start> Three Varieties of Thinkers . <end>
<start> There are streaming , flowing , trickling mineral springs , and three corresponding varieties of thinkers . <end>
<start> The layman values them by the volume of the water , the expert by the contents of the water in other words , by the elements in them that are not water . <end>
<start> The Picture of Life . <end>
<start> The task of painting the picture of life , often as it has been attempted by poets and philosophers , is nevertheless irrational . <end>
<start> Even in the hands of the greatest artist thinkers , pictures and miniatures of one life only their own have come into being , and indeed no other result is possible . <end>
<start> While in the process of developing , a thing that develops , cannon mirror itself as fixed and permanent , as a definite object . <end>
<start> Truth will have no Gods before it . <end>
<start> The belief in truth begins with the doubt of all truths in which one has previously believed . <end>
<start> Where Silence is Required . <end>
<start> If we speak of free-thinking as of a highly dangerous journey over glaciers and frozen seas , we find that those who do not care to travel on this track are offended , as if they had been reproached with cowardice and weak knees . <end>
<start> The difficult , which we find to be beyond our powers , must not even be mentioned in our presence . <end>
<start> Historia in nice . <end>
<start> The most serious parody I ever heard was this : In the beginning was the nonsense , and the nonsense was with God , and the nonsense was God . <end>
<start> Incurable . <end>
<start> The idealist is incorrigible : if he be thrown out of his Heaven , he makes himself a suitable ideal out of Hell . <end>
<start> Disillusion him , and lo ! <end>
<start> he will embrace disillusionment with no less ardour than he recently embraced hope . <end>
<start> In so far as his impulse belongs to the great incurable impulses of human nature , he can bring about tragic destinies and later become a subject for tragedy himself , for such tragedies as deal with the incurable , implacable , inevitable in the lot and character of man . <end>
<start> Applause Itself as the Continuation of the Play . <end>
<start> Sparkling eyes and an amiable smile are the tributes of applause paid to all the great comedy of world and existence but this applause is a comedy within a comedy , meant to tempt the other spectators to a plaudits amiri . <end>
<start> Courage for Tedium . <end>
<start> He who has not the courage to allow himself and his work to be considered tedious , is certainly no intellect of the first rank , whether in the arts or in the sciences . <end>
<start> A scoffed , who happened for once in a way to be a thinker , might add , with a glance at the world and at history : God did not possess this courage , for he wanted to make and he made all things so interesting . <end>
<start> From the Most Intimate Experience of the Thinker . <end>
<start> Nothing is harder for a man than to conceive of an object impersonally , I mean to see in it an object and not a person . <end>
<start> One may even ask whether it is possible for him to dispense for a single moment with the machinery of his instinct to create and construct a personality . <end>
<start> After all , he associates with his thoughts , however abstract they may be , as with individuals , against whom he must fight or to whom he must attach himself , whom he must protect , support and nourish . <end>
<start> Let us watch or listen to ourselves at the moment when we hear or discover a new idea . <end>
<start> Perhaps it displeased us because it is so defiant and so autocratic , and we unconsciously ask ourselves whether we cannon place a contradiction of it by its side as an enemy , or fasten on to it a perhaps or a sometimes : the mere little word probably gives us a feeling of satisfaction , for it shatters the oppressive tyranny of the unconditional . <end>
<start> If , on the other hand , the new idea enters in gentle shape , sweetly patient and humble , and falling at once into the arms of contradiction , we put our autocracy to the test in another way . <end>
<start> Can we not come to the aid of this weak creature , stroke it and feed it , give it strength and fullness , and truth and even unconditionally ? <end>
<start> Is it possible for us to show ourselves parental or chivalrous or compassionate towards our idea ? <end>
<start> Then again , we see here a judgment and there a judgment , sundered from each other , never looking at or making any move ment towards each other . <end>
<start> So we are tickled by the thought , whether it be not here feasible to make a match , to draw a conclusion , with the anticipation that if a consequence follows this conclusion it is not only the two judgments united in wedlock but the matchmakers that will gain honour . <end>
<start> If , however , we cannon acquire a hold upon that thought either on the path of defiance and ill will or on that of good will if we hold it to be true then we submit to it and do homage to it as a leader and a prince , give it a chair of honour , and speak not of it without a flourish of trumpets : for we are bright in its brightness . <end>
<start> Woe to him who tries to dim this brightness ! <end>
<start> Perhaps we ourselves one day grow suspicious of our idea . <end>
<start> Then we , the indefatigable king makers of the history of the intellect , cast it down from its throne and immediately exalt its adversary . <end>
<start> Surely if this be considered and thought out a little further , no one will speak of an absolute impulse to knowledge ! <end>
<start> Why , then , does man prefer the true to the untrue , in this secret combat with thought personalities , in this generally clandestine match making of thoughts , constitution founding of thoughts , childrearing of thoughts , nursing and of thoughts ? <end>
<start> For the same reason that he practises honesty in intercourse with real persons : now from habit , heredity , and training , originally because the true , like the fair and the just , is more expedient and more reputable than the untrue . <end>
<start> For in the realm of thought it is difficult to assume a power and glory that are built on error or on falsehood . <end>
<start> The feeling that such an edifice might at some time collapse is humiliating to the self esteem of the architect he is ashamed of the fragility of the material , and , as he considers himself more important than the rest of the world , he would fain construct nothing that is less durable than the rest of the world . <end>
<start> In his longing for truth he embraces the belief in a personal immortality , the most arrogant and defiant idea that exists , closely allied as it is to the underlying thought , great fundus , dum ego salvos sim ! <end>
<start> His work has become his ego , he transforms himself into the Imperishable with its universal challenge . <end>
<start> It is his immeasurable pride that will only employ the best and hardest stones for the work truths , or what he holds for such . <end>
<start> Arrogance has always been justly called the vice of the sage yet without this vice , fruitful in impulses , Truth and her status on earth would be in a parlous plight . <end>
<start> In our propensity to fear our thoughts , concepts and words , and yet to honour ourselves in them , unconsciously to ascribe to them the power of rewarding , despising , praising , and blaming us , and so to associate with them as with free intellectual personalities , as with independent powers , as with our equals herein lie the roots of the remarkable phenomenon which I have called intellectual conscience . <end>
<start> Thus something of the highest moral species has bloomed from a black root . <end>
<start> The obscurantist . <end>
<start> The essential feature of the black art of obscurantism is not its intention of clouding the brain , but its attempt to darken the picture of the world and cloud our idea of existence . <end>
<start> It often employs the method of thwarting all illumination of the intellect , but at times it uses the very opposite means , seeking by the highest refinement of the intellect to induce a satiety of the intellect s fruits . <end>
<start> Hair splitting metaphysician , who pave the way for scepticism and by their excessive acumen provoke a distrust of acumen , are excellent instruments of the more subtle form of obscurantism . <end>
<start> Is it possible that even Kant may be applied to this purpose ? <end>
<start> Did he even intend something of the sort , for a time at least , to judge from his own notorious exposition : to clear the way for belief by setting limitations to knowledge ? <end>
<start> Certainly he did not succeed , nor did his followers , on the wolf and fox tracks of this highly refined and dangerous form of obscurantism the most dangerous of all , for the black art here appears in the garb of light . <end>
<start> By what Kind of Philosophy Art is Corrupted . <end>
<start> When the mists of a philosophy succeed in making all aesthetic phenomena opaque , it follows that these phenomena cannon be comparatively valued , inasmuch as each becomes individually inexplicable . <end>
<start> But when once they cannon be compared for the sake of valuation , there arises an entire absence of criticism , a blind indulgence . <end>
<start> From this source springs a continual diminution of the enjoyment of art which is only distinguished from the crude satisfaction of a need by the highest refinement of taste and appreciation . <end>
<start> The more taste diminishes , the more does the desire for art change and revert to a vulgar hunger , which the artist henceforth seeks to appease by ever coarser fare . <end>
<start> On Gethsemane . <end>
<start> The most painful thing a thinker can say to artists is : Could ye not watch with me one hour ? <end>
<start> At the Loom . <end>
<start> There are many artists and women , for instance who work against the few that take a pleasure in untying the knot of things and unravelling their woof . <end>
<start> The former always want to weave the woof together again and entangle it and so turn the conceived into the if possible inconceivable . <end>
<start> Whatever the result may be , the woof and knot always look rather untidy , because too many hands are working and tugging at them . <end>
<start> In the Desert of Science . <end>
<start> As the man of science proceeds on his modest and noisome wanderings , which must often enough be journeys in the desert , he is confronted with those brilliant mirages known as philosophic systems . <end>
<start> With magic powers of deception they show him that the solution of all riddles and the most refreshing draught of true water of life are close at hand . <end>
<start> His weary heart rejoices , and he well nigh touches with his lips the goal of all scientific endurance and hardship , so that almost unconsciously he presses forward . <end>
<start> Other natures stand still , as if spellbound by the beautiful illusion : the desert swallows them up , they become lost to science . <end>
<start> Other natures , again , that have often experienced these subjective consolations , become very disheartened and curse the salty taste which these mirages leave behind in the mouth and from which springs a raging thirst without one s having come one step nearer to any sort of a spring . <end>
<start> The So called Real Reality . <end>
<start> When the poet depicts the various callings such as those of the warrior , the silk weaver , the sailor he reigns to know all these things thoroughly , to be an expert . <end>
<start> Even in the exposition of human actions and destinies he behaves as if he had been present at the spinning of the whole web of existence . <end>
<start> In so far he is an impostor . <end>
<start> He practises his frauds on pure ignoramus , and that is why he succeeds . <end>
<start> They praise him for his deep , genuine knowledge , and lead him finally into the delusion that he really knows as much as the individual experts and creators , yes , even as the great world spinners themselves . <end>
<start> In the end , the impostor becomes honest , and actually believes in his own sincerity . <end>
<start> Emotional people say to his very face that he has the higher truth and sincerity for they are weary of reality for the time being , and accept the poetic dream as a pleasant relaxation and a night s rest for head and heart . <end>
<start> The visions of the dream now appear to them of more value , because , as has been said , they find them more beneficial , and mankind has always held that what is apparently of more value is more true , more real . <end>
<start> All that is generally called reality , the poets , conscious of this power , proceed with intention to disparage and to distort into the uncertain , the illusory , the spurious , the impure , the sinful , sorrowful , and deceitful . <end>
<start> They make use of all doubts about the limits of knowledge , of all sceptical excesses , in order to spread over everything the rumpled veil of uncertainty . <end>
<start> For they desire that when this darkening process is complete their wizardry and soul magic may be accepted without hesitation as the path to true truth and real reality . <end>
<start> The Wish to be Just and the Wish to be A Judge . <end>
<start> Schopenhauer , whose profound understanding of what is human and all too human and original sense for facts was not a little impaired by the bright leopard skin of his metaphysic the skin must first be pulled off him if one wants to find the real moralist genius beneath Schopenhauermakes this admirable distinction , wherein he comes far nearer the mark than he would himself dare to admit : Insight into the stern necessity of human actions is the boundary line that divides philosophic from other brains . <end>
<start> He worked against that wonderful insight of which he was sometimes capable by the prejudice that he had in common with the moral man not the moralist , a prejudice that he expresses quite guileless and devoutly as follows : The ultimate and true explanation of the inner being of the entirety of things must of necessity be closely connected with that about the ethical significance of human actions . <end>
<start> This connection is not necessary at all : such a connection must rather be rejected by that principle of the stern necessity of human actions , that is , the unconditioned non freedom and non responsibility of the will . <end>
<start> Philosophic brains will accordingly be distinguished from others by their disbelief in the metaphysical significance of morality . <end>
<start> This must create between the two kinds of brain a gulf of a depth and of which the much deplored gulf between cultured and uncultured scarcely gives a conception . <end>
<start> It is true that many back doors , which the philosophic brains , like Schopenhauer s own , have left for themselves , must be recognised as useless . <end>
<start> None leads into the open , into the fresh air of the free will , but every door through which people had slipped hitherto showed behind it once more the gleaming brass wall of fate . <end>
<start> For we are in a prison , and can only dream of freedom , not make ourselves free . <end>
<start> That the recognition of this fact cannon be resisted much longer is shown by the despairing and incredible postures and grimaces of those who still press against it and continue their wrestling bout with it . <end>
<start> Their attitude at present is something like this : So no one is responsible for his actions ? <end>
<start> And all is full of guilt and the consciousness of guilt ? <end>
<start> But some one must be the sinner . <end>
<start> If it is no longer possible or permissible to accuse and sentence the individual , the one poor wave in the inevitable rough and tumble of the waves of development well , then , let this stormy sea , this development itself , be the sinner . <end>
<start> Here is free will : this totality can be accused and sentenced , can atone and expiate . <end>
<start> So let God be the sinner and man his redeemer . <end>
<start> Let the world s history be guilt , expiation , and self murder . <end>
<start> Let the evil doer be his own judge , the judge his own hangman . <end>
<start> This Christianity strained to its limits for what else is it ? <end>
<start> is the last thrust in the fencing match between the teaching of unconditioned morality and the teaching of unconditioned non freedom . <end>
<start> It would be quite horrible if it were anything more than a logical pose , a hideous grimace of the underlying thought , perhaps the death convulsion of the heart that seeks a remedy in its despair , the heart to which delirium whispers : Behold , thou art the lamb which taketh away the sin of God . <end>
<start> This error lies not only in the feeling , I am responsible , but just as much in the contradiction , I am not responsible , but some one must be . <end>
<start> That is simply not true . <end>
<start> Hence the philosopher must say , like Christ , Judge not , and the final distinction between the philosophic brains and the others would be that the former wish to be just and the latter wish to be judges . <end>
<start> Sacrifice . <end>
<start> You hold that sacrifice is the hallmark of moral action ? <end>
<start> Just consider whether in every action that is done with deliberation , in the best as in the worst , there be not a sacrifice . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> Against the Triers of the Reins of Morality . <end>
<start> One must know the best and the worst that a man is capable of in theory and in practice before one can judge how strong his moral nature is and can be . <end>
<start> But this is an experiment that one can never carry out . <end>
<start> Serpent s Tooth . <end>
<start> Whether we have a serpent s tooth or not we cannon know before some one has set his heel upon our necks . <end>
<start> A wife or a mother could say : until some one has put his heel upon the neck of our darling , our child . <end>
<start> Our character is determined more by the absence of certain experiences than by the experiences we have undergone . <end>
<start> Deception in Love . <end>
<start> We forget and purposely banish from our minds a good deal of our past . <end>
<start> In other words , we wish our picture , that beams at us from the past , to belie us , to flatter our vanity we are constantly engaged in this self deception . <end>
<start> And you who talk and boast so much of in love , of the absorption of the ego in the other person you hold that this is something different ? <end>
<start> So you break the mirror , throw yourselves into another personality that you admire , and enjoy the new portrait of your ego , though calling it by the other person s name and this whole proceeding is not to be thought self deception , self seeking , you marvellous beings ? <end>
<start> It seems to me that those who hide something of themselves from themselves , or hide their whole selves from themselves , are alike committing a theft from the treasury of knowledge . <end>
<start> It is clear , then , against what transgression the maxim Know thyself is a warning . <end>
<start> To the Denier of his Vanity . <end>
<start> He who denies his own vanity usually possesses it in so brutal a form that he instinctively shuts his eyes to avoid the necessity of despising himself . <end>
<start> Why the Stupid so often Become Malignant . <end>
<start> To those arguments of our adversary against which our head feels too weak our heart replies by throwing suspicion on the motives of his arguments . <end>
<start> The Art of Moral Exceptions . <end>
<start> An art that points out and glorifies the exceptional cases of morality where the good becomes bad and the unjust just should rarely be given a hearing : just as now and again we buy something from gipsies , with the fear that they are diverting to their own pockets much more than their mere profit from the purchase . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> Enjoyment and Non enjoyment of Poisons . <end>
<start> The only decisive argument that has always deterred men from drinking a poison is not that it is deadly , but that it has an unpleasant taste . <end>
<start> The World without Consciousness of Sin . <end>
<start> If men only committed such deeds as do not give rise to a bad conscience , the human world would still look bad and rascally enough , but not so sickly and pitiable as at present . <end>
<start> Enough wicked men without conscience have existed at all times , and many good honest folk lack the feeling of pleasure in a good conscience . <end>
<start> The Conscientious . <end>
<start> It is more convenient to follow one s conscience than one s intelligence , for at every failure conscience finds an excuse and an encouragement in itself . <end>
<start> That is why there are so many conscientious and so few intelligent people . <end>
<start> Opposite Means of Avoiding Bitterness . <end>
<start> One temperament finds it useful to be able to give vent to its disgust in words , being made sweeter by speech . <end>
<start> Another reaches its full bitterness only by speaking out : it is more advisable for it to have to gulp down something the restraint that men of place upon themselves in the presence of enemies and superiors improves their character and prevents it from becoming too acrid and sour . <end>
<start> Not to be Too Dejected . <end>
<start> To get bed sores is unpleasant , but no proof against the merits of the cure that prescribes that you should take to your bed . <end>
<start> Men who have long lived outside themselves , and have at last devoted themselves to the inward philosophic life , know that one can also get sores of character and intellect . <end>
<start> This , again , is on the whole no argument against the chosen way of life , but necessitates a few small exceptions and apparent relapses . <end>
<start> The Human Thing in Itself . <end>
<start> The most vulnerable and yet most unconquerable of things is human vanity : nay , through being wounded its strength increases and can grow to giant proportions . <end>
<start> The Farce of Many Industrious Persons . <end>
<start> By an excess of effort they win leisure for themselves , and then they can do nothing with it but count the hours until the tale is ended . <end>
<start> The Possession of Joy Abounding . <end>
<start> He that has joy abounding must be a good man , but perhaps he is not the cleverest of men , although he has reached the very goal towards which the cleverest man is striving with all his cleverness . <end>
<start> In the Mirror of Nature . <end>
<start> Is not a man fairly well described , when we are told that he likes to walk between tall fields of golden corn : that he prefers the forest and flower colours of were and chilly autumn to all others , because they point to something more beautiful than Nature has ever attained : that he feels as much at home under big broad leaved walnut trees as among his nearest kinsfolk : that in the mountains his greatest joy is to come across those tiny distant lakes from which the very eyes of solitude seem to peer at him : that he loves that grey calm of the misty twilight that steals along the windows on autumn and early winter evenings and shuts out all soulless sounds as with velvet curtains : that in unseen stones he recognises the last remaining traces of the primeval age , eager for speech , and honours them from childhood upwards : that , lastly , the sea with its shifting serpent skin and wild beast beauty is , and remains to him , unfamiliar ? <end>
<start> Yes , something of the man is described herewith , but the mirror of Nature does not say that the same man , with and not even in spite of all his idyllic sensibilities , might be disagreeable , stingy , and conceited . <end>
<start> Horace , who was a good judge of such matters , in his famous beats Me qui procul puts the tenderest feeling for country life into the mouth of a Roman money lender . <end>
<start> Power without Victory . <end>
<start> The strongest coin tion that of the complete non freedom of the human will is yet the poorest in results , for it has always had the mightiest of opponents human vanity . <end>
<start> Pleasure and Error . <end>
<start> A beneficial influence on friends is exerted by one man unconsciously , through his nature by another consciously , through isolated actions . <end>
<start> Although the former nature is held to be the higher , the latter alone is allied to good conscience and pleasure the pleasure in justification by good works , which rests upon a belief in the vocational character of our good and evil doing that is to say , upon a mistake . <end>
<start> The Folly of Committing Injustice . <end>
<start> The injustice we have inflicted ourselves is far harder to bear than the injustice inflicted upon us by others not always from moral grounds , be it observed . <end>
<start> After all , the doer is always the sufferer that is , if he be capable of feeling the sting of conscience or of perceiving that by his action he has armed society against himself and cut himself off . <end>
<start> For this reason we should beware still more of doing than of suffering injustice , for the sake of our own inward happiness so as not to lose our feeling of well being quite apart from any consideration of the precepts of religion and morality . <end>
<start> For in suffering injustice we have the consolation of a good conscience , of hope and of revenge , together with the sympathy and applause of the just , nay of the whole of society , which is afraid of the evil doer . <end>
<start> Not a few are skilled in the impure self deception that enables them to transform every injustice of their own into an injustice inflicted upon them from without , and to reserve for their own acts the exceptional right to the plea of self defence . <end>
<start> Their object , of course , is to make their own burden lighter . <end>
<start> Envy with or without a Mouthpiece . <end>
<start> Ordinary envy is wont to cackle when the envied hen has laid an egg thereby relieving itself and becoming milder . <end>
<start> But there is a yet deeper envy that in such a case becomes dead silent , desiring that every mouth should be sealed and always more and more angry because this desire is not gratified . <end>
<start> Silent envy grows in silence . <end>
<start> Anger as a Spy . <end>
<start> Anger exhausts the soul and brings its very dregs to light . <end>
<start> Hence , if we know no other means of gaining certainty , we must understand how to arouse anger in our dependents and adversaries , in order to learn what is really done and thought to our detriment . <end>
<start> Defence Morally more Difficult than Attack . <end>
<start> The true heroic deed and masterpiece of the good man does not lie in attacking opinions and continuing to love their propounded , but in the far harder task of defending his own position without causing or intending to cause bitter heartburn to his opponent . <end>
<start> The sword of attack is honest and broad , the sword of defence usually runs out to a needle point . <end>
<start> Honest towards Honesty . <end>
<start> One who is openly honest towards himself ends by being rather conceited about this honesty . <end>
<start> He knows only too well why he is honest for the same reason that another man prefers outward show and hypocrisy . <end>
<start> COALS of Fire . <end>
<start> The heaping of coals of fire on another s head is generally misunderstood and falls flat , because the other knows himself to be just as much in the right , and on his side too has thought of collecting coals . <end>
<start> Dangerous Books . <end>
<start> A man says : Judging from my own case , I find that this book is harmful . <end>
<start> Let him but wait , and perhaps one day he will confess that the book did him a great service by thrusting forward and bringing to light the hidden disease of his soul . <end>
<start> Altered opinions alter not at all or very little the character of a man : but they illuminate individual facets of his personality , which hitherto , in another constellation of opinions , had remained dark and unrecognisable . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> Simulated Pity . <end>
<start> We simulate pity when we wish to show ourselves superior to the feeling of animosity , but generally in vain . <end>
<start> This point is not noticed without a considerable enhancement of that feeling of animosity . <end>
<start> Open Contradiction often Conciliatory . <end>
<start> At the moment when a man openly makes known his difference of opinion from a well known party leader , the whole world thinks that he must be angry with the latter . <end>
<start> Sometimes , however , he is just on the point of ceasing to be angry with him . <end>
<start> He ventures to put himself on the same plane as his opponent , and is free from the tortures of suppressed envy . <end>
<start> Seeing our Light Shining . <end>
<start> In the darkest hour of depression , sickness , and guilt , we are still glad to see others taking a light from us and making use of us as of the disk of the moon . <end>
<start> By this roundabout route we derive some light from our own illuminating faculty . <end>
<start> Fellowship in Joy . <end>
<start> The snake that stings us means to hurt us and rejoices in so doing : the lowest animal can picture to itself the pain of others . <end>
<start> The German word Mitfrende coined by Nietzsche in opposition to titled sympathy , is untranslatable . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> But to picture to oneself the joy of others and to rejoice threat is the highest privilege of the highest animals , and again , amongst them , is the of the most select specimens accordingly a rare human thing . <end>
<start> Hence there have been philosophers who denied fellowship in joy . <end>
<start> Supplementary Pregnancy . <end>
<start> Thosewhohave arrived at works and deeds are in an obscure way , they know not how , all the more pregnant with them , as if to prove supplementary that these are their children and not those of chance . <end>
<start> Hard hearted from Vanity . <end>
<start> Just as justice is so often a cloak for weakness , so men who are fairly intelligent , but weak , sometimes attempt dissimulation from ambitious motives and purposely show themselves unjust and hard , in order to leave behind them the impression of strength . <end>
<start> Humiliation . <end>
<start> If in a large sack of profit we find a single grain of humiliation we still make a wry face even at our good luck . <end>
<start> Extreme Herostratism . <end>
<start> There might be Herostratus of Ephesus in B . <end>
<start> C . <end>
<start> set fire to the temple of Diana in order as he confessed on the rack to gain notoriety . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> Herostratuses who set fire to their own temple , in which their images are honoured . <end>
<start> A World of Diminutives . <end>
<start> The fact that all that is weak and in need of help appeals to the heart induces in us the habit of designating diminutive and softening terms all that appeals to our hearts and accordingly making such things weak and clinging to our imaginations . <end>
<start> The Bad Characteristic of Sympathy . <end>
<start> Sympathy has a peculiar impudence for its companion . <end>
<start> For , wishing to help at all costs , sympathy in no perplexity either as to the means of assistance or as to the nature and cause of the disease , and goes on courageously administering all its quack medicines to restore the health and reputation of the patient . <end>
<start> importunate . <end>
<start> There is even an importunate in relation to works , and the act of associating oneself from early youth on an intimate footing with the illustrious works of all times evinces an entire absence of shame . <end>
<start> Others are only importunate from ignorance , not knowing with whom they have to do for instance classical scholars young and old in relation to the works of the Greeks . <end>
<start> The Will is Ashamed of the Intellect . <end>
<start> In all coolness we make reasonable plans against our passions . <end>
<start> But we make the most serious mistake in this connection in being often ashamed , when the design has to be carried out , of the coolness and calculation with which we conceived it . <end>
<start> So we do just the unreasonable thing , from that sort of defiant magnanimity that every passion involves . <end>
<start> Why the Sceptics Offend Morality . <end>
<start> He who takes his morality solemnly and seriously is enraged against the sceptics in the domain of morals . <end>
<start> For where he lavished all his force , he wishes others to marvel but not to investigate and doubt . <end>
<start> Then there are natures whose last shred of morality is just the belief in morals . <end>
<start> They behave in the same way towards sceptics , if possible still more passionately . <end>
<start> SHYNESS . <end>
<start> All moralists are shy , because they know they are confounded with spies and traitors , so soon as their penchant is noticed . <end>
<start> Besides , they are generally conscious of being impotent in action , for in the midst of work the motives of their activity almost withdraw their attention from the work . <end>
<start> A Danger to Universal Morality . <end>
<start> People who are at the same time noble and honest come to defy every devilry that brings out their honesty , and to suspend for a time the balance of their moral judgment . <end>
<start> The Saddest Error . <end>
<start> It is an unpardonable offence when one discovers that where one was convinced of being loved , one is only regarded as a household utensil and decoration , whereby the master of the house can find an outlet for his vanity before his guests . <end>
<start> Love and Duality . <end>
<start> What else is love but understanding and rejoicing that another lives , works , and feels in a different and opposite way to ourselves ? <end>
<start> That love may be able to bridge over the contrasts by joys , w . <end>
<start> e must not remove or deny those contrasts . <end>
<start> Even self love presupposes an irreconcilable duality or plurality in one person . <end>
<start> Signs from Dreams . <end>
<start> What one sometimes does not know and feel accurately in waking hours whether one has a good or a bad conscience as regards some person is revealed completely and unambiguously by dreams . <end>
<start> Debauchery . <end>
<start> Not joy but joblessness is the mother of debauchery . <end>
<start> Reward and Punishment . <end>
<start> No one accuses without an underlying notion of punishment and revenge , even when he accuses his fate or himself . <end>
<start> All complaint is accusation , all self congratulation is praise . <end>
<start> Whether we do one or the other , we always make some one responsible . <end>
<start> Doubly Unjust . <end>
<start> We sometimes advance truth by a twofold injustice : when we see and represent consecutively the two sides of a case which we are not in a position to see together , but in such a way that every time we mistake or deny the other side , fancying that what we see is the whole truth . <end>
<start> MISTRUST . <end>
<start> Self mistrust does not always proceed uncertainly and shyly , but sometimes in a furious rage , having worked itself into a frenzy in order not to tremble . <end>
<start> Philosophy of Parvenus . <end>
<start> If you want to be a personality you must even hold your shadow in honour . <end>
<start> Knowing how to Wash Oneself Clean . <end>
<start> We must know how to emerge cleaner from unclean conditions , and , if necessary , how to wash ourselves even with dirty water . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> S Letting Yourself Go . <end>
<start> The more you let yourself go , the less others let you go . <end>
<start> The Innocent Rogue . <end>
<start> There is a slow , gradual path to vice and rascally of every description . <end>
<start> In the end , the traveller is quite abandoned by the insect swarms of a bad conscience , and although a thorough scoundrel he walks in innocence . <end>
<start> Making Plans . <end>
<start> Making plans and conceiving projects involves many agreeable sentiments . <end>
<start> He that had the strength to be nothing but a contrived of plans all his life would be a happy man . <end>
<start> But one must occasionally have a rest from this activity by carrying a plan into execution , and then comes anger and sobriety . <end>
<start> herewith We See the Ideal . <end>
<start> Every efficient man is blocked by his efficiency and cannon look out freely from its prison . <end>
<start> Had he not also a goodly share of imperfection , he could , by reason of his virtue , never arrive at an intellectual or moral freedom . <end>
<start> Our shortcomings are the eyes with which we see the ideal . <end>
<start> Dishonest Praise . <end>
<start> Dishonest praise causes many more twinges of conscience than dishonest blame , probably only because we have exposed our capacity for judgment far more completely through excessive praise than through excessive and unjust blame . <end>
<start> How One Dies is Indifferent . <end>
<start> The whole way in which a man thinks of death during the prime of his life and strength is very expressive and significant for what we call his character . <end>
<start> But the hour of death itself , his behaviour on the death bed , is almost indifferent . <end>
<start> The exhaustion of waning life , especially when old people die , the irregular or insufficient nourishment of the brain during this last period , the occasionally violent pain , the novel and untried nature of the whole position , and only too often the ebb and flow of superstitious impressions and fears , as if dying were of much consequence and meant the crossing of bridges of the most terrible kind all this forbids our using death as a testimony concerning the living . <end>
<start> Nor is it true that the dying man is generally more honest than the living . <end>
<start> On the contrary , through the solemn attitude of the bystanders , the repressed or flowing streams of tears and emotions , every one is inveigled into a comedy of vanity , now conscious , now unconscious . <end>
<start> The serious way in which every dying man is treated must have been to many a poor despised devil the highest joy of his whole life and a sort of compensation and repayment for many privations . <end>
<start> Morality and its Sacrifice . <end>
<start> The origin of morality may be traced to two ideas : The community is of more value than the individual , and The permanent interest is to be preferred to the temporary . <end>
<start> The conclusion drawn is that the permanent interest of the community is unconditionally to be set above the temporary interest of the individual , especially his momentary well being , but also his permanent interest and even the prolongation of his existence . <end>
<start> Even if the individual suffers by an arrangement that suits the mass , even if he is depressed and ruined by it , morality must be maintained and the victim brought to the sacrifice . <end>
<start> Such a trend of thought arises , however , only in those who are not the victims for in the victim s case it enforces the claim that the individual might be worth more than the many , and that the present enjoyment , the moment in paradise , should perhaps be rated higher than a tame succession of untroubled or comfortable circumstances . <end>
<start> But the philosophy of the sacrificial victim always finds voice too late , and so victory remains with morals and morality : which are really nothing more than the sentiment for the whole concept of morals under which one lives and has been reared and reared not as an individual but as a member of the whole , as a cipher in a majority . <end>
<start> Hence it constantly happens that the individual makes himself into a majority by means of his morality . <end>
<start> The Good and the Good Conscience . <end>
<start> You hold that all good things have at all times had good conscience ? <end>
<start> Science , which is certainly a very good thing , has come into the world without such a conscience and quite free from all pathos , rather clandestinely , by roundabout ways , walking with shrouded or masked face like a sinner , and always with the feeling at least of being a smuggler . <end>
<start> Good conscience has bad conscience for its stepping-stone , not for its opposite . <end>
<start> For all that is good has at one time been new and consequently strange , against morals , immoral , and has gnawed like a worm at the heart of the fortunate discoverer . <end>
<start> Success sanctified the Intentions . <end>
<start> We should not shrink from treading the road to a virtue , even when we see clearly that nothing but egotism , and accordingly utility , personal comfort , fear , considerations of health , reputation , or glory , are the impelling motives . <end>
<start> These motives are styled ignoble and selfish . <end>
<start> Very well , but if they stimulate us to some virtue for example , self denial , , order , thrift , measure , and moderation let us listen to them , whatever their epithets may be ! <end>
<start> For if we reach the goal to which they summon us , then the virtue we have attained , by means of the pure air it makes us breathe and the spiritual well being it communicates , ennobled the remoter impulses of our action , and afterwards we no longer perform those actions from the same coarse motives that inspired us before . <end>
<start> Education should therefore force the virtues on the pupil , as far as possible , according to his disposition . <end>
<start> Then virtue , the sunshine and summer atmosphere of the soul , can contribute her own share of work and add yellowness and sweetness . <end>
<start> Dabblers in Christianity , not Christians . <end>
<start> So that is your Christianity ! <end>
<start> To annoy humanity you praise God and His Saints , and again when you want to praise humanity you go so far that God and His Saints must be annoyed . <end>
<start> I wish you would at least learn Christian manners , as you are so deficient in the civility of the Christian heart . <end>
<start> The Religious and Irreligious Impression OF Nature . <end>
<start> A true believer must be to us an object of veneration , but the same holds good of a true , sincere , convinced unbeliever . <end>
<start> With men of the latter stamp we are near to the high mountains where mighty rivers have their source , and with believers we are under vigorous , shady , restful trees . <end>
<start> Judicial Murder . <end>
<start> The two greatest judicial murders in the world s history are , to speak without exaggeration , concealed and well concealed suicide . <end>
<start> In both cases a man willed to die , and in both cases he let his breast be pierced by the sword in the hand of human injustice . <end>
<start> Love . <end>
<start> The finest artistic conception wherein Christianity had the advantage over other religious This , of course , refers to Jesus and Socrates . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> vol . <end>
<start> D systems lay in one word Love . <end>
<start> Hence it became the lyric religion whereas in its two other creations feminism bestowed heroic epical religions upon the world . <end>
<start> In the word love there is so much meaning , so much that stimulates and appeals to memory and hope , that even the meanest intelligence and the coldest heart feel some glimmering of its sense . <end>
<start> The cleverest woman and the lowest man think of the comparatively unselfish moments of their whole life , even if with them Eros never soared high : and the vast number of beings who miss love from their parents or children or sweethearts , especially those whose sexual instincts have been refined away , have found their heart s desire in Christianity . <end>
<start> The Fulfilment of Christianity . <end>
<start> In Christianity there is also an Epicurean trend of thought , starting from the idea that God can only demand of man , his creation and his image , what it is possible for man to fulfil , and accordingly that Christian virtue and perfection are attainable and often attained . <end>
<start> Now , for instance , the belief in loving one s enemies even if it is only a belief or fancy , and by no means a psychological reality a real love gives unalloyed happiness , so long as it is genuinely believed . <end>
<start> As to the reason of this , psychologist and Christian might well differ . <end>
<start> Hence earthly life , through the belief , I mean the fancy , that it satisfies not only the injunction to love our enemies , but all the other injunctions of Christianity , and that it has really assimilated and embodied in itself the Divine perfection according to the command , Be perfect as your Father in heaven is perfect , might actually become a holy life . <end>
<start> Thus error can make Christ s promise come true . <end>
<start> Of the Future of Christianity . <end>
<start> We may be allowed to form a conjecture as to the disappearance of Christianity and as to the places where it will be the slowest to retreat , if we consider where and for what reasons Protestantism spread with such startling rapidity . <end>
<start> As is well known , Protestantism promised to do far more cheaply all that the old Church did , without costly masses , pilgrimages , and priestly pomp and circumstance . <end>
<start> It spread particularly among the Northern nations , which were not so deeply rooted as those of the South in the old Church s symbolism and love of ritual . <end>
<start> In the South the more powerful pagan religion survived in Christianity , whereas in the North Christianity meant an opposition to and a break with the old time creed , and hence was from the first more thoughtful and less sensual , but for that very reason , in times of peril , more fanatical and more obstinate . <end>
<start> If from the standpoint of thought we succeed in uprooting Christianity , we can at once know the point where it will begin to disappear the very point at which it will be most stubborn in defence . <end>
<start> In other places it will bend but not break , lose its leaves but burst into leaf afresh , because the senses , and not thought , have gone over to its side . <end>
<start> But it is the senses that maintain the belief that with all its expensive outlay the Church is more cheaply and conveniently managed than under the stern conditions of work and wages . <end>
<start> Yet what does one hold leisure or semi idleness to be worth , when once one has become accustomed to it ? <end>
<start> The senses plead against a world , saying that there would be too much work to do in it and an insufficient supply of leisure . <end>
<start> They take the part of magic that is , they let God work himself primus nos , Deus cabaret . <end>
<start> Theatricality and Honesty of Unbelievers . <end>
<start> There is no book that contains in such abundance or expresses so faithfully all that man occasionally finds salutary ecstatic inward happiness , ready for sacrifice or death in the belief in and contemplation of his truth as the book that tells of Christ . <end>
<start> From that book a clever man may learn all the means whereby a book can be made into a world book , a made tecum for all , and especially that master means of representing everything as discovered , nothing as future and uncertain . <end>
<start> All influential books try to leave the same impression , as if the widest intellectual horizon were circumscribed here and as if about the sun that shines here every constellation visible at present or in the future must revolve . <end>
<start> Must not then all purely scientific books be poor in influence on the same grounds as such books are rich in influence ? <end>
<start> Is not the book fated to live humble and among humble folk , in order to be crucified in the end and never resurrected ? <end>
<start> In relation to what the religious inform us of their knowledge and their holy spirit , are not all upright men of science poor in spirit ? <end>
<start> Can any religion demand more self denial and draw the selfish out of themselves more inexorably than science ? <end>
<start> This and similar things we may say , in any case with a certain theatricality , when we have to defend ourselves against believers , for it is impossible to conduct a defence without a certain amount of theatricality . <end>
<start> But between ourselves our language must be more honest , and we employ a freedom that those believers are not even allowed , in their own interests , to understand . <end>
<start> Away , then , with the monastic cowl of self denial , with the appearance of humility ! <end>
<start> Much more and much better so rings our truth ! <end>
<start> If science were not linked with the pleasure of knowledge , the utility of the thing known , what should we care for science ? <end>
<start> If a little faith , love , and hope did not lead our souls to knowledge , what would attract us to science ? <end>
<start> And if in science the ego means nothing , still the inventive , happy ego , every upright and industrious ego , means a great deal in the republic of the men of science . <end>
<start> The homage of those who pay homage , the joy of those whom we wish well or honour , in some cases glory and a fair share of immortality , is the personal reward for every suppression of personality : to say nothing here of meaner views and rewards , although it is just on this account that the majority have sworn and always continue to swear fidelity to the laws of the republic and of science . <end>
<start> If we had not remained in some degree unscientific , what would science matter to us ? <end>
<start> Taking everything together and speaking in plain language : To a purely knowing being knowledge would be indifferent . <end>
<start> Not the quality but the quantity of faith and deviousness distinguishes us from the pious , the believers . <end>
<start> We are content with less . <end>
<start> But should one of them cry out to us : Be content and show yourselves contented ! <end>
<start> we could easily answer : As a matter of fact , we do not belong to the most discontented class . <end>
<start> But you , if your faith makes you happy , show yourselves to be happy . <end>
<start> Your faces have always done more harm to your faith than our reasons ! <end>
<start> If that glad message of your Bible were written in your faces , you would not need to demand belief in the authority of that book in such stiff necked fashion . <end>
<start> Your words , your actions should continually make the Bible superfluous in fact , through you a new Bible should continually come into being . <end>
<start> As it is , your apologia for Christianity is rooted in your christianity , and with your defence you write your own condemnation . <end>
<start> If you , however , should wish to emerge from your dissatisfaction with Christianity , you should ponder over the experience of two thousand years , which , clothed in the modest form of a question , may be voiced as follows : If Christ really intended to redeem the world , may he not be said to have failed ? <end>
<start> The Poet as Guide to the Future . <end>
<start> Allhumanity , but is not used under our conditions of a definite goal not to depicting the present nor to reviving and summarising the past , but to pointing the way to the future . <end>
<start> Nor should this be so done as if the poet , like an imaginative political economist , had to anticipate a more favourable national and social state of things and picture their realisation . <end>
<start> Rather will he , just as the earlier poets portrayed the images of the Gods , portray the fair images of men . <end>
<start> He will divine those cases where , in the midst of our modern world and reality which will not be shirked or repudiated in the usual poetic fashion , a great , noble soul is still possible , where it may be embodied in harmonious , equable conditions , where it may become permanent , visible , and representative of a type , and so , by the stimulus to imitation and envy , help to create the future . <end>
<start> The poems of such a poet would be distinguished by appearing secluded and protected from the heated atmosphere of the passions . <end>
<start> The irremediable failure , the shattering of all the strings of the human instrument , the scornful laughter and gnashing of teeth , and all tragedy and comedy in the usual old sense , would appear by the side of this new art as mere archaic lumber , a blurring of the outlines of the world picture . <end>
<start> Strength , kindness , gentleness , purity , and an sought , innate moderation in the personalities and their action : a levelled soil , giving rest and pleasure to the foot : a shining heaven mirrored in faces and events : science and art welded into a new unity : the mind living together with her sister , the soul , without arrogance or jealousy , and enticing from contrasts the grace of seriousness , not the impatience of discord all this would be the general environment , the background on which the delicate differences of the embodied ideals would make the real picture , that of ever growing human majesty . <end>
<start> Many roads to this poetry of the future start from Goethe , but the quest needs good pathfinders and above all a far greater strength than is possessed by modern poets , who unscrupulous represent the half animal and the immaturity and intemperance that are mistaken by them for power and naturalness . <end>

<start> The Muse as Penthesilea Better to rot than to be a woman without charm . <end>
<start> When once the Muse thinks thus , the end of her art is again at hand . <end>
<start> But it can be a tragic and also a comic finale . <end>
<start> The Circuitous Path to the Beautiful . <end>
<start> If the beautiful is to be identified with that which gives pleasure and thus sang the Muses once the useful is often the necessary circuitous path to the beautiful , and has a perfect right to spurn the short sighted censure of men who live for the moment , who will not wait , and who think that they can reach all good things without ever taking a circuitous path . <end>
<start> An Excuse for many a Transgression . <end>

<start> Tr . <end>

<start> Satisfying the Best People . <end>
<start> If we have satisfied the best people of our time with our art , it is a sign that we shall not satisfy the best people of the succeeding period . <end>

<start> Of One Substance . <end>
<start> If we are of one substance with a book or a work of art , we think in our heart of hearts that it must be excellent , and are offended if others find it ugly , over spiced , or pretentious . <end>
<start> Speech and Emotion . <end>
<start> That speech is not the fact that all simple men are ashamed to From Schiller , wallenstein s Lager : Wer den beaten seiner Zeit genus bethan , der hat select fur alle zliten He that has satisfied the best men of his time has lived for all time . <end>
<start> feelings are expressed only in actions , and even here such men blush if others seem to divine their motives . <end>
<start> After all , among poets , to whom God generally denies this shame , the more noble are more monosyllabic in the language of emotion , and evince a certain constraint : whereas the real poets of emotion are for the most part shameless in practical life . <end>
<start> A Mistake about a Privation . <end>
<start> He that has not for a long time been completely weaned from an art , and is still always at home in it , has no idea how small a privation it is to live without that art . <end>
<start> Three quarter Strength . <end>
<start> A work that is meant to give an impression of health should be produced with three quarters , at the most , of the strength of its creator . <end>
<start> If he has gone to his farthest limit , the work excites the observer and disconcert him by its tension . <end>
<start> All good things have something lazy about them and lie like cows in the meadow . <end>
<start> Refusing to have Hunger as a Guest . <end>
<start> As refined fare serves a hungry man as well as and no better than coarser food , the more pretentious artist will not dream of inviting the hungry man to his meal . <end>
<start> Living without Art and Wine . <end>
<start> It is with works of art as with wine it is better if one can do without both and keep to water , and if from the inner fire and inner sweetness of the soul the water spontaneously changes again into wine . <end>
<start> no . <end>
<start> The Pirate Genius . <end>
<start> The pirate genius in art , who even knows how to deceive subtle minds , arises when some one unscrupulous and from youth upwards regards all good things , that are not protected by law , as the property of a particular person , as his legitimate spoil . <end>
<start> Now all the good things of past ages and masters lie free around us , hedged about and protected by the reverential awe of the few who know them . <end>
<start> To these few our robber genius , by the force of his impudence , bids defiance and accumulates for himself a wealth that once more calls forth homage and awe . <end>
