<start> To the Poets of Great Towns . <end>
<start> In the gardens of modern poetry it will clearly be observed that the sewers of great towns are too near . <end>
<start> With the fragrance of flowers is mingled something that betrays abomination and . <end>
<start> With pain I ask : Must you poets always request wit and dirt to stand godfather , when an innocent and beautiful sensation has to be christened by you ? <end>
<start> Are you obliged to dress your noble goddess in a hood of devilry and caricature ? <end>
<start> But whence this necessity , this obligation ? <end>
<start> The reason is because you live too near the sewers . <end>
<start> DO HUMAN , ALL TOO HUMAN . <end>
<start> Of the Salt of Speech . <end>
<start> No one has ever explained why the Greek writers , having at command such an unparalleled wealth and power of language , made so sparing a use of their resources that every post classical Greek book appears by comparison crude , over coloured , and extravagant . <end>
<start> It is said that towards the North Polar ice and in the hottest countries salt is becoming less and less used , whereas on the other hand the dwellers on the plains and by the coast in the more temperate zones use salt in great abundance . <end>
<start> Is it possible that the Greeks from a twofold reason because their intellect was colder and clearer but their fundamental passionate nature far more tropical than ours did not need salt and spice to the same extent that we do ? <end>
<start> The Freest Writer . <end>
<start> In a book for free spirits one cannon avoid mention of Laurence Sterne , the man whom Goethe honoured as the freest spirit of his century . <end>
<start> May he be satisfied with the honour of being called the freest writer of all times , in comparison with whom all others appear stiff , , intolerant , and downright boorish ! <end>
<start> In his case we should not speak of the clear and rounded but of the endless melody if by this phrase we arrive at a name for an artistic style in which the definite form is continually broken , thrust aside and transferred to the realm of the indefinite , so that it signifies one and the other at the same time . <end>
<start> Sterne is the great master of double entendres , this phrase being naturally used in a far wider sense than is commonly done when one applies it to sexual relations . <end>
<start> We may give up for lost the reader who always wants to know exactly what Sterne thinks about a matter , and whether he be making a serious or a smiling face for he can do both with one wrinkling of his features he can be and even wishes to be right and wrong at the same moment , to interleaved profundity and farce . <end>
<start> His digressions are at once continuations and further developments of the story , his maxims contain a satire on all that is sententious , his dislike of seriousness is bound up with a disposition to take no matter merely externally and on the surface . <end>
<start> So in the proper reader he arouses a feeling of uncertainty whether he be walking , lying , or standing , a feeling most closely akin to that of floating in the air . <end>
<start> He , the most versatile of writers , communicates something of this versatility to his reader . <end>
<start> Yes , Sterne unexpectedly changes the parts , and is often as much reader as author , his book being like a play within a play , a theatre audience before another theatre audience . <end>
<start> We must surrender at discretion to the mood of Sterne , although we can always expect it to be gracious . <end>
<start> It is strangely instructive to see how so great a writer as Diderot has affected this double entendres of Sterne s to be equally ambiguous throughout is just the serbian super humour . <end>
<start> Did Diderot imitate , admire , ridicule , or parody Sterne in his Jacques le fatalist One cannon be exactly certain , and this uncertainty was perhaps intended by the author . <end>
<start> This very doubt makes the French unjust to the work of one of their first masters , one who need not be ashamed of comparison with any of the ancients or moderns . <end>
<start> For humour and especially for this humorous attitude towards humour itself the French are too serious . <end>
<start> Is it necessary to add that of all great authors Sterne is the worst model , in fact the inimitable author , and that even Diderot had to pay for his daring ? <end>
<start> What the worthy Frenchmen and before them some Greeks and Romans aimed at and attained in prose is the very opposite of what Sterne aims at and attains . <end>
<start> He raises himself as a masterly exception above all that artists in writing demand of themselves propriety , reserve , character , steadfastness of purpose , comprehensiveness , perspicacity , good deportment in gait and feature . <end>
<start> Unfortunately Sterne the man seems to have been only too closely related to Sterne the writer . <end>
<start> His squirrel soul sprang with insatiable unrest from branch to branch he knew what lies between sublimity and rascally he had sat on every seat , always with unabashed watery eyes and mobile play of feature . <end>
<start> He was if language does not revolt from such a combination of a hard hearted kindness , and in the midst of the joys of a grotesque and even corrupt imagination he showed the bashful grace of innocence . <end>
<start> Such a carnal and spiritual hermaphroditic , such untrammelled wit penetrating into every vein and muscle , was perhaps never possessed by any other man . <end>
<start> A Choice Reality . <end>
<start> Just as the good prose writer only takes words that belong to the language of daily intercourse , though not by a long way all its words whence arises a choice style so the good poet of the future will only represent the real and turn his eyes away from all fantastic , superstitious , half voiced , forgotten stories , to which earlier poets devoted their powers . <end>
<start> Only reality , though by a long way not every reality but a choice reality . <end>
<start> Degenerate Species of Art . <end>
<start> Side by side with the genuine species of art , those of great repose and great movement , there are degenerate species weary , blase art and excited art . <end>
<start> Both would have their weakness taken for strength and wish to be confounded with the genuine species . <end>
<start> A Hero Impossible from Lack of Colour . <end>
<start> The typical poets and artists of our age like to compose their pictures upon a background of shimmering red , green , grey , and gold , on the background of nervous sensuality a condition well understood by the children of this century . <end>
<start> The drawback comes when we do not look at these pictures with the eyes of our century . <end>
<start> Then we see that the great figures painted by these artists have something flickering , tremulous , and dizzy about them , and accordingly we do not ascribe to them heroic deeds , but at best mock heroic , swaggering . <end>
<start> overtaken Style . <end>
<start> The overtaken style is a consequence of the impoverishment of the organic ing force together with a lavish stock of expedients and intentions . <end>
<start> At the beginnings of art the conditions sometimes appear . <end>
<start> fulcrum est homing . <end>
<start> History and experience tell us that the significant that mysteriously excites the imagination and carries one beyond everyday reality , is older and grows more luxuriantly than the beautiful and reverence for the beautiful in art : and that it begins to flourish exceedingly when the sense for beauty is on the wane . <end>
<start> For the vast majority of mankind this grotesque seems to be a higher need than the beautiful , presumably because it contains a coarser narcotic . <end>
<start> Origins of Taste in Works of Art . <end>
<start> If we consider the primary germs of the artistic sense , and ask ourselves what are the various kinds of joy produced by the of art as , for example , among savage tribes we find first of all the joy of understanding what another means . <end>
<start> Art in this case is a sort of conundrum , which causes its solver pleasure in his own quick and keen perceptions . <end>
<start> Then the roughest works of art remind us of the pleasant things we have actually experienced , and so give joy as , for example , when the artist alludes to a chase , a victory , a wedding . <end>
<start> Again , the representation may cause us to feel excited , touched , inflamed , as for instance in the glorification of revenge and danger . <end>
<start> Here the enjoyment lies in the excitement itself , in the victory over tedium . <end>
<start> The memory , too , of unpleasant things , so far as they have been overcome or make us appear interesting to the listener as subjects for art as when the singer describes the mishaps of a daring seaman , can inspire great joy , the credit for which is given to art . <end>
<start> A more subtle variety is the joy that arises at the sight of all that is regular and symmetrical in lines , points , and rhythms . <end>
<start> For by a certain analogy is awakened the feeling for all that is orderly and regular in life , which one has to thank alone for all well being . <end>
<start> So in the cult of symmetry we unconsciously do homage to rule and proportion as the source of our previous happiness , and the joy in this case is a kind of hymn of thanksgiving . <end>
<start> Only when a certain satiety of the last mentioned joy arises does a more subtle feeling step in , that enjoyment might even lie in a violation of the symmetrical and regular . <end>
<start> This feeling , for example , impels us to seek reason in apparent unreason , and the sort of aesthetic riddle guessing that results is in a way the higher species of the first named artistic joy . <end>
<start> He who pursues this speculation still further will know what kind of hypotheses for the explanation of aesthetic phenomena are hereby fundamentally rejected . <end>
<start> Not too Near . <end>
<start> It is a disadvantage for good thoughts when they follow too closely on one another , for they hide the view from each other . <end>
<start> vol . <end>
<start> E That is why great artists and writers have made an abundant use of the mediocre . <end>
<start> Roughness and Weakness . <end>
<start> Artists of all periods have made the discovery that in roughness lies a certain strength , and that not every one can be rough who wants to be : also that many varieties of weakness have a powerful effect on the emotions . <end>
<start> From this source are derived many artistic substitutes , which not even the greatest and most conscientious artists can abstain from using . <end>
<start> Good Memory . <end>
<start> Many a man fails to become a thinker for the sole reason that his memory is too good . <end>
<start> Arousing instead of Appeasing Hunger . <end>
<start> Great artists fancy that they have taken full possession of a soul . <end>
<start> In reality , and often to their painful disappointment , that soul has only been made more capacious and insatiable , so that a dozen greater artists could plunge into its depths without filling it up . <end>
<start> Artists Anxiety . <end>
<start> The anxiety lest people may not believe that their figures are alive can mislead many artists of declining taste to portray these figures so that they appear as if mad . <end>
<start> From the same anxiety , on the other hand , Greek artists of the earliest ages gave even dead and sorely wounded men that smile which they knew as the most vivid sign of life careless of the actual forms bestowed by nature on life at its last gasp . <end>
<start> The Circle must be Completed . <end>
<start> He who follows a philosophy or a genre of art to the end of its career and beyond , understands from inner experience why the masters and disciples who come after have so often turned , with a deprecatory gesture , into a new groove . <end>
<start> The circle must be described but the individual , even the greatest , sits firm on his point of the circumference , with an inexorable look of obstinacy , as if the circle ought never to be completed . <end>
<start> The Older Art and the Soul of the Present . <end>
<start> Since every art becomes more and more adapted to the expression of spiritual states , of the more lively , delicate , energetic , and passionate states , the later masters , spoilt by these means of expression , do not feel at their ease in the presence of the old time works of art . <end>
<start> They feel as if the ancients had merely been lacking in the means of making their souls speak clearly , also perhaps in some necessary technical preliminaries . <end>
<start> They think that they must render some assistance in this quarter , for they believe in the similarity or even unity of all souls . <end>
<start> In truth , however , measure , symmetry , a contempt for graciousness and charm , an unconscious severity and morning chilliness , an evasion of passion , as if passion meant the death of art such are the constituents of sentiment and morality in all old masters , who selected and arranged their means of expression not at random but in a necessary connection with their morality . <end>
<start> Knowing this , are we to deny those that come after the right to animate the older works with their soul ? <end>
<start> No , for these works can only survive through our giving them our soul , and our blood alone enables them to speak to us . <end>
<start> The real historic discourse would talk ghostly speech to ghosts . <end>
<start> We honour the great artists less by that barren timidity that allows every word , every note to remain intact than by energetic endeavours to aid them continually to a new life . <end>
<start> True , if Beethoven were suddenly to come to life and hear one of his works performed with that modern animation and nervous refinement that bring glory to our masters of execution , he would probably be silent for a long while , uncertain whether he should raise his hand to curse or to bless , but perhaps say at last : Well , well ! <end>
<start> That is neither I nor not I , but a third thing it seems to me , too , something right , if not just the tight thing . <end>
<start> But you must know yourselves what to do , as in any case it is you who have to listen . <end>
<start> As our Schiller says , the living man is right . <end>
<start> So have it your own way , and let me go down again . <end>
<start> Against the disparage of Brevity . <end>
<start> A brief dictum may be the fruit and harvest of long reflection . <end>
<start> The reader , however , who is a novice in this field and has never considered the case in point , sees something embryonic in all brief dicta , not without a reproachful hint to the author , requesting him not to serve up such raw and ill prepared food . <end>
<start> Against the Short Sighted . <end>
<start> Do you think it is piece work because it is and must be offered you in pieces ? <end>
<start> Readers of Aphorisms . <end>
<start> The worst readers of aphorisms are the friends of the author , if they make a point of referring the general to the particular instance to which the aphorism owes its origin . <end>
<start> This nimby pammy attitude brings all the author s trouble to naught , and instead of a philosophic lesson and a philosophic frame of mind , they deservedly gain nothing but the satisfaction of a vulgar curiosity . <end>
<start> Readers Insults . <end>
<start> The reader offers a twofold insult to the author by praising his second book at the expense of his first or vice versa and by expecting the author to be grateful to him on that account . <end>
<start> The Exciting Element in the History of Art . <end>
<start> We fall into a state of terrible tension when we follow the history of an art as , for example , that of Greek oratory and , passing from master to master , observe their increasing precautions to obey the old and the new laws and all these self imposed limitations . <end>
<start> We see that the bow must snap , and that the so called loose composition , with the wonderful means of expression smothered and concealed in this particular case the florid style of arianism , was once necessary and almost beneficial . <end>
<start> To the Great in Art . <end>
<start> That enthusiasm for some object which you , O great man , introduce into this world causes the intelligence of the many to be stunted . <end>
<start> The knowledge of this fact spells humiliation . <end>
<start> But the enthusiast wears his hump with pride and pleasure , and you have the consolation of feeling that you have increased the world s happiness . <end>
<start> Conscienceless aesthetes . <end>
<start> The real fanatics of an artistic school are perhaps those utterly inartistic natures that are not even grounded in the elements of artistic study and creation , but are impressed with the strongest of all the elementary influences of an art . <end>
<start> For them there is no aesthetic conscience hence nothing to hold them back from fanaticism . <end>
<start> How the Soul should be Moved by the New Music . <end>
<start> The artistic purpose followed by the new music , in what is now forcibly but none too lucidly termed endless melody , can be understood by going into the sea , gradually losing one s firm tread on the bottom , and finally surrendering unconditionally to the fluid element . <end>
<start> One has to swim . <end>
<start> In the previous , older music one was forced , with delicate or stately or impassioned movement , to dance . <end>
<start> The measure necessary for dancing , the observance of a distinct balance of time and force in the soul of the hearer , imposed a continual self-control . <end>
<start> Through the contraction of the cooler draught of air which came from this caution and the warmer breath of musical enthusiasm , that music exercised its spell . <end>
<start> Richard Wagner aimed at a different excitation of the soul , allied , as above said , to swimming and floating . <end>
<start> This is perhaps the most essential of his innovations . <end>
<start> His famous method , originating from this aim and adapted to it the endless melody strives to break and sometimes even to despise all mathematical equilibrium of time and force . <end>
<start> He is only too rich in the invention of such effects , which sound to the old school like rhythmic paradoxes and blasphemies . <end>
<start> He dreads putrefaction , crystallisation , the development of music into the architectural . <end>
<start> He accordingly sets up a three time rhythm in opposition to the , not infrequently introduces five time and seventies , immediately repeats a phrase , but with a probation , so that its time is again doubled and trebled . <end>
<start> From an easy going imitation of such art may arise a great danger to music , for by the side of the superabundance of rhythmic emotion demoralisation and decadence lurk in ambush . <end>
<start> The danger will become very great if such music comes to associate itself more and more closely with a quite naturalistic art of acting and pantomime , trained and dominated by no higher plastic models an art that knows no measure in itself and can impart no measure to the kindred element , the all too womaniser nature of music , Poet and Reality . <end>
<start> The Muse of the poet who is not in love with reality will not be reality , and will bear him children with hollow eyes and all too tender bones . <end>
<start> Means and End . <end>
<start> In art the end does not justify the means , but holy means can justify the end . <end>
<start> The Worst Readers . <end>
<start> The worst readers are those who act like plundering soldiers . <end>
<start> They take out some things that they might use , cover the rest with filth and confusion , and blaspheme about the whole . <end>
<start> Signs of a Good Writer . <end>
<start> Good writers have two things in common : they prefer being understood to being admired , and they do not write for the critical and over shrewd reader . <end>
<start> The Mixed Species . <end>
<start> The mixed species in art bear witness to their authors distrust of their own strength . <end>
<start> They seek auxiliary powers , advocates , hiding places such is the case with the poet who calls in philosophy , the musician who calls in the drama , and the thinker who calls in rhetoric to his aid . <end>
<start> Shutting One s Mouth . <end>
<start> When his book opens its mouth , the author must shut his . <end>
<start> Badges of Rank . <end>
<start> All poets and men of letters who are in love with the superlative want to do more than they can . <end>
<start> COLD Books . <end>
<start> The deep thinker reckons on readers who feel with him the happiness that lies in deep thinking . <end>
<start> Hence a book that looks cold and sober , if seen in the right light , may seem bathed in the sunshine of spiritual cheerfulness and become a genuine soul comforter . <end>
<start> A Knack of the Slow fitted . <end>
<start> The slow-witted thinker generally allies himself with loquacity and . <end>
<start> By the former he thinks he is gaining mobility and fluency , by the latter he gives his peculiarity the appearance of being a result of free will and artistic purpose , with a view to dignity , which needs slow movement . <end>
<start> Le Style Baroque He who as thinker and writer is not born or trained to dialectic and the consecutive arrangement of ideas , will unconsciously turn to the rhetoric and dramatic forms . <end>
<start> For , after all , his object is to make himself understood and to carry the day by force , and he is indifferent whether , as shepherd , he honestly guides to himself the hearts of his fellow men , or , as robber , he captures them by surprise . <end>
<start> This is true of the plastic arts as of music : where the feeling of insufficient dialectic or a deficiency in expression or narration , together with an urgent , over powerful impulse to form , gives birth to that species of style known as baroque . <end>
<start> Only the ill educated and the arrogant will at once find a deprecatory force in this word . <end>
<start> The baroque style always arises at the time of decay of a great art , when the demands of art in classical expression have become too great . <end>
<start> It is a natural phenomenon which will be observed with melancholy for it is a forerunner of the night but at the same time with admiration for its peculiar compensatory arts of expression and narration . <end>
<start> To this style belongs already a choice of material and subjects of the highest dramatic tension , at which the heart trembles even when there is no art , because heaven and hell are all too near the emotions : then , the oratory of strong passion and gestures , of ugly sublimity , of great In German Barockstil i . <end>
<start> e . <end>
<start> the degenerate post Renaissance style in art and literature , which spread from Italy in the seventeenth century . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> masses , in fact of absolute quantity per se as is shown in Michael Angelo , the father or grandfather of the Italian baroque stylists : the lights of dusk , illumination and conflagration playing upon those strongly moulded forms : ever new ventures in means and aims , strongly underscored by artists for artists , while the layman must fancy he sees an unconscious overflowing of all the horns of plenty of an original nature art : all these characteristics that constitute the greatness of that style are neither possible nor permitted in the earlier ante classical and classical periods of a branch of art . <end>
<start> Such luxuries hang long on the tree like forbidden fruit . <end>
<start> Just now , when music is passing into this last phase , we may learn to know the phenomenon of the baroque sty le in peculiar splendour , and , by comparison , find much that is instructive for earlier ages . <end>
<start> For from Greek times onward there has often been a baroque style , in poetry , oratory , prose writing , sculpture , and , as is well known , in architecture . <end>
<start> This style , though wanting in the highest nobility , the nobility of an innocent , unconscious , triumphant perfection , has nevertheless given pleasure to many of the best and most serious minds of their time . <end>
<start> Hence , as aforesaid , it is presumptuous to depreciate it without reserve , however happy we may feel because our taste for it has not made us insensible to the purer and greater style . <end>
<start> The Value of Honest Books . <end>
<start> Honest books make the reader honest , at least by exciting his hatred and aversion , which otherwise cunning clever ness knows so well how to conceal . <end>
<start> Against a book , however , we let ourselves go , however restrained we may be in our relations with men . <end>
<start> How Art makes Partisans . <end>
<start> Individual fine passages , an exciting general tenor , a moving and absorbing finale so much of a work of art is accessible even to most laymen . <end>
<start> In an art period when it is desired to win over the great majority of the laymen to the side of the artists and to make a party perhaps for the very preservation of art , the creative artist will do well to offer nothing more than the above . <end>
<start> Then he will not be a squandered of his strength , in spheres where no one is grateful to him . <end>
<start> For to perform the remaining functions , the imitation of Nature in her organic development and growth , would in that case be like sowing seeds in water . <end>
<start> Becoming Great to the Detriment of History . <end>
<start> Every later master who leads the taste of art lovers into his channel unconsciously gives rise to a selection and revaluation of the older masters and their works . <end>
<start> Whatever in them is conformable and akin to him , and anticipates and foreshadows him , appears henceforth as the only important element in them and their works a fruit in which a great error usually lies hidden like a worm . <end>
<start> How an Epoch becomes Lured to Art . <end>
<start> If we teach people by all the enchantments of artists and thinkers to feel reverence for their defects , their intellectual poverty , their absurd infatuation and passions as it is quite possible to do if we show them only the lofty side of crime and folly , only the touching and appealing element in weakness and shabbiness and blind devotion that too has often enough been done : we have employed the means for inspiring even an philosophical and inartistic age with an ecstatic love of philosophy and art especially of thinkers and artists as personalities and , in the worst case , perhaps with the only means of defending the existence of such tender and fragile beings . <end>
<start> Criticism and Joy . <end>
<start> Criticism , one sided and unjust as well as intelligent criticism , gives so much pleasure to him who exercises it that the world is indebted to every work and every action that inspires much criticism and many critics . <end>
<start> For criticism draws after it a glittering train of joyousness , wit , self admiration , pride , instruction , designs of improvement . <end>
<start> The God of joy created the bad and the mediocre for the same reason that he created the good . <end>
<start> Beyond his Limits . <end>
<start> When an artist wants to be more than an artist for example , the moral awakened of his people he at last falls in love , as a punishment , with a monster of moral substance . <end>
<start> The Muse laughs , for , though a kind hearted Goddess , she can also be malignant from jealousy . <end>
<start> Milton and lostock are cases in point . <end>
<start> A Glass Eye . <end>
<start> The tendency of a talent towards moral subjects , characters , motives , towards the beautiful soul of the work of art , is often only a glass eye put on by the artist who lacks a beautiful soul . <end>
<start> It may result , though rarely , that his eye finally becomes living Nature , if indeed it be Nature with a somewhat troubled look . <end>
<start> But the ordinary result is that the whole world thinks it sees Nature where there is only cold glass . <end>
<start> Writing and Desire for Victory . <end>
<start> Writing should always indicate a victory , indeed a conquest of oneself which must be communicated to others for their behalf . <end>
<start> There are , however , dyspeptic authors who only write when they cannon digest something , or when something has remained stuck in their teeth . <end>
<start> Through their anger they try unconsciously to disgust the reader too , and to exercise violence upon him that is , they desire victory , but victory over others . <end>
<start> A Good Book Needs Time . <end>
<start> Every good book tastes bitter when it first comes out , for it has the defect of newness . <end>
<start> Moreover , it suffers damage from its living author , if he is well known and much talked about . <end>
<start> For all the world is accustomed to confuse the author with his work . <end>
<start> Whatever of profundity , sweetness , and brilliance the work may contain must be developed as the years go by , under the care of growing , then old , and lastly traditional reverence . <end>
<start> Many hours must pass , many a spider must have woven its web about the book . <end>
<start> A book is made better by good readers and clearer by good opponents . <end>
<start> Extravagance as an Artistic Means . <end>
<start> Artists well understand the idea of using extravagance as an artistic means in order to convey an impression of wealth . <end>
<start> This is one of those innocent wiles of soul seduction that the artist must know , for in his world , which has only appearance in view , the means to appearance need not necessarily be genuine . <end>
<start> The Hidden Barrel Organ . <end>
<start> Genius , by virtue of its more ample drapery , knows better than talent how to hide its barrel organ . <end>
<start> Yet after all it too can only play its seven old pieces over and over again . <end>
<start> The Name on the Title Page . <end>
<start> It is now a matter of custom and almost of duty for the author s name to appear on the book , and this is a main cause of the fact that books have so little influence . <end>
<start> If they are good , they are worth more than the personalities of their authors , of which they are the quintessence . <end>
<start> But as soon as the author makes himself known on the title page , the quintessence , from the reader s point of view , be comes diluted with the personal , the most personal element , and the aim of the book is frustrated . <end>
<start> It is the ambition of the intellect no longer to appear individual . <end>
<start> The Most Cutting Criticism . <end>
<start> We make the most cutting criticism of a man or a book when we indicate his or its ideal . <end>
<start> Little or no Love . <end>
<start> Every good book is written for a particular reader and men of his stamp , and for that very reason is looked upon unfavourably by all other readers , by the vast majority . <end>
<start> Its reputation accordingly rests on a narrow basis and must be built up by degrees . <end>
<start> The mediocre and bad book is mediocre and bad because it seeks to please , and does please , a great number . <end>
<start> Music and Disease . <end>
<start> The danger of the new music lies in the fact that it puts the cup of rapture and exaltation to the lips so invitingly , and with such a show of moral ecstasy , that even the noble and temperate man always drinks a drop too much . <end>
<start> This minimum of intemperance , , can in the end bring about a deeper convulsion and destruction of mental health than any coarse excess could do . <end>
<start> Hence nothing remains but some day to fly from the grotto of the nymph , and through perils and billowy seas to forge one s way to the smoke of Ithaca and the embraces of a simpler and more human spouse . <end>
<start> Advantage for Opponents . <end>
<start> A book full of intellect communicates something thereof even to its opponents . <end>
<start> Youth and Criticism . <end>
<start> To criticise a book means , for the young , not to let oneself be touched by a single productive thought therefrom , and to protect one s skin with hands and feet . <end>
<start> The youngster lives in opposition to all novelty that he cannon love in the lump , in a position of self-defence , and in this connection he commits , as often as he can , a superfluous sin . <end>
<start> Effect of Quantity . <end>
<start> The greatest paradox in the history of poetic art lies in this : that in all that constitutes the greatness of the old poets a man may be a barbarian , faulty and deformed from top to toe , and still remain the greatest of poets . <end>
<start> This is the case with Shakespeare , who , as compared with Sophocles , is like a mine of immeasurable wealth in gold , lead , and rubble , whereas Sophocles is not merely gold , but gold in its noblest form , one that almost makes us forget the of the metal . <end>
<start> But quantity in its highest intensity has the same effect as quality . <end>
<start> That is a good thing for Shakespeare . <end>
<start> All Beginning is Dangerous . <end>
<start> The Poet can choose whether to raise emotion from one grade to another , and so finally to exalt it to a great height or to try a surprise attack , and from the start to pull the bell rope with might and main . <end>
<start> Both processes have their danger in the first case his hearer may run away from him through boredom , in the second through terror . <end>
<start> In Favour of Critics . <end>
<start> Insects sting , not from malice , but because they too want to live . <end>
<start> It is the same with our critics they desire our blood , not our pain . <end>
<start> Success of Aphorisms . <end>
<start> The inexperienced , when an aphorism at once illuminates their minds with its naked truth , always think that it is old and well known . <end>
<start> They look askance at the author , as if he had wanted to steal the common property of all , whereas they enjoy highly spiced half truths , and give the author to understand as much . <end>
<start> He knows how to appreciate the hint , and easily guesses thereby where he has succeeded and failed . <end>
<start> The Desire for Victory . <end>
<start> An artist who exceeds the limit of his strength in all that he undertakes will end by carrying the multitude along with him through the spectacle of violent wrestling that he affords . <end>
<start> Success is not always the accompaniment only of victory , but also of the desire for victory . <end>
<start> sib S crib ere . <end>
<start> The sensible author writes for no other posterity than his own that is , for his age so as to be able even then to take pleasure in himself . <end>
<start> Praise of the Aphorism . <end>
<start> A good aphorism is too hard for the tooth of time , and is not worn away by all the centuries , although it serves as food for every epoch . <end>
<start> Hence it is the greatest paradox in literature , the imperishable in the midst of change , the nourishment which always remains highly valued , as salt does , and never becomes stupid like salt . <end>
<start> The Art Need of the Second Order . <end>
<start> The people may have something of what can be called art need , but it is small , and can be cheaply satisfied . <end>
<start> On the whole , the remnant of art it must be honestly confessed suffices for this need . <end>
<start> Let us consider , for example , the kind of melodies and songs in which the most vigorous , unspoiled , and true hearted classes of the population find genuine delight let us live among shepherds , cowards , peasants , huntsmen , soldiers , and sailors , and give ourselves the answer . <end>
<start> And in the country town , just in the houses that are the homes of inherited civic virtue , is it not the worst music at present produced that is loved and , one might say , cherished ? <end>
<start> He who speaks of deeper needs and unsatisfied yearnings for art among the people , as it is , is a crank or an impostor . <end>
<start> Be honest ! <end>
<start> Only in exceptional men is there now an art need in the highest sense because art is once more on the down grade , and human powers and hopes are for the time being directed to other matters . <end>
<start> Apart from this , outside the populace , there exists indeed , in the higher and highest strata of society , a broader and more comprehensive partnered , but of the second order . <end>
<start> Here there is a sort of artistic commune , which possibly means to be sincere . <end>
<start> But let us look at the elements ! <end>
<start> They are in general the more refined malcontents , who attain no genuine pleasure in themselves the cultured , who have not become free enough to dispense with the consolations of religion , and yet do not find its incense sufficiently fragrant the half aristocratic , who are too weak to combat by a heroic conversion or renunciation the one fundamental error of their lives or the pernicious bent of their characters the highly gifted , who think themselves too dignified to be of service by modest activity , and are too lazy for real , self sacrificing work girls who cannon create for themselves a satisfactory sphere of duties women who have tied themselves by a light hearted or nefarious marriage , and know that they are not tied securely enough scholars , physicians , merchants , officials who specialised too early and never gave their lives a free enough scope who do their work efficiently , it is true , but with a worm gnawing at their hearts finally , all imperfect artists these are nowadays the true feeders of art ! <end>
<start> What do they really desire from art ? <end>
<start> Art is to drive away hours and moments of discomfort , boredom , half bad conscience , and , if possible , transform the faults of their lives and characters into faults of world destiny . <end>
<start> Very different were the Greeks , who realised in their art the outflow and overflow of their own sense of well being and health , and loved to see their perfection once more from a standpoint outside themselves . <end>
<start> They were led to art by delight in themselves our contemporaries by disgust of themselves . <end>
<start> The Germans in the Theatre . <end>
<start> The real theatrical talent of the Germans was Kotzebue . <end>
<start> He and his Germans , those of higher as well as those of middle class society , were necessarily associated , and his contemporaries should have said of him in all seriousness , in him we live and move and have our being . <end>
<start> Here was nothing no constraint , pretence , or half enjoyment : what he could and would do was understood . <end>
<start> Yes , until now the honest theatrical success on the German stage has been in the hands of the shamefaced or unashamed heirs of Kotzebue s methods and influence that is , as far as comedy still flourishes at all . <end>
<start> The result is that much of the germanium of that age , sometimes far off from the great towns , still survives . <end>
<start> Good natured incontinent in small pleasures always ready for tears with the desire , in the theatre at any rate , to be able to get rid of their innate sobriety and strict attention to duty and exercise a smiling , nay , a laughing indulgence confusing goodness and sum path and welding them into one , as is the essential characteristic of German sentimentality exceedingly happy at a noble , magnanimous action for the rest , submissive towards superiors , envious of each other , and yet in their heart of hearts thoroughly self satisfied such were they and such was he . <end>
<start> The second dramatic talent was Schiller . <end>
<start> He discovered a class of hearers which had hitherto never been taken into consideration : among the callow German youth of both sexes . <end>
<start> His poetry responded to their higher , nobler , more violent if more confused emotions , their delight in the jingle of moral words a delight that begins to disappear when we reach the thirties . <end>
<start> Thus he won for himself , by virtue of the and partisanship of the young , a success which gradually reacted with advantage upon those of riper years . <end>
<start> Generally speaking , Schiller rejuvenated the Germans . <end>
<start> Goethe stood and still stands above the Germans in every respect . <end>
<start> To them he will never belong . <end>
<start> How could a nation in well being and well wishing come up to the intellectuality of Goethe ? <end>
<start> Beethoven composed and Schopenhauer philosophies above the heads of the Germans , and it was above their heads , in the same way , that Goethe wrote his Tasso , his Iphigenie . <end>
<start> He was followed by a small company of highly cultured persons , who were educated by antiquity , life , and travel , and had grown out of German ways of thought . <end>
<start> He himself did not wish it to be otherwise . <end>
<start> When the Romantics set up their well conceived Goethe cult when their amazing skill in appreciation was passed on to the disciples of Hegel , the real educators of the Germans of this century when the awakening national ambition turned out advantageous to the fame of the German poets when the real standard of the nation , as to whether it could honestly find enjoyment in anything , became inexorably subordinated to the judgment of individuals and to that national ambition , that is , when people began to enjoy by compulsion , then arose that false , spurious German culture which was ashamed of Kotzebue which brought Sophocles , Calderon , and even the Second Part of Goethe s Faust on the stage and which , on account of its foul tongue and congested stomach , no longer knows now what it likes and what it finds tedious . <end>
<start> Happy are those who have taste , even if it be a bad taste ! <end>
<start> Only by this characteristic can one be wise as well as happy . <end>
<start> Hence the Greeks , who were very refined in such matters , designated the sage by a word that means man of taste , and called wisdom , artistic as well as scientific , taste sophia . <end>
<start> Music as a Late Comer in every Culture . <end>
<start> Among all the arts that are accustomed to grow on a definite culture soil and under definite social and political conditions , music is the last plant to come up , arising in the autumn and fading season of the culture to which it belongs . <end>
<start> At the same time , the first signs and harbingers of a new spring are usually already noticeable , and sometimes music , like the language of a forgotten age , rings out into a new , astonished world , and comes too late . <end>
<start> In the art of the Dutch and Flemish musicians the soul of the Christian middle ages at last found its fullest tone : their sound architecture is the posthumous but legitimate and equal sister of Gothic . <end>
<start> Not until Handel s music was heard the note of the best in the soul of Luther and his kin , the great Judaeoheroical impulse that created the whole Reformation movement . <end>
<start> Mozart first expressed in golden melody the age of Louis XIV . <end>
<start> and the art of Racine and Claude lorraine . <end>
<start> The eighteenth century that century of rhapsody , of broken ideals and transitory happiness only sang itself out in the music of Beethoven and Rossini . <end>
<start> A lover of sentimental similes might say that all really important music was a swan song . <end>
<start> Music is , in fact , not a universal language for all time , as is so often said in its praise , but responds exactly to a particular period and warmth of emotion which involves a quite definite , individual culture , determined by time and place , as its inner law . <end>
<start> The music of Palestrina would be quite unintelligible to a Greek and again , what would the music of Rossini convey to Palestrina ? <end>
<start> It may be that our most modern German music , with all its preeminence and desire of pre eminence , will soon be no longer understood . <end>
<start> For this music sprang from a culture that is undergoing a rapid decay , from the soil of that epoch of reaction and restoration in which a certain Catholicism of feeling , as well as a delight in all indigenous , national , primitive manners , burst into bloom and scattered a blended perfume over Europe . <end>
<start> These two emotional tendencies , adopted in their greatest strength and carried to their farthest limits , found final expression in the music of Wagner . <end>
<start> Wagner s predilection for the old native sagas , his free idealisation of their unfamiliar gods and heroes , who are really sovereign beasts of prey with occasional fits of thoughtfulness , magnanimity , and boredom , his re animation of those figures , to which he gave in addition the mediaeval Christian thirst for ecstatic sensuality and all this Wagnerian give and take with regard to materials , souls , figures , and words would clearly express the spirit of his music , if it could not , like all music , speak quite unambiguously of itself . <end>
<start> This spirit wages the last campaign of reaction against the spirit of illumination which passed into this century from the last , and also against the supranational ideas of French revolutionary romanticism and of English and American in the reconstruction of state and society . <end>
<start> But is it not evident that the spheres of thought and emotion apparently suppressed by Wagner and his school have long since acquired fresh strength , and that his late musical protest against them generally rings into ears that prefer to hear different and opposite notes so that one day that high and wonderful art will suddenly become unintelligible and will be covered by the spider s web of oblivion ? <end>
<start> In considering this state of affairs we must not let ourselves be led astray by those transitory fluctuations which arise like a reaction within a reaction , as a temporary sinking of the mountainous wave in the midst of the general upheaval . <end>
<start> Thus , this decade of national war , ultramontane martyrdom , and socialistic unrest may , in its remoter after effect , even aid the Wagnerian art to acquire a sudden halo , without guaranteeing that it has a future or that itpoHUMAN , ALL TOO HUMAN . <end>
<start> has the future . <end>
<start> It is in the very nature of music that the fruits of its great culture vintage should lose their taste and wither earlier than the fruits of the plastic arts or those that grow on the tree of knowledge . <end>
<start> Among all the products of the human artistic sense ideas are the most solid and lasting . <end>
<start> The Poet no longer a Teacher . <end>
<start> Strange as it may sound to our time , there were once poets and artists whose soul was above the passions with their delights and convulsions , and who therefore took their pleasure in purer materials , worthier men , more delicate complications and denouement . <end>
<start> If the artists of our day for the most part unfettered the will , and so are under certain circumstances for that very reason emancipators of life , those were tamers of the will , enchanter of animals , creators of men . <end>
<start> In fact , they moulded , re moulded , and new moulded life , whereas the fame of poets of our day lies in harnessing , unchanging , and shattering . <end>
<start> The ancient Greeks demanded of the poet that he should be the teacher of grown men . <end>
<start> How ashamed the poet would be now if this demand were made of him ! <end>
<start> He is not even a good student of himself , and so never himself becomes a good poem or a fine picture . <end>
<start> Under the most favourable circumstances he remains the shy , attractive ruin of a temple , but at the same time a cavern of cravings , overgrown like a ruin with flowers , nettles , and poisonous weeds , inhabited and haunted by snakes , worms , spiders , and birds an object for sad reflection as to why the noblest and most precious must grow up at once . <end>
<start> Looking Forward and Backward . <end>
<start> An art like that which streams out of Homer , Sophocles , Theocritus , Calderon , Racine , Goethe , as the superabundance of a wise and harmonious conduct of life that is the true art , at which we grasp when we have ourselves become wiser and more harmonious . <end>
<start> It is not that barbaric , if ever so delightful , outpouring of hot and highly coloured things from an undisciplined , chaotic soul , which is what we understood by art in our youth . <end>
<start> It is obvious from the nature of the case that for certain periods of life an art of , excitement , antipathy to the orderly , monotonous , simple , logical , is an inevitable need , to which artists must respond , lest the soul of such periods should unburden itself in other ways , through all kinds of disorder and impropriety . <end>
<start> Hence youths as they generally are , full , fermenting , tortured above all things by boredom , and women who lack work that fully occupies their soul , require that art of delightful disorder . <end>
<start> All the more violently on that account are they inflamed with a desire for satisfaction without change , happiness without stupor and intoxication . <end>
<start> Against the Art of Works of Art . <end>
<start> Art is above all and first of all meant to embellish life , to make us ourselves endurable and if possible agreeable in the eyes of others . <end>
<start> With this task in view , art moderates us and holds us in restraint , creates forms of intercourse , binds over the uneducated to laws of decency , cleanliness , politeness , well timed speech and silence . <end>
<start> Hence art must conceal or transfigured everything that is ugly the painful , terrible , and disgusting elements which in spite of every effort will always break out afresh in accordance with the very origin of human nature . <end>
<start> Art has to perform this duty especially in regard to the passions and spiritual agonies and anxieties , and to cause the significant factor to shine through unavoidable or unconquerable ugliness . <end>
<start> To this great , task the so called art proper , that of works of art , is a mere accessory . <end>
<start> A man who feels within himself a surplus of such powers of embellishment , concealment , and transfiguration will finally seek to unburden himself of this surplus in works of art . <end>
<start> The same holds good , under special circumstances , of a whole nation . <end>
<start> But as a rule we nowadays begin art at the end , hang on to its tail , and think that works of art constitute art proper , and that life should be improved and transformed by this means fools that we are ! <end>
<start> If we begin a dinner with dessert , and try sweet after sweet , small wonder that we ruin our digestions and even our appetites for the good , hearty , nourishing meal to which art invites us ! <end>
<start> Continued Existence of Art . <end>
<start> Why , really , does a creative art nowadays continue to exist ? <end>
<start> Because the majority who have hours of leisure and such an art is for them only think that they cannon fill up their time without music , theatres and , novels and poetry . <end>
<start> Granted that one could keep them from this indulgence , either they would strive less eagerly for leisure , and the invidious sight of the rich would be less common a great gain for the stability of society , or they would have leisure , but would learn to reflect on what can be learnt and unlearn : on their work , for instance , their associations , the pleasure they could bestow . <end>
<start> All the world , with the exception of the artist , would in both cases reap the advantage . <end>
<start> Certainly , there are many vigorous , sensible readers who could take objection to this . <end>
<start> Still , it must be said on behalf of the coarse and malignant that the author himself is concerned with this protest , and that there is in his book much to be read that is not actually written down therein . <end>
<start> The Mouthpiece of the Gods . <end>
<start> The poet expresses the universal higher opinions of the nation , he is its mouthpiece and flute but by virtue of metre and all other artistic means he so expresses them that the nation regards them as something quite new and wonderful , and believes in all seriousness that he is the mouthpiece of the Gods . <end>
<start> Yes , under the clouds of creation the poet himself forgets whence he derives all his intellectual wisdom from father and mother , from teachers and books of all kinds , from the street and particularly from the priest . <end>
<start> He is deceived by his own art , and really believes , in a naive period , that a God is speaking through him , that he is creating in a state of religious inspiration . <end>
<start> As a matter of fact , he is only saying what he has learnt , a medley of popular wisdom and popular foolishness . <end>
<start> Hence , so far as a poet is really vox popular he is held to be vox dei . <end>
<start> What all Art wants to Do and cannon . <end>
<start> The last and hardest task of the artist is the resentment of what remains the same , repose in itself , is lofty and simple and free from the bizarre . <end>
<start> Hence the noblest forms of moral perfection are rejected as inartistic by weaker artists , because the sight of these fruits is too painful for their ambition . <end>
<start> The fruit gleams at them from the topmost branches of art , but they lack the ladder , the courage , the grip to venture so high . <end>
<start> In himself a pheidias is quite possible as a poet , but , if modern strength be taken into consideration , almost solely in the sense that to God nothing is impossible . <end>
<start> The desire for a poetical Claude lorraine is already an immodest at present , however earnestly one man s heart may yearn for such a consummation . <end>
<start> The resentment of the highest man , the most simple and at the same time the most complete , has hitherto been beyond the scope of all artists . <end>
<start> Perhaps , however , the Greeks , in the ideal of Athene , saw farther than any men did before or after their time . <end>
<start> Art and Restoration . <end>
<start> The retrograde movements in history , the so called periods of restoration , which try to revive intellectual and social conditions that existed before those immediately preceding , and seem really to succeed in giving them a brief resurrection , have the charm of sentimental recollection , ardent longing for what is almost lost , hasty embracing of a transitory happiness . <end>
<start> It is on account of this strange trend towards seriousness that in such transient and almost dreamy periods art and poetry find a natural soil , just as the tenderest and rarest plants grow on mountain slopes of steep declivity . <end>
<start> Thus many a good artist is unwittingly impelled to a restoration way of thinking in politics and society , for which , on his own account , he prepares a quiet little corner and garden . <end>
<start> Here he collects about himself the human remains of the historical epoch that appeals to him , and plays his lyre to many who are dead , half dead , and weary to death , perhaps with the above mentioned result of a brief resurrection . <end>
<start> Happiness of the Age . <end>
<start> In two respects our age is to be accounted happy . <end>
<start> With respect to the past , we enjoy all cultures and their productions , and nurture ourselves on the noblest blood of all periods . <end>
<start> We stand sufficiently near to the magic of the forces from whose womb these periods are born to be able in passing to submit to their spell with pleasure and terror whereas earlier cultures could only enjoy themselves , and never looked beyond themselves , but were rather overreached by a bell of broader or narrower dome , through which indeed light streamed down to them , but which their gaze could not pierce . <end>
<start> With respect to the future , there opens out to us for the first time a mighty , comprehensive vista of human and economic purposes encircling the whole inhabited globe . <end>
<start> At the same time , we feel conscious of a power ourselves to take this new task in hand without presumption , without requiring supernatural aids . <end>
<start> Yes , whatever the result of our enterprise , however much we may have overestimated our strength , at any rate we need render account to no one but ourselves , and mankind can henceforth begin to do with itself what it will . <end>
<start> There are , it is true , peculiar human bees , who only know how to suck the bitterest and worst elements from the chalice of every flower . <end>
<start> It is true that all flowers contain something that is not honey , but these bees may be allowed to feel in their own way about the happiness of our time , and continue to build up their hive of discomfort . <end>
<start> A Vision . <end>
<start> Hours of instruction and meditation for adults , even the most mature , and such institutions visited without compulsion but in accordance with the moral injunction of the whole community the churches as the meeting places most worthy and rich in memories for the purpose at the same time daily festivals in honour of the reason that is attained and attainable by man a newer and fuller budding and blooming of the ideal of the teacher , in which the clergyman , the artist and the physician , the man of science and the sage are blended , and their individual virtues should come to the fore as a collective virtue in their teaching itself , in their discourses , in their method this is my ever recurring vision , of which I firmly believe that it has raised a corner of the veil of the future . <end>
<start> Education a Distortion . <end>
<start> The extraordinary of the whole system of education , which leads every adult to say nowadays that his sole educator was chance , and the weathercock nature of educational methods and aims , may be explained as follows . <end>
<start> The oldest and the newest , as in a turbulent mass meeting , would rather be heard than understood , and wish to prove at all costs by their outcries and clamouring that they still exist or already exist . <end>
<start> The poor teachers and educators are first dazed by this senseless noise , then become silent and finally apathetic , allowing anything to be done to them just as they in their turn allow anything to be done to their pupils . <end>
<start> They are not trained themselves , so how are they to train others ? <end>
<start> They are themselves no , vigorous , succulent trees , and he who wishes to attach himself to them must wind and bend himself and finally become distorted and deformed as they . <end>
<start> Philosophers and Artists of the Age . <end>
<start> Rhapsody and frigidity , burning desires and waning of the heart s glow this wretched medley is to be found in the picture of the highest European society of the present day . <end>
<start> There the artist thinks vol . <end>
<start> G that he is achieving a great deal when through his art he lights the torch of the heart as well as the torch of desire . <end>
<start> The philosopher has the same notion , when in the chilliness of his heart , which he has in common with his age , he cools hot desires in himself and his following by his world denying judgments . <end>
<start> Not to be a Soldier of Culture without Necessity . <end>
<start> At last people are learning what it costs us so dear not to know in our youth that we must first do superior actions and secondly seek the superior wherever and under whatever names it is to be found that we must at once go out of the way of all badness and mediocrity without fighting it and that even doubt as to the excellence of a thing such as quickly arises in one of practised taste should rank as an argument against it and a reason for completely avoiding it . <end>
<start> We must not shrink from the danger of occasionally making a mistake and confounding the less accessible good with the bad and imperfect . <end>
<start> Only he who can do nothing better should attack the world s evils as the soldier of culture . <end>
<start> But those who should support culture and spread its teachings ruin themselves if they go about armed , and by precautions , , and bad dreams turn the peace of their domestic and artistic life into sinister unrest . <end>
<start> How Natural History should be Expounded . <end>
<start> Natural history , like the history of the war and victory of moral and intellectual forces in the campaign against anxiety , self delusion , laziness , superstition , folly , should be so expounded that every reader or listener may be continually aroused to strive after mental and physical health and soundness , after the feeling of joy , and be awakened to the desire to be the heir and continuator of mankind , to an ever nobler adventurous impulse . <end>
<start> Hitherto natural history has not found its true language , because the inventive and eloquent artists who are needed for this purpose never rid themselves of a secret mistrust of it , and above all never wish to learn from it a thorough lesson . <end>
<start> Nevertheless it must be conceded to the English that their scientific manuals for the lower strata of the people have made admirable strides towards that ideal . <end>
<start> But then such books are written by their foremost men of learning , full , complete , and inspiring natures , and not , as among us , by mediocre investigators . <end>
<start> Genius in Humanity . <end>
<start> If genius , according to Schopenhauer s observation , lies in the coherent and vivid recollection of our own experience , a striving towards genius in humanity collectively might be deduced from the striving towards knowledge of the whole historic past which is beginning to mark off the modern age more and more as compared with earlier ages and has for the first time broken down the barriers between nature and spirit , men and animals , morality and physics . <end>
<start> A perfectly conceived history would be cosmic self-consciousness . <end>
<start> inhuman , ALL TOO HUMAN . <end>
<start> The Cult of Culture . <end>
<start> On great minds is bestowed the terrifying all too human of their natures , their blindness , deformities , and extravagances , so that their more powerful , easily all-too powerful influence may be continually held within bounds through the distrust aroused by such qualities . <end>
<start> For the sum total of all that humanity needs for its continued existence is so comprehensive , and demands powers so diverse and so numerous , that for every one sided predilection , whether in science or politics or art or commerce , to which such natures would persuade us , mankind as a whole has to pay a heavy price . <end>
<start> It has always been a great disaster to culture when human beings are worshipped . <end>
<start> In this sense we may understand the precept of Mosaic law which forbids us to have any other gods but God . <end>
<start> Side by side with the cult of genius and violence we must always place , as its complement and remedy , the cult of culture . <end>
<start> This cult can find an intelligent appreciation even for the material , the inferior , the mean , the misunderstood , the weak , the imperfect , the one-sided , the incomplete , the untrue , the apparent , even the wicked and horrible , and can grant them the concession that all this is necessary . <end>
<start> For the continued harmony of all things human , attained by amazing toil and strokes of luck , and just as much the work of cyclops and ants as of geniuses , shall never be lost . <end>
<start> How , indeed , could we dispense with that deep , universal , and often uncannyMISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> ii bass , without which , after all , melody cannon be melody ? <end>
<start> The Antique World and Pleasure . <end>
<start> The man of the antique world understood better how to rejoice , we understand better how to grieve less . <end>
<start> They continually found new motives for feeling happy , for celebrating festivals , being inventive with all their wealth of shrewdness and reflection . <end>
<start> We , on the other hand , concentrate our intellect rather on the solving of problems which have in view pointlessness and the removal of sources of discomfort . <end>
<start> With regard to suffering existence , the ancients sought to forget or in some way to convert the sensation into a pleasant one , thus trying to supply palliatives . <end>
<start> We attack the causes of suffering , and on the whole prefer to use prophylactic . <end>
<start> Perhaps we are only building upon a foundation whereon a later age will once more set up the temple of joy . <end>
<start> The Muses as Liars . <end>
<start> We know how to tell many lies , so sang the Muses once , when they revealed themselves to Hesiod . <end>
<start> The conception of the artist as deceiver , once grasped , leads to important discoveries . <end>
<start> How Paradoxical Homer can be . <end>
<start> Is there anything more desperate , more horrible , more incredible , shining over human destiny like a winter sun , than that idea of Homer s : So the decree of the Gods willed it , and doomed man to perish , that it might be a matter for song even to distant generations ? <end>
<start> In other words , we suffer and perish so that poets may not lack material , and this is the dispensation of those very Gods of Homer who seem much concerned about the joyousness of generations to come , but very little about us men of the present . <end>
<start> To think that such ideas should ever have entered the head of a Greek ! <end>
<start> Supplementary Justification of Existence . <end>
<start> Many ideas have come into the world as errors and fancies but have turned out truths , because men have afterwards given them a genuine basis to rest upon . <end>
<start> Pro and Con Necessary . <end>
<start> He who has not realised that every great man must not only be encouraged but also , for the sake of the common welfare , opposed , is certainly still a great child or himself a great man . <end>
<start> Injustice of Genius . <end>
<start> Genius is most unjust towards geniuses , if they be contemporary . <end>
<start> Either it thinks it has no need of them and considers them superfluous for it can do without them , or their influence crosses the path of its electric current , in which case it even calls them pernicious . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> The Saddest Destiny of a Prophet . <end>
<start> He has worked twenty years to convince his contemporaries , and succeeds at last , but in the meantime his adversaries have also succeeded he is no longer convinced of himself . <end>
<start> Three Thinkers like one Spider . <end>
<start> In every philosophical school three thinkers follow one another in this relation : the first produces from himself sap and seed , the second draws it out in threads and spins a cunning web , the third waits in this web for the victims who are caught in it and tries to live upon this philosophy . <end>
<start> From Association with Authors . <end>
<start> It is as bad a habit to go about with an author grasping him by the nose as grasping him by the horn and every author has his horn . <end>
<start> A Team of Two . <end>
<start> Vagueness of thought and outbursts of sentimentality are as often wedded to the reckless desire to have one s own way by hook or by crook , to make oneself alone of any consequence , as a genuinely helpful , gracious , and kindly spirit is wedded to the impulse towards clearness and purity of thought and towards emotional moderation and self restraint . <end>
<start> Binding and Separating Forces . <end>
<start> Surely it is in the heads of men that there arises the force that binds them an understanding of their common interest or the reverse and in their hearts the force that separates them a blind choosing and groping in love and hate , a devotion to one at the expense of all , and a consequent contempt for the common utility . <end>
<start> Marksmen and Thinkers . <end>
<start> There are curious marksmen who miss their mark , but leave the shooting gallery with secret pride in the fact that their bullet at any rate flew very far beyond the mark , it is true , or that it did not hit the mark but hit something else . <end>
<start> There are thinkers of the same stamp . <end>
<start> Attack from Two Sides . <end>
<start> We act as enemies towards an intellectual tendency or movement when we are superior to it and disapprove of its aim , or when its aim is too high and unrecognisable to our eye in other words , when it is superior to us . <end>
<start> So the same party may be attacked from two sides , from above and from below . <end>
<start> Not infrequently the assailants , from common hatred , form an alliance which is more repulsive than all that they hate . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> ORIGINAL . <end>
<start> Original minds are distinguished not by being the first to see a new thing , but by seeing the old , well known thing , which is seen and overlooked by every one , as something new . <end>
<start> The first discoverer is usually that quite ordinary and intellectual visionary chance . <end>
<start> Error of Philosophers . <end>
<start> The philosopher believes that the value of his philosophy lies in the whole , in the structure . <end>
<start> Posterity finds it in the stone with which he built and with which , from that time forth , men will build oftener and better in other words , in the fact that the structure may be destroyed and yet have value as material . <end>
<start> Wit . <end>
<start> Wit is the epitaph of an emotion . <end>
<start> The Moment before Solution . <end>
<start> In science it occurs every day and every hour that a man , immediately before the solution , remains stuck , being convinced that his efforts have been entirely in vain like one who , in untying a noose , hesitates at the moment when it is nearest to coming loose , because at that very moment it looks most like a knot . <end>
<start> Among the Visionaries . <end>
<start> The thoughtful man , and he who is sure of his intelligence , may profitably consort with visionaries for a decade and abandon himself in their torrid zone to a moderate insanity . <end>
<start> He will thus have travelled a good part of the road towards that cosmopolitanism of the intellect which can say without presumption , Nothing intellectual is alien to me . <end>
<start> Keen Air . <end>
<start> The best and healthiest element in science as amid the mountains is the keen air that plays about it . <end>
<start> Intellectual molly cuddles such as artists dread and abuse science on account of this atmosphere . <end>
<start> Why Savants are Nobler than Artists . <end>
<start> Science requires nobler natures than does poetry natures that are more simple , less ambitious , more restrained , calmer , that think less of posthumous fame and can bury themselves in studies which , in the eye of the many , scarcely seem worthy of such a sacrifice of personality . <end>
<start> There is another loss of which they are conscious . <end>
<start> The nature of their occupation , its continual exaction of the greatest sobriety , weakens their will the fire is not kept up so vigorously as on the hearths of poetic minds . <end>
<start> As such , they often lose their strength and prime earlier than artists do and , as has been said , they are aware of their danger . <end>
<start> Under all circumstances they seem less gifted because they shine less , and thus they will always be rated below their value . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> How Far Piety Obscures . <end>
<start> In later centuries the great man is credited with all the great qualities and virtues of his century . <end>
<start> Thus all that is best is continually obscured by piety , which treats the picture as a sacred one , to be surrounded with all manner of votive offerings . <end>
<start> In the end the picture is completely veiled and covered by the offerings , and thenceforth is more an object of faith than of contemplation . <end>
<start> Standing on One s Head . <end>
<start> If we make truth stand on its head , we generally fail to notice that our own head , too , is not in its right position . <end>
<start> Origin and Utility of Fashion . <end>
<start> The obvious satisfaction of the individual with his own form excites imitation and gradually creates the form of the many that is , fashion . <end>
<start> The many desire , and indeed attain , that same comforting satisfaction with their own form . <end>
<start> Consider how many reasons every man has for anxiety and shy self concealment , and how , on this account , three fourths of his energy and goodwill is crippled and may become unproductive ! <end>
<start> So we must be very grateful to fashion for unflattering that three fourths and communicating self confidence and the power of cheerful compromise to those who feel themselves bound to each other by its law . <end>
<start> Even foolish laws give freedom and calm of the spirit , so long as many persons have submitted to their sway . <end>
<start> looseness of Tongues . <end>
<start> The value of many men and books rests solely on their faculty for compelling all to speak out the most hidden and intimate things . <end>
<start> They are looseness of tongues and crowbars to open the most stubborn teeth . <end>
<start> Many events and misdeeds which are apparently only sent as a curse to mankind possess this value and utility . <end>
<start> Intellectual Freedom of Domicile . <end>
<start> Who of us could dare to call himself a free spirit if he could not render homage after his fashion , by taking on his own shoulders a portion of that burden of public dislike and abuse , to men to whom this name is attached as a reproach ? <end>
<start> We might as well call ourselves in all seriousness spirits free of domicile Freiziigig and without that arrogant or high-spirited defiance because we feel the impulse to freedom dug zur freshest as the strongest instinct of our minds and , in contrast to fixed and limited minds , practically see our ideal in an intellectual nomadism to use a modest and almost deprecatory expression . <end>
<start> The original word , Freiziigig means , in the modern German Empire , possessing the free right of migration , without pecuniary burdens or other restrictions , from one German state to another . <end>
<start> The play on words in dug zur freshest impulse to freedom is untranslatable . <end>

<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> IO . <end>
<start> Yes , the Favour of the infuses ! <end>
<start> What Homer says on this point goes right to our heart , so true , so terrible is it : The Muse loved him with all her heart and gave him good and evil , for she took away his eyes and vouchsafed him sweet song . <end>
<start> This is an endless text for thinking men : she gives good and evil , that is her manner of loving with all her heart and soul ! <end>
<start> And each man will interpret specially for himself why we poets and thinkers have to give up our eyes in her service . <end>
<start> Against the Cultivation of Music The artistic training of the eye from childhood upwards by means of drawing , painting , landscape sketching , figures , scenes , involves an estimable gain in life , making the eyesight keen , calm , and enduring in the observation of men and circumstances . <end>
<start> No similar secondary advantage arises from the artistic cultivation of the ear , whence public schools will generally do well to give the art of the eye a preference over that of the ear . <end>
<start> The Discoverers of Trivialities . <end>
<start> Subtle minds , from which nothing is farther than trivialities , often discover a triviality after taking all manner of Nietzsche seems to allude to his own case , for he ultimately contracted a myopia which bordered on blindness . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> ii HUMAN , ALL TOO HUMAN . <end>
<start> circuitous routes and mountain paths , and , to the astonishment of the non subtle , rejoice exceedingly . <end>
<start> Morals of Savants . <end>
<start> A regular and rapid advance in the sciences is only possible when the individual is compelled to be not so distrustful as to test every calculation and assertion of others , in fields which are remote from his own . <end>
<start> A necessary condition , however , is that every man should have competitors in his own sphere , who are extremely distrustful and keep a sharp eye upon him . <end>
<start> From this juxtaposition of not too distrustful and extremely distrustful arises sincerity in the republic of learning . <end>
<start> Reasons for Sterility . <end>
<start> There are highly gifted minds which are always sterile only because , from temperamental weakness , they are too impatient to wait for their pregnancy . <end>
<start> The Perverted World of Tears . <end>
<start> The manifold discomforts which the demands of higher culture cause to man finally pervert his nature to such an extent that he usually keeps himself stoical and unbending . <end>
<start> Thus he has tears in reserve only for rare occasions of happiness , so that many must weep even at the enjoyment of pointlessness only when happy does his heart still beat . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> Ill . <end>
<start> The Greeks as Interpreters . <end>
<start> When we speak of the Greeks we unwittingly speak of to day and yesterday their universally known history is a blank mirror , always reflecting something that is not in the mirror itself . <end>
<start> We enjoy the freedom of speaking about them in order to have the right of being silent about others so that these Greeks themselves may whisper something in the ear of the reflective reader . <end>
<start> Thus the Greeks facilitate to modern men the communication of much that is debatable and hard to communicate . <end>
<start> Of the Acquired Character oftheGreeks . <end>
<start> We are easily led astray by the renowned Greek clearness , transparency , simplicity , and order , by their crystal like naturalness and crystal like art , into believing that all these gifts were bestowed on the Greeks for instance , that they could not but write well , as Lichtenberg expressed it on one occasion . <end>
<start> Yet no statement could be more hasty and more untenable . <end>
<start> The history of prose from gorgios to Demosthenes shows a course of toiling and wrestling towards light from the obscure , overloaded , and tasteless , reminding one of the labour of heroes who had to construct the first roads through forest and bog . <end>
<start> The dialogue of tragedy was the real achievement of the dramatist , owing to its uncommon clearness and precision , whereas the national tendency was to riot in symbolism and innuendo , a tendency expressly fostered by the great . <end>
