<start> Similarly it was the achievement of Homer to liberate the Greeks from Asiatic pomp and gloom , and to have attained the clearness of architecture in details great and small . <end>
<start> Nor was it by any means thought easy to say anything in a pure and illuminating style . <end>
<start> How else should we account for the great admiration for the epigram of Simonides , which shows itself so simple , with no gilded points or arabesques of wit , but says all that it has to say plainly and with the calm of the sun , not with the straining after effect of the lightning . <end>
<start> Since the struggle towards light from an almost native twilight is Greek , a thrill of jubilation runs through the people when they hear a laconic sentence , the language of elegy or the maxims of the Seven Wise Men . <end>
<start> Hence they were so fond of giving precepts in verse , a practice that we find objectionable . <end>
<start> This was the true Apolline task of the Hellenic spirit , with the aim of rising superior to the perils of metre and the obscurity which is otherwise characteristic of poetry . <end>
<start> Simplicity , flexibility , and sobriety were wrestled for and not given by nature to this people . <end>
<start> The danger of a relapse into arianism constantly hovered over the Greeks , and really overtook them from time to time like a murky , overflowing tide of mystical impulses , primitive savagery and darkness . <end>
<start> We see them plunge in we see Europe , as it were , flooded , washed away for Europe was very small then but they always emerge once more to the light , good swimmers and divers that they are , those fellow countrymen of Odysseus . <end>
<start> miscellaneous MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> II . <end>
<start> The Pagan Characteristic . <end>
<start> Perhaps there is nothing more astonishing to the observer of the Greek world than to discover that the Greeks from time to time held festivals , as it were , for all their passions and evil tendencies alike , and in fact even established a kind of series of festivals , by order of the State , for their all too human . <end>
<start> This is the pagan characteristic of their world , which Christianityhas never understood and never can understand , and has always combated and despised . <end>
<start> They accepted this all too human as unavoidable , and preferred , instead of railing at it , to give it a kind of secondary right by grafting it on to the usages of society and religion . <end>
<start> All in man that has power they called divine , and wrote it on the walls of their heaven . <end>
<start> They do not deny this natural instinct that expresses itself in evil characteristics , but regulate and limit it to definite cults and days , so as to turn those turbulent streams into as harmless a course as possible , after devising sufficient precautionary measures . <end>
<start> That is the root of all the moral broad mindedness of antiquity . <end>
<start> To the wicked , the dubious , the backward , the animal element , as to the barbaric , pre-hellenic and Asiatic , which still lived in the depths of Greek nature , they allowed a moderate outflow , and did not strive to destroy it utterly . <end>
<start> The whole system was under the domain of the State , which was built up not on individuals or castes , but on common human qualities . <end>
<start> In the structure of the State the Greeks show that wonderful sense for typical facts which later on enabled them to become investigators of Nature , historians , geographers , and vol . <end>
<start> H philosophers . <end>
<start> It was not a limited moral law of priests or castes , which had to decide about the constitution of the State and State worship , but the most comprehensive view of the reality of all that is human . <end>
<start> Whence do the Greeks derive this freedom , this sense of reality ? <end>
<start> Perhaps from Homer and the poets who preceded him . <end>
<start> For just those poets whose nature is generally not the most wise or just possess , in compensation , that delight in reality and activity of every kind , and prefer not to deny even evil . <end>
<start> It suffices for them if evil moderates itself , does not kill or inwardly poison everything in other words , they have similar ideas to those of the founders of Greek constitutions , and were their teachers and forerunners . <end>
<start> Exceptional Greeks . <end>
<start> In Greece , deep , thorough , serious minds were the exception . <end>
<start> The national instinct tended rather to regard the serious and thorough as a kind of grimace . <end>
<start> To borrow forms from a foreign source , not to create but to transform into the fairest shapes that is Greek . <end>
<start> To imitate , not for utility but for artistic illusion , ever and anon to gain the mastery over forced seriousness , to arrange , beautify , simplify that is the continual task from Homer to the sophist of the third and fourth centuries of our era , who are all outward show , pompous speech , declamatory gestures , and address themselves to shallow souls that care only for appearance , sound , and effect . <end>
<start> And now let us estimate the greatness of those exceptional Greeks , who created science ! <end>
<start> Whoever tells of them , tells the most heroic story of the human mind ! <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> Simplicity not the First nor the Last Thing in Point of Time . <end>
<start> In the history of religious ideas many errors about development and false gradations are made in matters which in reality are not consecutive outgrowths but contemporary yet separate phenomena . <end>
<start> In particular , simplicity has still far too much the reputation of being the oldest , the initial thing . <end>
<start> Much that is human arises by subtraction and division , and not merely by doubling , addition , and unification . <end>
<start> For instance , men still believe in a gradual development of the idea of God from those unwieldy stones and blocks of wood up to the highest forms of anthropomorphism . <end>
<start> Yet the fact is that so long as divinity was attributed to and felt in trees , logs of wood , stones , and beasts , people shrank from womanising their forms as from an act of godlessness . <end>
<start> First of all , poets , apart from all considerations of cult and the ban of religious shame , have had to make the inner imagination of man accustomed and compliant to this notion . <end>
<start> Wherever more pious periods and phases of thought gained the upper hand , this liberating influence of poets fell into the background , and sanctity remained , after as before , on the side of the monstrous , uncanny , quite peculiarly inhuman . <end>
<start> And then , much of what the inner imagination ventures to picture to itself would exert a painful influence if externally and corporeal represented . <end>
<start> The inner eye is far bolder and more shameless than the outer whence the well known difficulty and , to some extent , impossibility , of working epic material into dramatic form . <end>
<start> The religious imagination for a long time entirely refuses to believe in the identity of God with an image : the image is meant to fix the lumen of the Deity , actually and specifically , although in a mysterious and not altogether intelligible way . <end>
<start> The oldest image of the Gods is meant to shelter and at the same time to hide the God to indicate him but not to expose him to view . <end>
<start> No Greek really looked upon his Apollo as a pointed pillar of wood , his Eros as a lump of stone . <end>
<start> These were symbols , which were intended to inspire dread of the manifestation of the God . <end>
<start> It was the same with those blocks of wood out of which individual limbs , generally in excessive number , were fashioned with the shanties of carving as , for instance , a lacanian Apollo with four hands and four ears . <end>
<start> In the incomplete , symbolic , or excessive lies a terrible sanctity , which is meant to prevent us from thinking of anything human or similar to humanity . <end>
<start> It is not an embryonic stage of art in which such things are made as if they were not able to speak more plainly and portray more sensibly in the age when such images were honoured ! <end>
<start> Rather , men are afraid of just one thing direct speaking out . <end>
<start> Just as the cella hides and conceals in a mysterious twilight , yet not completely , the holy of holies , the real lumen of the Deity just as , again , the peripatetic temple hides the cella , protecting it from indiscreet eyes as with a screen and a veil , yet not completely so it is with the image of the Deity , and at the same time the concealment of The play on bergen shelter and hide is untranslatable . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> the Deity . <end>
<start> Only when outside the cult , in the profane world of athletic contest , the joy in the victor had risen so high that the ripples thus started reacted upon the lake of religious emotion , was the statue of the victor set up before the temple . <end>
<start> Then the pious pilgrim had to accustom his eye and his soul , whether he would or no , to the inevitable sight of human beauty and super strength , so that the worship of men and Gods melted into each other from physical and spiritual contact . <end>
<start> Then too for the first time the fear of really womanising the figures of the Gods is lost , and the mighty arena for great plastic art is opened even now with the limitation that wherever there is to be adoration the primitive form and ugliness are carefully preserved and copied . <end>
<start> But the helene , as he dedicates and makes offerings , may now with religious sanction indulge in his delight in making God become a man . <end>
<start> Whither We must Travel . <end>
<start> Immediate is not enough , by a long way , to enable us to learn to know ourselves . <end>
<start> We need history , for the past continues to flow through us in a hundred channels . <end>
<start> We ourselves are , after all , nothing but our own sensation at every moment of this continued flow . <end>
<start> Even here , when we wish to step down into the stream of our apparently most peculiar and personal development , Heraclitus aphorism , You cannon step twice into the same river , holds good . <end>
<start> This is a piece of wisdom which has , indeed , gradually become trite , but nevertheless has remained as strong and true as it ever was . <end>
<start> It is the same with the saying that , in order to understand history , we must scrutinise the living remains of historical periods that we must travel , as old Herodotus travelled , to other nations , especially to those so called savage or half savage races in regions where man has doffed or not yet donned European garb . <end>
<start> For they are ancient and firmly established steps of culture on which we can stand . <end>
<start> There is , however , a more subtle art and aim in travelling , which does not always necessitate our passing from place to place and going thousands of miles away . <end>
<start> Very probably the last three centuries , in all their colourings and refraction of culture , survive even in our vicinity , only they have to be discovered . <end>
<start> In some families , or even in individuals , the strata are still superimposed on each other , beautifully and perceptibly in other places there are dispersions and displacements of the structure which are harder to understand . <end>
<start> Certainly in remote districts , in less known mountain valleys , circumscribed communities have been able more easily to maintain an admirable pattern of a far older sentiment , a pattern that must here be investigated . <end>
<start> On the other hand , it is improbable that such discoveries will be made in Berlin , where man comes into the world washed-out and hapless . <end>
<start> He who after long practice of this art of travel has become a hundred eyed Argus will accompany his Io I mean his ego everywhere , and in Egypt and Greece , Byzantium and Rome , France and Germany , in the age of wandering or settled races , in Renaissance or Reformation , at home and abroad , in sea , forest , plant , and mountain , will again light upon the travel adventure of this ever growing , ever altered ego . <end>
<start> Thus self knowledge becomes universal knowledge as regards the entire past , and , by another chain of observation , which can only be indicated here , self direction and self training in the freest and most far seeing spirits might become universal direction as regards all future humanity . <end>
<start> Balm and Poison . <end>
<start> We cannon ponder too deeply on this fact : Christianity is the religion of antiquity grown old it presupposes degenerate old culture stocks , and on them it had , and still has , power to work like balm . <end>
<start> There are periods when ears and eyes are full of slime , so that they can no longer hear the voice of reason and philosophy or see the wisdom that walks in bodily shape , whether it bears the name of Epictetus or of Epicurus . <end>
<start> Then , perhaps , the erection of the martyr s cross and the trumpet of the last judgment may have the effect of still inspiring such races to end their lives decently . <end>
<start> If we think of Juvenal s Rome , of that poisonous toad with the eyes of Venus , we understand what it means to make the sign of the Cross before the world , we honour the silent Christian community and are grateful for its having stifled the Greco Roman Empire . <end>
<start> If , indeed , most men were then born in spiritual slavery , with the sensuality of old men , what a pleasure to meet beings who were more soul than body , and who seemed to realise the Greek idea of the shades of the under world shy , scurrying , chirping , kindly creatures , with a never sion on the better life , and therefore so unassuming , so secretly scornful , so proudly patient ! <end>
<start> This Christianity , as the evening chime of the good antiquity , with cracked , weary and yet melodious bell , is balm in the ears even to one who only now traverses those centuries historically . <end>
<start> What must it have been to those men themselves ! <end>
<start> To young and fresh barbarian nations , on the other hand , Christianity is a poison . <end>
<start> For to implant the teaching of sinfulness and damnation in the heroic , childlike , and animal soul of the old Germans is nothing but poisoning . <end>
<start> An enormous chemical fermentation and decomposition , a medley of sentiments and judgments , a rank growth of adventurous legend , and hence in the long run a fundamental weakening of such barbarian peoples , was the inevitable result . <end>
<start> True , without this weakening what should we have left of Greek culture , of the whole cultured past of the human race ? <end>
<start> For the barbarians untouched by Christianity knew very well how to make a clean sweep of old cultures , as was only too clearly shown by the heathen conquerors of romanized Britain . <end>
<start> Thus Christianity , against its will , was compelled to aid in making the antique world immortal . <end>
<start> There remains , however , a counter question and the possibility of a counter reckoning . <end>
<start> Without this weakening through the poisoning referred to , would any of those fresh stocks the Germans , for instance have been in a position gradually to find by themselves a higher , a peculiar , a new culture , of which the most distant conception would therefore have been lost to humanity ? <end>
<start> In this , as in every case , we do not know , christian speaking , whether God owes the devil or the devil God more thanks for everything having turned out as it has . <end>
<start> Faith makes Holy and Condemns . <end>
<start> A Christian who happened upon forbidden paths of thought might well ask himself on some occasion whether it is really necessary that there should be a God , side by side with a representative Lamb , if faith in the existence of these beings suffices to produce the same influences ? <end>
<start> If they do exist after all , are they not superfluous beings ? <end>
<start> For all that is given by the Christian religion to the human soul , all that is beneficent , consoling , and edifying , just as much as all that depresses and crushes , emanates from that faith and not from the objects of that faith . <end>
<start> It is here as in another well known case there were indeed no witches , but the terrible effects of the belief in witches were the same as if they really had existed . <end>
<start> For all occasions where the Christian awaits the immediate intervention of a God , though in vain for there is no God , his religion is inventive enough to find subterfuges and reasons for tranquillity . <end>
<start> In so far Christianity is an ingenious religion . <end>
<start> Faith , indeed , has up to the present not been able to move real mountains , although I do not know who assumed that it could . <end>
<start> But it can put mountains where there are none . <end>
<start> The tragic Comedy of Regensburg . <end>
<start> Here and there we see with terrible clearness the of Fortune , how she fastens the rope , on which she wills that succeeding centuries should dance , on to a few days , one place , the condition and opinions of one brain . <end>
<start> Thus the fate of modern German history lies in the days of that disputation at Regensburg : the peaceful settlement of ecclesiastical and moral affairs , without religious wars or a counter reformation , and also the unity of the German nation , seemed assured : the deep , gentle spirit of contains hovered for one moment over the theological squabble , victorious , as representative of the riper Italian piety , reflecting the morning glory of intellectual freedom . <end>
<start> But Luther s hard head , full of suspicions and strange misgivings , showed resistance . <end>
<start> Because justification by grace appeared to him his greatest motto and discovery , he did not believe the phrase in the mouth of Italians whereas , in point of fact , as is well known , they had invented it much earlier and spread it throughout Italy in deep silence . <end>
<start> In this apparent agreement Luther saw the tricks of the devil , and hindered the work of peace as well as he could , thereby advancing to a great extent the aims of the Empire s foes . <end>
<start> And now , in order to have a still stronger idea of the dreadful farcically of it all , let us add that none of the principles about which men then disputed in Regensburg neither that of original sin , nor that of redemption by proxy , nor that of justification by faith is in any way true or even has any connection with truth : that they are now all recognised as incapable of being discussed . <end>
<start> Yet on this account the world was set on fire that is to say , by opinions which correspond to no things or realities whereas as regards purely philological questions as , for instance , that of the sacramental words in the Eucharist discussion at any rate is permitted , because in this case the truth can be said . <end>
<start> But where nothing is , even truth has lost her right . <end>
<start> Lastly , it only remains to be said that it is true these principles give rise to sources of power so mighty that without them all the mills of the modern world could not be driven with such force . <end>
<start> And it is primarily a matter of force , only secondarily of truth and perhaps not even secondarily is it not so , my dear up to date friends ? <end>
<start> Goethe s Errors . <end>
<start> Goethe is a signal exception among great artists in that he did not live within the limited confines of his real capacity , as if that must be the essential , the distinctive , the unconditional , and the last thing in him and for all the world . <end>
<start> Twice he intended to possess something higher than he really possessed and went astray in the second half of his life , where he seems quite convinced that he is one of the great scientific discoverers and illuminators . <end>
<start> So too in the first half of his life he demanded of himself something higher than the poetic art seemed to him and here already he made a mistake . <end>
<start> That nature wished to make him a plastic artist , this was his inwardly glowing and scorching secret , which finally drove him to Italy , that he might give vent to his mania in this direction and make to it every possible sacrifice . <end>
<start> At last , shrewd as he was , and honestly averse to Allusion to German proverb : Where there is nothing , the Emperor has lost his rights . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> any mental perversion in himself , he discovered that a tricksy elf of desire had attracted him to the belief in this calling , and that he must free himself of the greatest passion of his heart and bid it farewell . <end>
<start> The painful conviction , tearing and gnawing at his vitals , that it was necessary to bid farewell , finds full expression in the character of Tasso . <end>
<start> Over Tasso , that weather intensified , hovers the premonition of something worse than death , as when one says : Now it is over , after this farewell : how shall I go on living without going mad ? <end>
<start> These two fundamental errors of his life gave Goethe , in face of a purely literary attitude towards poetry the only attitude then known to the world , such an unembarrassed and apparently almost arbitrary position . <end>
<start> Not to speak of the period when Schiller poor Schiller , who had no time himself and left no time to others drove away his shy dread of poetry , his fear of all literary life and craftsmanship , Goethe appears like a Greek who now and then visits his beloved , doubting whether she be not a Goddess to whom he can give no proper name . <end>
<start> In all his poetry one notices the inspiring neighbourhood of plastic art and Nature . <end>
<start> The features of these figures that floated before him and perhaps he always thought he was on the track of the metamorphoses of one Goddess became , without his will or knowledge , the features of all the children of his art . <end>
<start> Without the extravagances of error he would not have been Goethe that is , the only German artist in writing who has not yet become out of date just because he desired as little to be a writer as a German by vocation . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> Travellers and their Grades . <end>
<start> Among travellers we may distinguish five grades . <end>
<start> The first and lowest grade is of those who travel and are seen they become really travelled and are , as it were , blind . <end>
<start> Next come those who really see the world . <end>
<start> The third class experience the results of their seeing . <end>
<start> The fourth weave their experience into their life and carry it with them henceforth . <end>
<start> Lastly , there are some men of the highest strength who , as soon as they have returned home , must finally and necessarily work out in their lives and productions all the things seen that they have experienced and incorporated in themselves . <end>
<start> Like these five species of travellers , all mankind goes through the whole pilgrimage of life , the lowest as purely passive , the highest as those who act and live out their lives without keeping back any residue of inner experiences . <end>
<start> In Climbing Higher . <end>
<start> So soon as we climb higher than those who hitherto admired us , we appear to them as sunken and fallen . <end>
<start> For they imagined that under all circumstances they were on the heights in our company maybe also through our agency . <end>
<start> Measure and Moderation . <end>
<start> Of lofty things , measure and moderation , it is best never to speak . <end>
<start> A few know their force and significance , from the mysterious paths of inner experiences and conversions : they honour in them something quite godlike , and are afraid to speak aloud . <end>
<start> All the rest hardly listen when they are spoken about , and think the subjects under discussion are tedium and mediocrity . <end>
<start> We must perhaps except those who have once heard a warning note from that realm but have stopped their ears against the sound . <end>
<start> The recollection of it makes them angry and exasperated . <end>
<start> Humanity of Friendship and Comradeship . <end>
<start> If thou wilt take the left hand , then I will go to the right , that feeling is the hall mark of humanity in intimate intercourse , and without that feeling every friendship , every band of apostles or disciples , sooner or later becomes a fraud . <end>
<start> The Profound . <end>
<start> Men of profound thought appear to themselves in intercourse with others like comedians , for in order to be understood they must always simulate superficiality . <end>
<start> For the corners of Herd Humanity . <end>
<start> He who regards human beings as a herd , and flies from them as fast as he can , will certainly be caught up by them and gored upon their horns . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> The Main Transgression against the VAIN . <end>
<start> In society , he who gives another an opportunity of favourably setting forth his knowledge , sentiments , and experience sets himself above him . <end>
<start> Unless he is felt by the other to be a superior being without limitation , he is guilty of an attack upon his vanity , while what he aimed at was the gratification of the other man s vanity . <end>
<start> Disappointment . <end>
<start> When a long life of action distinguished by speeches and writings gives publicity to a man s personality , personal intercourse with him is generally disappointing on two grounds . <end>
<start> Firstly , one expects too much from a brief period of intercourse namely , all that the thousand and one opportunities of life can alone bring out . <end>
<start> Secondly , no recognised person gives himself the trouble to woo recognition in individual cases . <end>
<start> He is too careless , and we are at too high a tension . <end>
<start> Two Sources of Kindness . <end>
<start> To treat all men with equal good humour , and to be kind without distinction of persons , may arise as much from a profound contempt for mankind as from an ingrained love of humanity . <end>
<start> The Wanderer in the Mountains to Himself . <end>
<start> There are certain signs that you have gone farther and higher . <end>
<start> There is a freer , wider prospect before you , the air blows cooler yet milder in your face you have unlearned the folly of confounding mildness with warmth , your gait is more firm and vigorous , courage and discretion have waxed together . <end>
<start> On all these grounds your journey may now be more lonely and in any case more perilous than heretofore , if indeed not to the extent believed by those who from the misty valley see you , the rohmer , striding on the mountains . <end>
<start> With the Exception of Our Neighbour . <end>
<start> I admit that my head is set wrong on my neck only , for every other man , as is well known , knows better than I what I should do or leave alone . <end>
<start> The only one who cannon help me is myself , poor beggar ! <end>
<start> Are we not all like statues on which false heads have been placed ? <end>
<start> Eh , dear neighbour ? <end>
<start> Ah no you , just you , are the exception ! <end>
<start> Caution . <end>
<start> We must either not go about at all with people who are lacking in the reverence for personalities , or inexorably fetter them beforehand with the manacles of convention . <end>
<start> The Wish to Appear Vain . <end>
<start> In conversation with strangers or little known acquaintances , to express only selected thoughts , to speak of one s famous acquaintances , and important experiences and travels , is a sign that one is not proud , or at least would not like to appear proud . <end>
<start> Vanity is the polite mask of pride . <end>
<start> Good Friendship . <end>
<start> A good friendship arises when the one man deeply respects the other , more even than himself loves him also , though not so much as himself and finally , to facilitate intercourse , knows how to add the delicate bloom and veneer of intimacy , but at the same time wisely refrains from a true , real intimacy , from the confounding of mum and tum . <end>
<start> Friends as Ghosts . <end>
<start> If we change ourselves vitally , our friends , who have not changed , become ghosts of our own past : their voice sounds shadowy and dreadful to us , as if we heard our own voice speaking , but younger , harder , less mellow . <end>
<start> One Eye andTwo Glances . <end>
<start> The same people whose eyes naturally plead for favours and indulgences are accustomed , from their frequent humiliations and cravings for revenge , to assume a shameless glance as well . <end>
<start> The Haze of Distance . <end>
<start> A child throughout life that sounds very touching , but is only the verdict from the distance . <end>
<start> Seen and known close at hand , he is always called puerile throughout life . <end>
<start> Advantage and Disadvantage in the Same Misunderstanding . <end>
<start> The mute perplexity of the subtle brain is usually understood by the unsubtle as a silent superiority , and is much dreaded whereas the perception of perplexity would produce good will . <end>
<start> The Sage giving Himself out to be a Fool . <end>
<start> The philanthropy of the sage sometimes makes him decide to pretend to be excited , enraged , or delighted , so that he may not hurt his surroundings by the coldness and rationality of his true nature . <end>
<start> Forcing Oneself to Attention . <end>
<start> So soon as we note that any one in intercourse and conversation with us has to force himself to attention , we have adequate evidence that he loves us not , or loves us no longer . <end>
<start> The Way to a Christian Virtue . <end>
<start> Learning from one s enemies is the best way to love them , for it inspires us with a grateful mood towards them . <end>
<start> Stratagem of the Importunate . <end>
<start> The importunate man gives us gold coins as change for our convention coins , and thereby tries to force us afterwards to treat our convention as an oversight and him as an exception . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> Reason for Dislike . <end>
<start> We become hostile to many an artist or writer , not because we notice in the end that he has duped us , but because he did not find more subtle means necessary to entrap us . <end>
<start> IN PARTING . <end>
<start> Not by the way one soul approaches another , but by the way it separates , do I recognise its relationship and homogeneity with the other . <end>
<start> selenium . <end>
<start> We must not speak about our friends , or we renounce the sentiment of friendship . <end>
<start> politeness . <end>
<start> politeness is often the sign of a clumsy modesty , which when taken by surprise loses its head and would fain hide the fact by means of rudeness . <end>
<start> Honesty s Miscalculation . <end>
<start> Our newest acquaintances are sometimes the first to learn what we have hitherto kept dark . <end>
<start> We have the foolish notion that our proof of confidence is the strongest fetter herewith to hold them fast . <end>
<start> But they do not know enough about us to feel so strongly the sacrifice involved in our speaking out , and betray our secrets to others without any idea of betrayal . <end>
<start> Hereby we possibly lose our old friends . <end>
<start> In the Ante Chamber of Favour . <end>
<start> All men whom we let stand long in the ante chamber of our favour get into a state of fermentation or become bitter . <end>
<start> Warning to the Despised . <end>
<start> When we have sunk unmistakably in the estimation of mankind we should cling tooth and nail to modesty in intercourse , or we shall betray to others that we have sunk in our own estimation as well . <end>
<start> Cynicism in intercourse is a sign that a man , when alone , treats himself too as a dog . <end>
<start> Ignorance often ennobled . <end>
<start> With regard to the respect of those who pay respect , it is an advantage ostensibly not to understand certain things . <end>
<start> Ignorance , too , confers privileges . <end>
<start> The Opponent of Grace . <end>
<start> The impatient and arrogant man does not care for grace , feeling it to be a corporeal , visible reproach against himself . <end>
<start> For grace is heartfelt toleration in movement and gesture . <end>
<start> On Seeing Again . <end>
<start> When old friends see each other again after a long separation , it often happens that they affect an interest in matters to which they have long since become indifferent . <end>
<start> Sometimes both remark this , but dare not raise the veil from a mournful doubt . <end>
<start> Hence arise conversations as in the realm of the dead . <end>
<start> Making Friends only with the Industrious . <end>
<start> The man of leisure is dangerous to his friends , for , having nothing to do , he talks of what his friends are doing or not doing , interferes , and finally makes himself a nuisance . <end>
<start> The clever man will only make friends with the industrious . <end>
<start> One Weapon twice as much as Two . <end>
<start> It is an unequal combat when one man defends his cause with head and heart , the other with head alone . <end>
<start> The first has sun and wind against him , as it were , and his two weapons interfere with each other : he loses the prize in the eyes of truth . <end>
<start> True , the victory of the second , with his one weapon , is seldom a victory after the hearts of all the other spectators , and makes him unpopular . <end>
<start> Depth and Troubled Waters . <end>
<start> The public easily confounds him who fishes in troubled waters with him who pumps up from the depths . <end>
<start> Demonstrating One s Vanity to Friend AND Foe . <end>
<start> Many a man , from vanity , maltreated even his friends , when in the presence of witnesses to whom he wishes to make his own preponderance clear . <end>
<start> Others exaggerate the merits of their enemies , in order to point proudly to the fact that they are worthy of such foes . <end>
<start> Cooling Off . <end>
<start> The over heating of the heart is generally allied with illness of the head and judgment . <end>
<start> He who is concerned for a time with the health of his head must know what he has to cool , careless of the future of his heart . <end>
<start> For if we are capable at all of giving warmth , we are sure to become warm again and then have our summer . <end>
<start> Mingled Feelings . <end>
<start> Towards science women and self seeking artists entertain a feeling that is composed of envy and sentimentality . <end>
<start> Where Danger is Greatest . <end>
<start> We seldom break our leg so long as life continues a noisome upward climb . <end>
<start> The danger comes when we begin to take things easily and choose the convenient paths . <end>
<start> Not TOO Early . <end>
<start> We must beware of becoming sharp too early , or we shall also become thin too early . <end>
<start> Joy in Refractoriness . <end>
<start> The good teacher knows cases where he is proud that his pupil re mains true to himself in opposition to him at times when the youth must not understand the man or would be harmed by understanding him . <end>
<start> The Experiment of Honesty . <end>
<start> Young men , who wish to be more honest than they have been , seek as victim some one acknowledged to be honest , attacking him first with an attempt to reach his height by abuse with the underlying notion that this first experiment at any rate is void of danger . <end>
<start> For just such a one has no right to chastise the impudence of the honest man . <end>
<start> The Eternal Child . <end>
<start> We think , short sighted that we are , that fairy tales and games belong to childhood . <end>
<start> As if at any age we should care to live without fairy tales and games ! <end>
<start> Our words and sentiments are indeed different , but the essential fact remains the same , as is proved by the child himself looking on games as his work and fairytales as his truth . <end>
<start> The shortness of life ought to preserve us from a pedantic distinction between the different ages as if every age brought something new and a poet ought one day to portray a man of two hundred , who really lives without fairy tales and games . <end>
<start> Every Philosophy is the Philosophy of a Period of Life . <end>
<start> The period of life in which a philosopher finds his teaching is manifested by his teaching he cannon avoid that , however elevated above time and hour he may feel himself . <end>
<start> Thus , Schopenhauer s philosophy remains a mirror of his hot and melancholy youth it is no mode of thought for older men . <end>
<start> Plato s philosophy reminds one of the middle thirties , when a warm and a cold current generally rush together , so that spray and delicate clouds and , under favourable circumstances and glimpses of sunshine , enchanting rainbow pictures result . <end>
<start> Of the Intellect of Women . <end>
<start> The intellectual strength of a woman is best proved by the fact that she offers her own intellect as a sacrifice out of love for a man and his intellect , and that nevertheless in the new domain , which was previously foreign to her nature , a second intellect at once arises as an , to which the man s mind impels her . <end>
<start> Raising and Lowering in the Sexual DOMAIN . <end>
<start> The storm of desire will sometimes carry a man up to a height where all desire is silenced , where he really loves and lives in a better state of being rather than in a better state of choice . <end>
<start> On the other hand , a good woman , from true love , often climbs down to desire , and lowers herself in her own eyes . <end>
<start> The latter action in particular is one of the most pathetic sensations which the idea of a good marriage can involve . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> Man Promises , Woman Fulfils . <end>
<start> By woman Nature shows how far she has hitherto achieved her task of fashioning humanity , by man she shows what she has had to overcome and what she still proposes to do for humanity . <end>
<start> The most perfect woman of every age is the holiday task of the Creator on every seventh day of culture , the recreation of the artist from his work . <end>
<start> Transplanting . <end>
<start> If we have spent our intellect in order to gain mastery over the intemperance of the passions , the sad result often follows that we transfer the intemperance to the intellect , and from that time forth are extravagant in thought and desire of knowledge . <end>
<start> Laughter as Treachery . <end>
<start> How and when a woman laughs is a sign of her culture , but in the ring of laughter her nature reveals itself , and in highly cultured women perhaps even the last insoluble residue of their nature . <end>
<start> Hence the psychologist will say with Horace , though from different reasons : ride cuellar . <end>
<start> From the Youthful Soul . <end>
<start> Youths varying show devotion and impudence towards the same person , because at bottom they only despise or admire themselves in that other person , and between the two feelings but stagger to and fro in themselves , so long as they have not found in experience the measure of their will and ability . <end>
<start> For the Amelioration of the World . <end>
<start> If we forbade the discontented , the sullen , and the to propagate , we might transform the world into a garden of happiness . <end>
<start> This aphorism belongs to a practical philosophy for the female sex . <end>
<start> Not to Distrust your Emotions . <end>
<start> The feminine phrase Do not distrust your emotions does not mean much more than Eat what tastes good to you . <end>
<start> This may also , especially for moderate natures , be a good everyday rule . <end>
<start> But other natures must live according to another maxim : You must eat not only with your mouth but also with your brain , in order that the greediness of your mouth may not prove your undoing . <end>
<start> A Cruel Fancy of Love . <end>
<start> Every great love involves the cruel thought of killing the object of love , so that it may be removed once for all from the mischievous play of change . <end>
<start> For love is more afraid of change than of destruction . <end>
<start> DOORS . <end>
<start> In everything that is learnt or experienced , the child , just like the man , sees doors but for the former they are places to go to , for the latter to go through . <end>
<start> Sympathetic Women . <end>
<start> The sympathy of women , which is talkative , takes the sick bed to market . <end>
<start> Early Merit . <end>
<start> He who acquires merit early in life tends to forget all reverence for age and old people , and accordingly , greatly to his disadvantage , excludes himself from the society of the mature , those who confer maturity . <end>
<start> Thus in spite of his early merit he remains green , importunate , and boyish longer than others . <end>
<start> Souls All of a Piece . <end>
<start> Women and artists think that where we do not contradict them we cannon . <end>
<start> Reverence on ten counts and silent disapproval on ten others appears to them an impossible combination , because their souls are all of a piece . <end>
<start> YOUNG Talents . <end>
<start> With respect to young talents we must strictly follow Goethe s maxim , that we should often avoid harming error in order to avoid harming truth . <end>
<start> Their condition is like the diseases of pregnancy , and involves strange appetites . <end>
<start> These appetites should be satisfied and humoured as far as possible , for the sake of the fruit they may be expected to produce . <end>
<start> It is true that , as nurse of these remarkable invalids , one must learn the difficult art of voluntary self abasement . <end>
<start> Disgust with Truth . <end>
<start> Women are so constituted that all truth in relation to men , love , children , society , aim of life disgusts them and that they try to be revenged on every one who opens their eyes . <end>
<start> The Source of Great Love . <end>
<start> Whence arises the sudden passion of a man for a woman , a passion so deep , so vital ? <end>
<start> Least of all from sensuality only : but when a man finds weakness , need of help , and high spirits united in the same creature , he suffers a sort of overflowing of soul , and is touched and offended at the same moment . <end>
<start> At this point arises the source of great love . <end>
<start> Cleanliness . <end>
<start> In the child , the sense for cleanliness should be fanned into a passion , and then later on he will raise himself , in ever new phases , to almost every virtue , and will finally appear , in compensation for all talent , as a shining cloud of purity , temperance , gentleness , and character , happy in himself and spreading happiness around . <end>
<start> Of Vain Old Men . <end>
<start> Profundity of thought belongs to youth , clarity of thought to old age . <end>
<start> When , in spite of this , old men sometimes speak and write in the manner of the profound , they do so from vanity , imagining that they thereby assume the charm of juvenilia , enthusiasm , growth , , hopefulness . <end>
<start> Enjoyment of Novelty . <end>
<start> Men use a new lesson or experience later on as a ploughshare or perhaps also as a weapon , women at once make it into an ornament . <end>
<start> How both Sexes behave when in the Right . <end>
<start> If it is conceded to a woman that she is right , she cannon deny herself the triumph of setting her heel on the neck of the vanquished she must taste her victory to the full . <end>
<start> On the other hand , man towards man in such a case is ashamed of being right . <end>
<start> But then man is accustomed to victory with woman it is an exception . <end>
<start> Abnegation in the Will to Beauty . <end>
<start> In order to become beautiful , a woman must not desire to be considered pretty . <end>
<start> That is to say , in ninety-nine out of a hundred cases where she could please she must scorn and put aside all thoughts of pleasing . <end>
<start> Only then can she ever reap the delight of him whose soul s portal is wide enough to admit the great . <end>
<start> Unintelligible , Unendurable . <end>
<start> A youth cannon understand that an old man has also had his delights , his dawns of feeling , his changing and soaring of thought . <end>
<start> It offends him to think that such things have existed before . <end>
<start> But it makes him very bitter to hear that , to become fruitful , he must lose those buds and dispense with their fragrance . <end>
<start> The Party with the Air of Martyrdom . <end>
<start> Every party that can assume an air of martyrdom wins good natured souls over to its side and thereby itself acquires an air of good nature greatly to its advantage . <end>
<start> Assertions surer than Arguments . <end>
<start> An assertion has , with the majority of men at any rate , more effect than an argument , for arguments provoke mistrust . <end>
<start> Hence demagogues seek to strengthen the arguments of their party by assertions . <end>
<start> The Best concealer . <end>
<start> All regularly successful men are profoundly cunning in making their faults and weaknesses look like manifestations of strength . <end>
<start> This proves that they must know their defects uncommonly well . <end>
<start> From Time to Time . <end>
<start> He sat in the city gateway and said to one who passed through that this was the city gate . <end>
<start> The latter replied that this was true , but that one must not be too much in the right if one expected to be thanked for it . <end>
<start> Oh , answered the other , I don t want thanks , but from time to time it is very pleasant not merely to be in the right but to remain in the right . <end>
<start> Virtue was not Invented by the Germans . <end>
<start> Goethe s noblenet and freedom from envy , Beethoven s fine heretical resignation , Mozart s cheerfulness and grace of heart , Handel s unbending manliness and freedom under the law , Bach s confident and luminous inner life , such as does not even need to renounce glamour and success are these qualities peculiarly German ? <end>
<start> If they are not , they at least prove to what goal Germans should strive and to what they can attain . <end>
<start> Pia fraud or Something Else . <end>
<start> I hope I am mistaken , but I think that in Germany of to day a V twofold sort of hypocrisy is set up as the duty of the moment for every one . <end>
<start> From imperial political misgivings germanium is demanded , and from social apprehensions Christianity but both only in words and gestures , and particularly in ability to keep silent . <end>
<start> It is the veneer that nowadays costs so much and is paid for so highly and for the benefit of the spectators the face of the nation assumes German and Christian wrinkles . <end>
<start> HOW FAR EVEN IN THE GOOD THE HALF aware constructed to last and demand the service of many hands , much that is less good must be made a rule , although the organiser knows what is better and harder very well . <end>
<start> He will calculate that there will never be a lack of persons who can correspond to the rule , and he knows that the middling good is the rule . <end>
<start> The youth seldom sees this point , and as an innovator thinks how marvellously he is in the right and how strange is the blindness of others . <end>
<start> The PARTISAN . <end>
<start> The true partisan learns nothing more , he only experiences and judges . <end>
<start> It is significant that Solon , who was never a partisan but pursued his aims above and apart from parties or even against them , was the father of that simple phrase wherein lies the secret of the health and vitality of Athens : I grow old , but I am always learning . <end>
<start> What is German according to Goethe . <end>
<start> They are really intolerable people of whom one cannon even accept the good , who have freedom of disposition but do not remark that they are lacking in freedom of taste and spirit . <end>
<start> Yet just this , according to Goethe s well weighed judgment , is German . <end>
<start> His voice and his example indicate that the German should be more than a German if he wishes to be useful or even endurable to other nations and which direction his striving should take , in order that he may rise above and beyond himself . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> When it is Necessary to Remain Stationary . <end>
<start> When the masses begin to rage , and reason is under a cloud , it is a good thing , if the health of one s soul is not quite assured , to go under a doorway and look out to see what the weather is like . <end>
<start> The Revolution Spirit and the Possession Spirit . <end>
<start> The only remedy against Socialism that still lies in your power is to avoid provoking Socialism in other words , to live in moderation and contentment , to prevent as far as possible all lavish display , and to aid the State as far as possible in its taxing of all and luxuries . <end>
<start> You do not like this remedy ? <end>
<start> Then , you rich bourgeois who call yourselves Liberals , confess that it is your own inclination that you find so terrible and menacing in Socialists , but allow to prevail in yourselves as unavoidable , as if with you it were something different . <end>
<start> As you are constituted , if you had not your fortune and the cares of maintaining it , this bent of yours would make Socialists of you . <end>
<start> Possession alone differentiates you from them . <end>
<start> If you wish to conquer the assailants of your prosperity , you must first conquer yourselves . <end>
<start> And if that prosperity only meant well being , it would not be so external and provocative of envy it would be more generous , more benevolent , more compensatory , more helpful . <end>
<start> But the spurious , histrionic element in your pleasures , which lie more in the feeling of contrast because others have them not , and feel envious VOL . <end>
<start> K than in feelings of realised and heightened power your houses , dresses , carriages , shops , the demands of your palates and your tables , your noisy operatic and musical enthusiasm lastly your women , formed and fashioned but of base metal , gilded but without the ring of gold , chosen by you for show and considering themselves meant for show these are the things that spread the poison of that national disease , which seizes the masses ever more and more as a Socialistic heart itch , but has its origin and breeding place in you . <end>
<start> Who shall now arrest this epidemic ? <end>
<start> PARTY Tactics . <end>
<start> When a party observes that a previous member has changed from an unqualified to a qualified adherent , it endures it so ill that it irritates and mortified him in every possible way with the object of forcing him to a decisive break and making him an opponent . <end>
<start> For the party suspects that the intention of finding a relative value in its faith , a value which admits of pro and con , of weighing and discarding , is more dangerous than downright opposition . <end>
<start> For the Strengthening of Parties . <end>
<start> Whoever wishes to strengthen a party internally should give it an opportunity of being forcibly treated with obvious injustice . <end>
<start> The party thus acquires a capital of good conscience , which hitherto it perhaps lacked . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> To Provide for One s Past . <end>
<start> As men after all only respect the old established and , he who would survive after his death must not only provide for posterity but still more for the past . <end>
<start> Hence tyrants of every sort including tyrannical artists and politicians like to do violence to history , so that history may seem a preparation and a ladder up to them . <end>
<start> Party Writers . <end>
<start> The beating of drums , which delights young writers who serve a party , sounds to him who does not belong to the party like a rattling of chains , and excites sympathy rather than admiration . <end>
<start> Taking Sides against Ourselves . <end>
<start> Our followers never forgive us for taking sides against ourselves , for we seem in their eyes not only to be spurning their love but to be exposing them to the charge of lack of intelligence . <end>
<start> Danger in Wealth . <end>
<start> Only a man of intellect should hold property : otherwise property is dangerous to the community . <end>
<start> For the owner , not knowing how to make use of the leisure which his possessions might secure to him , will continue to strive after more property . <end>
<start> This strife will be his occupation , his strategy in the war with ennui . <end>
<start> So in the end real wealth is produced from the moderate property that would be enough for an intellectual man . <end>
<start> Such wealth , then , is the glittering outcrop of intellectual dependence and poverty , but it looks quite different from what its humble origin might lead one to expect , because it can mask itself with culture and art it can , in fact , purchase the mask . <end>
<start> Hence it excites envy in the poor and uncultured who at bottom always envy culture and see no mask in the mask and gradually paves the way for a social revolution . <end>
<start> For a gilded coarseness and a histrionic blowing of trumpets in the pretended enjoyment of culture inspires that class with the thought , It is only a matter of money , whereas it is indeed to some extent a matter of money , but far more of intellect . <end>
<start> Joy in Commanding and Obeying . <end>
<start> Commanding is a joy , like obeying the former when it has not yet become a habit , the latter just when it has become a habit . <end>
<start> Old servants under new masters advance each other mutually in giving pleasure . <end>
<start> Ambition for a Forlorn Hope . <end>
<start> There is an ambition for a forlorn hope which forces a party to place itself at the post of extreme danger . <end>
<start> When Asses are Needed . <end>
<start> We shall not move the crowd to cry Hosanna ! <end>
<start> until we have ridden into the city upon an ass . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> Party Usage . <end>
<start> Every party attempts to represent the important elements that have sprung up outside it as unimportant , and if it does not succeed , it attacks those elements the more bitterly , the more excellent they are . <end>
<start> Becoming Empty . <end>
<start> Of him who abandons himself to the course of events , a smaller and smaller residue is continually left , Great politicians may therefore become quite empty men , although they were once full and rich . <end>
<start> Welcome Enemies . <end>
<start> The Socialistic movements are nowadays becoming more and more agreeable rather than terrifying to the dynastic governments , because by these movements they are provided with a right and a weapon for making exceptional rules , and can thus attack their real bogies , democrats and anti dynasts . <end>
<start> Towards all that such governments professed detest they feel a secret cordiality and inclination . <end>
<start> But they are compelled to draw the veil over their soul . <end>
<start> Possession Possesses . <end>
<start> Only up to a certain point does possession make men feel freer and more independent one step farther , and possession becomes lord , the possessor a slave . <end>
<start> The latter must sacrifice his time , his thoughts to the former , and feels himself compelled to an intercourse , nailed to a spot , incorporated with the State perhaps quite in conflict with his real and essential needs . <end>
<start> Of the Mastery of Them that Know . <end>
<start> It is easy , ridiculously easy , to set up a model for the choice of a legislative body . <end>
<start> First of all the honest and reliable men of the nation , who at the same time are masters and experts in some one branch , have to become prominent by mutual scenting out and recognition . <end>
<start> From these , by a narrower process of selection , the learned and expert of the first rank in each individual branch must again be chosen , also by mutual recognition and guarantee . <end>
<start> If the legislative body be composed of these , it will finally be necessary , in each individual case , that only the voices and judgments of the most specialised experts should decide the honesty of all the rest should have become so great that it is simply a matter of decency to leave the voting also in the hands of these men . <end>
<start> The result would be that the law , in the strictest sense , would emanate from the intelligence of the most intelligent . <end>
<start> As things now are , voting is done by parties , and at every division there must be hundreds of uneasy consciences among the ill taught , the incapable of judgment , among those who merely repeat , imitate , and go with the tide . <end>
<start> Nothing lowers the dignity of a new law so much as this inherent shamefaced feeling of insincerity that necessarily results at every party division . <end>
<start> But , as has been said , it is easy , ridiculously easy , to set up such a model : no power on earth is at present strong enough to realise such an ideal unless the belief in the highest utility of knowledge , and of those that know , at last dawns even upon the most hostile minds and is preferred to the prevalent belief in majorities . <end>
<start> In the sense of such a future may our watchword be : More reverence for them that know , and down with all parties ! <end>
<start> Of the Nation of Thinkers or of Bad Thinking . <end>
<start> The vague , vacillating , premonitory , elementary , intuitive elements to choose obscure names for obscure things that are attributed to the German nature would be , if they really still existed , a proof that our culture has remained several stages behind and is still surrounded by the spell and atmosphere of the Middle Ages . <end>
<start> It is true that in this backwardness there are certain advantages : by these qualities the Germans if , as has been said before , they still possess them would possess the capacity , which other nations have now lost , for doing certain things and particularly for understanding certain things . <end>
<start> Much undoubtedly is lost if the lack of sense which is just the common factor in all those qualities is lost . <end>
<start> Here too , however , there are no losses without the highest compensatory gains , so that no reason is left for lamenting , granting that we do not , like children , and , wish to enjoy at once the fruits of all seasons of the year . <end>
<start> Carrying Coals to Newcastle . <end>
<start> The governments of the great States have two instruments for keeping the people dependent , in fear and obedience : a coarser , the army , and a more refined , the school . <end>
<start> With the aid of the former they win over to their side the ambition of the higher strata and the strength of the lower , so far as both are characteristic of active and energetic men of moderate or inferior gifts . <end>
<start> With the aid of the latter they win over gifted poverty , especially the intellectually pretentious semi poverty of the middle classes . <end>
<start> Above all , they make teachers of all grades into an intellectual court looking unconsciously towards the heights . <end>
<start> By putting obstacle after obstacle in the way of private schools and the wholly distasteful individual tuition they secure the disposal of a considerable number of educational posts , towards which numerous hungry and submissive eyes are turned to an extent five times as great as can ever be satisfied . <end>
<start> These posts , however , must support the holder but meagrely , so that he maintains a feverish thirst for promotion and becomes still more closely attached to the views of the government . <end>
<start> For it is always more advantageous to foster moderate discontent than contentment , the mother of courage , the grandmother of free thought and exuberance . <end>
<start> By means of this physically and mentally bridled body of teachers , the youth of the country is as far as possible raised to a certain level of culture that is useful to the State and arranged on a suitable sliding scale . <end>
<start> Above all , the immature and ambitious minds of all classes are almost imperceptibly imbued with the idea that only a career which is recognised and hall marked by the State can lead immediately to social distinction . <end>
<start> The effect of this belief in government examinations and titles goes so far that even men who have remained independent and have risen by trade or handicraft still feel a pang of discontent in their hearts until their position too is marked and acknowledged by a gracious bestowal of rank and orders from above until one becomes a somebody . <end>
<start> Finally the State connects all these hundreds of offices and posts in its hands with the obligation of being trained and hallmark in these State schools if one ever wishes to enter this charmed circle . <end>
<start> Honour in society , daily bread , the possibility of a family , protection from above , the feeling of community in a common culture all this forms a network of hopes into which every young man walks : how should he feel the slightest breath of mistrust ? <end>
<start> In the end , perhaps , the obligation of being a soldier for one year has become with every one , after the lapse of a few generations , an reflecting habit , an understood thing , with an eye to which we construct the plan of our lives quite early . <end>
<start> Then the State can venture on the master stroke of weaving together school and army , talent , ambition and strength by means of common advantages that is , by attracting the more highly gifted on favourable terms to the army and inspiring them with the military spirit of joyful obedience so that finally , perhaps , they become attached permanently to the flag and endow it by their talents with an ever new and more brilliantHUMAN , ALL TOO HUMAN . <end>
<start> lustre . <end>
<start> Then nothing more is wanted but an opportunity for great wars . <end>
<start> These are provided from professional reasons and so in all innocence by diplomats , aided by newspapers and Stock Exchanges . <end>
<start> For the nation , as a nation of soldiers , need never be supplied with a good conscience in war it has one already . <end>
<start> The Press . <end>
<start> If we consider how even to day all great political transactions glide upon the stage secretly and stealthily how they are hidden by unimportant events , and seem small when close at hand how they only show their far reaching effect , and leave the soil still quaking , long after they have taken place what significance can we attach to the Press in its present position , with its daily expenditure of lung power in order to bawl , to deafen , to excite , to terrify ? <end>
<start> Is it anything more than an everlasting false alarm , which tries to lead our ears and our wits into a false direction ? <end>
<start> After a Great Event . <end>
<start> A nation and a man whose soul has come to light through some great event generally feel the immediate need of some act of childishness or coarseness , as much from shame as for purposes of recreation . <end>
<start> To be a Good German means to de GerMANISE ONESELF . <end>
<start> National differences consist , far more than has hitherto been observed , only in the differences of various grades of culture , and are only to a very small extent permanent nor even that in a strict sense . <end>
<start> For this reason all arguments based on national character are so little binding on one who aims at the alteration of convictions in other words , at culture . <end>
<start> If , for instance , we consider all that has already been German , we shall improve upon the hypothetical question , What is German ? <end>
<start> by the counter question , What is now German ? <end>
<start> and every good German will answer it practically , by overcoming his German characteristics . <end>
<start> For when a nation advances and grows , it bursts the girdle previously given to it by its national outlook . <end>
<start> When it remains stationary or declines , its soul is surrounded by a fresh girdle , and the crust , as it becomes harder and harder , builds a prison around , with walls growing ever higher . <end>
<start> Hence if a nation has much that is firmly established , this is a sign that it wishes to verify and would like to become nothing but a monument . <end>
<start> This happened , from a definite date , in the case of Egypt . <end>
<start> So he who is well disposed towards the Germans may for his part consider how he may more and more grow out of what is German . <end>
<start> The tendency to be un German has therefore always been a mark of efficient members of our nation . <end>
<start> Foreignisms . <end>
<start> A foreigner who travelled in Germany found favour or the reverse by certain assertions of his , according to the districts in which he stayed . <end>
<start> All intelligent fabians , he used to say , are coquettish . <end>
<start> The other fabians still believed that upland was a poet and Goethe immoral . <end>
<start> The best about German novels now in vogue was that one need not read them , for one knew already what they contained . <end>
<start> The native of Berlin seemed more good humoured than the South German , for he was all too fond of mocking , and so could endure mockery himself , which the South German could not . <end>
<start> The intellect of the Germans was kept down by their beer and their newspapers : he recommended them tea and pamphlets , of course as a cure . <end>
<start> He advised us to contemplate the different nations of worn out Europe and see how well each displayed some particular quality of old age , to the delight of those who sit before the great spectacle : how the French successfully represent the cleverness and amiability of old age , the English the experience and reserve , the Italians the innocence and candour . <end>
<start> Can the other masks of old age be wanting ? <end>
<start> Where is the proud old man , the domineering old man , the covetous old man ? <end>
<start> The most dangerous region in Germany was Saxony and Thuringia : nowhere else was there more mental nimbleness , more knowledge of men , side by side with freedom of thought and all this was so modestly veiled by the ugly dialect and the zealous of the inhabitants that one hardly noticed that one here had to deal with the intellectual drill sergeants of Germany , her teachers for good or evil . <end>
<start> The arrogance of the North Germans was kept in check by their tendency to obey , that of the South Germans by their tendency to make themselves comfortable . <end>
<start> It appeared to him that in their women German men possessed awkward but self opinionated housewives , who bearded themselves so persevering that they had almost persuaded the world , and at any rate their husbands , of their peculiarly German housewifely virtue . <end>
<start> When the conversation turned on Germany s home and foreign policy , he used to say he called it betray the secret that Germany s greatest statesman did not believe in great statesmen . <end>
<start> The future of Germany he found menaced and menacing , for Germans had forgotten how to enjoy themselves an art that the Italians understood so well , but , by the great games of chance called wars and dynastic revolutions , had accustomed themselves to emotionalism , and consequently would one day have an mute . <end>
<start> For that is the strongest emotion that a nation can procure for itself . <end>
<start> The German Socialist was all the more dangerous because impelled by no definite necessity : his trouble lay in not knowing what he wanted so , even if he attained many of his objects , he would still pine away from desire in the midst of delights , just like Faust , but presumably like a very vulgar Faust . <end>
<start> For the FaustDevil , he finally exclaimed , by whom cultured Germans were so much plagued , was exorcised by Bismarck but now the Devil has entered into the swine and is worse than ever ! <end>
<start> Opinions . <end>
<start> Most men are nothing and count for nothing until they have arrayed themselves universal convictions and public opinions . <end>
<start> This is in accordance with the tailors philosophy , The apparel makes the man . <end>
<start> Of exceptional men , however , it must be said , The wearer primarily makes the apparel . <end>
<start> Here opinions cease to be public , and become something else than masks , ornament , and disguise . <end>
<start> Two Kinds of Sobriety . <end>
<start> In order not to confound the sobriety arising from mental exhaustion with that arising from moderation , one must remark that the former is peevish , the latter cheerful . <end>
<start> Debasement of Joy . <end>
<start> To call a thing good not a day longer than it appears to us good , and above all not a day earlier that is the only way to keep joy pure . <end>
<start> Otherwise , joy all too easily becomes insipid and rotten to the taste , and counts , for whole strata of the people , among the unadulterated foodstuffs . <end>
<start> The Scapegoat of Virtue . <end>
<start> When a man does his very best , those who mean well towards him , but are not capable of appreciating him , speedily seek a scapegoat to isolate , thinking it is the scapegoat of sin but it is the scapegoat of virtue . <end>
<start> Sovereignty . <end>
<start> o honour and acknowledge even the bad , when it pleases one , and to have no conception of how one could be ashamed of being pleased threat , is the mark of sovereignty in things great and small . <end>
<start> Influence a Phantom , not a Reality The man of mark gradually learns that so far as he has influence he is a phantom in other brains , and perhaps he falls into a state of subtle vexation of soul , in which he asks himself whether he must not maintain this phantom of himself for the benefit of his fellow men . <end>
<start> Giving and Taking . <end>
<start> When one takes away or anticipates the smallest thing that another possesses , the latter is blind to the fact that he has been given something greater , nay , even the greatest thing . <end>
<start> Good Ploughland . <end>
<start> All rejection and negation betoken a deficiency in fertility . <end>
<start> If we were good ploughland , we should allow nothing to be unused or lost , and in every thing , event , or person we should welcome manure , rain , or sunshine . <end>
<start> Intercourse as an Enjoyment . <end>
<start> If a man renounces the world and intentionally lives in solitude , he may come to regard intercourse with others , which he enjoys but seldom , as a special delicacy . <end>
<start> To Know how to Suffer in Public . <end>
<start> We must advertise our misfortunes and from time to time heave audible sighs and show visible marks of impatience . <end>
<start> For if we could let others see how assured and happy we are in spite of pain and privation , how envious and ill tempered they would become at the sight ! <end>
<start> But we must take care not to corrupt our fellow men besides , if they knew the truth , they would levy a heavy toll upon us . <end>
<start> At any rate our public misfortune is our private advantage . <end>
<start> Warmth on the Heights . <end>
<start> On the heights it is warmer than people in the valleys suppose , especially in winter . <end>
<start> The thinker recognises the full import of this simile . <end>
<start> To Will the Good and be Capable of the Beautiful . <end>
<start> It is not enough to practise the good one must have willed it , and , as the poet says , include the Godhead in our will . <end>
<start> But the beautiful we must not will , we must be capable of it , in innocence and blindness , without any psychical curiosity . <end>
<start> He that lights his lantern to find perfect men should remember the token by which to know them . <end>
<start> They are the men who always act for the sake of the good and in so doing always attain to the beautiful without thinking of the beautiful . <end>
<start> Many better and nobler men , from impotence or from want of beauty in their souls , remain une fleshing and ugly to behold , with all their good will and good works . <end>
<start> They rebuff and injure even virtue through the repulsive garb in which their bad taste arrays her . <end>
<start> Danger of Renunciation . <end>
<start> We must beware of basing our lives on too narrow a foundation of appetite . <end>
<start> For if we renounce all the joys involved in positions , honours , associations , revels , creature comforts , and arts , a day may come when we perceive that this repudiation has led us not to wisdom but to satiety of life . <end>
<start> Final Opinion on Opinions . <end>
<start> Either we should hide our opinions or hide ourselves behind our opinions . <end>
<start> Whoever does otherwise , does not know the way of the world , or belongs to the order of pious fire eaters . <end>
<start> Gaudeamus dimitur Joy must contain edifying and healing forces for the moral nature of man . <end>
<start> Otherwise , how comes it that our soul , as soon as it basks in the sunshine of joy , unconsciously vows to itself , I will be good ! <end>
<start> I will become perfect ! <end>
<start> and is at once seized by a premonition of perfection that is like a shudder of religious awe ? <end>
<start> To One who is Praised . <end>
<start> So long as you are praised , believe that you are not yet on your own course but on that of another . <end>
<start> Loving the Master . <end>
<start> The apprentice and the master love the master in different ways . <end>
<start> All too Beautiful and Human . <end>
<start> Nature is too beautiful for thee , poor mortal , one often feels . <end>
<start> But now and then , at a profound contemplation of all that is human , in its fullness , vigour , tenderness , and complexity , I have felt as if I must say , in all humility , Man also is too beautiful for the contemplation of man ! <end>
<start> Nor did I mean the moral man alone , but every one . <end>
<start> Real and Personal Estate . <end>
<start> When life has treated us in true robber fashion , and has taken away all that it could of honour , joys , connections , health , and property of every kind , we perhaps discover in the end , after the first shock , that we are richer than before . <end>
<start> For now we know for the first time what is so peculiarly ours that no robber hand can touch it , and perhaps , after all the plunder and devastation , we come forward with the airs of a mighty real estate owner . <end>
<start> Involuntarily Idealised . <end>
<start> The most for something higher than we really are . <end>
<start> forgot some element of fraud your speech , your expression , your bearing , your eye , your dealings and this deceitful something is as necessary as your usual honesty , but constantly destroys its effect and its value . <end>
<start> Idealist and Liar . <end>
<start> We must not let ourselves be tyrannies even by that finest faculty of realising things : otherwise , truth will one day part company from us with the insulting remark : Thou arch liar , what have I to do with thee ? <end>
<start> Being Misunderstood . <end>
<start> When one is misunderstood generally , it is impossible to remove a particular misunderstanding . <end>
<start> This point must be recognised , to save superfluous expenditure of energy in self defence . <end>
<start> The Water Drinker Speaks . <end>
<start> Go on drinking your wine , which has refreshed you all your life what affair is it of yours if I have to be a ? <end>
<start> Are not wine and water peaceable , brotherly elements , that can live side by side without mutual recriminations ? <end>
<start> From Cannibal Country . <end>
<start> In solitude the lonely man is eaten up by himself , among crowds by the many . <end>
<start> Choose which you prefer . <end>
<start> The Freezing Point of the Will . <end>
<start> Some time the hour will come at last , the hour that will envelop you in the golden cloud of pointlessness when the soul enjoys its own weariness and , happy in patient playing with patience , resembles the waves of a lake , which on a quiet summer day , in the reflection of a many hurd evening sky , sip and sip at the shore and again are hushed without end , without purpose , without satiety , without need all calm rejoicing in change , all ebb and flow of Nature s pulse . <end>
<start> Such is the feeling and talk of all invalids , but if they attain that hour , a brief period of enjoyment is followed by ennui . <end>
<start> But this is the of the frozen will , which awakes , stirs , and once more begets desire upon desire . <end>
<start> Desire is a sign of convalescence or recovery . <end>
<start> The Disclaimed Ideal . <end>
<start> It happens sometimes by an exception that a man only reaches the highest when he disclaims his ideal . <end>
<start> For this ideal previously drove him onward too violently , so that in the middle of the track he regularly got out of breath and had to rest . <end>
<start> Si . <end>
<start> A Treacherous Inclination . <end>
<start> It should be regarded as a sign of an envious but aspiring man , when he feels himself attracted by the thought that with regard to the eminent there is but one salvation love . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> Staircase Happiness . <end>
<start> Just as the wit of many men does not keep pace with opportunity so that opportunity has already passed through the door while wit still waits on the staircase outside , so others have a kind of staircase happiness , which walks too slowly to keep pace with swift footed Time . <end>
<start> The best that it can enjoy of an experience , of a whole span of life , falls to its share long afterwards , often only as a weak , spicy fragrance , giving rise to longing and sadness as if it might have been possible some time or other to drink one s fill of this element : but now it is too late . <end>
<start> Worms . <end>
<start> The fact that an intellect contains a few worms does not detract from its ripeness . <end>
<start> The Seat of Victory . <end>
<start> A good seat on horseback robs an opponent of his courage , the spectator of his heart why attack such a man ? <end>
<start> Sit like one who has been victorious ! <end>
<start> Danger in Admiration . <end>
