<start> From excessive admiration for the virtues of others one can lose the sense of one s own , and finally , through lack of practice , lose these virtues themselves , without retaining the alien virtues as compensation . <end>
<start> Uses of sickness . <end>
<start> He who is often ill not only has a far greater pleasure in health , on account of his so often getting well , but acquires a very keen sense of what is healthy or sickly in actions and achievements , both his own and others . <end>
<start> Thus , for example , it is just the writers of uncertain health among whom , unfortunately , nearly all great writers must be classed who are wont to have a far more even and assured tone of health in their writings , because they are better versed than are the physically robust in the philosophy of psychical health and convalescence and in their teachers morning , sunshine , forest , and fountain . <end>
<start> Disloyalty a Condition of Mastery . <end>
<start> It cannon be helped every master has but one pupil , and he becomes disloyal to him , for he also is destined for mastery . <end>
<start> Never IN Vain . <end>
<start> In the mountains of truth you never climb in vain . <end>
<start> Either you already reach a higher point to day , or you exercise your strength in order to be able to climb higher to morrow . <end>
<start> Through Grey Window Panes . <end>
<start> Is what you see through this window of the world so beautiful that you do not wish to look through any other window ay , and even try to prevent others from so doing ? <end>
<start> A Sign of Radical Changes . <end>
<start> When we dream of persons long forgotten or dead , it is a sign that we have suffered radical changes , and that the soil on which we live has been completely undermined . <end>
<start> The dead rise again , and our antiquity becomes modernity . <end>
<start> Medicine of the Soul . <end>
<start> To lie still and think little is the cheapest medicine for all diseases of the soul , and , with the aid of good will , becomes pleasanter every hour that it is used . <end>
<start> Intellectual Order of Precedence . <end>
<start> You rank far below others when you try to establish the exception and they the rule . <end>
<start> The Fatalist . <end>
<start> You must believe in fate science can compel you thereto . <end>
<start> All that develops in you out of that belief cowardice , devotion or loftiness , and uprightness bears witness to the soil in which the grain was sown , but not to the grain itself , for from that seed anything and everything can grow . <end>
<start> The Reason for Much Fretfulness . <end>
<start> He that prefers the beautiful to the useful in life will undoubtedly , like children who prefer sweetmeats to bread , destroy his digestion and acquire a very fretful outlook on the world . <end>
<start> Excess as a Remedy . <end>
<start> We can make our own talent once more acceptable to ourselves by honouring and enjoying the opposite talent for some time to excess . <end>
<start> Using excess as a remedy is one of the more refined devices in the art of life . <end>
<start> Will a Self . <end>
<start> Active , successful natures act , not according to the maxim , Know thyself , but as if always confronted with the command , Will a self , so you will become a self . <end>
<start> Fate seems always to have left them a choice . <end>
<start> Inactive , contemplative natures , on the other hand , reflect on how they have chosen their self once for all at their entry into life . <end>
<start> To Live as Far as Possible without a Following . <end>
<start> How small is the importance of followers we first grasp when we have ceased to be the followers of our followers . <end>
<start> Obscuring Oneself . <end>
<start> We must understand how to obscure ourselves in order to get rid of the gnat swarms of pestering admirers . <end>
<start> Ennui . <end>
<start> There is an ennui of the most subtle and cultured brains , to which the best that the world can offer has become stale . <end>
<start> Accustomed to eat ever more and more recherche fare and to feel disgust at coarser diet , they are in danger of dying of hunger . <end>
<start> For the very best exists but in small quantities , and has sometimes become inaccessible or hard as stone , so that even good teeth can no longer bite it . <end>
<start> The Danger in Admiration . <end>
<start> The admiration of a quality or of an art may be so strong as to deter us from aspiring to possess that quality or art . <end>
<start> What is Required of Art . <end>
<start> One man wants to enjoy himself by means of art , another for a time to get out of or above himself . <end>
<start> To meet both requirements there exists a twofold species of artists . <end>
<start> Secessions . <end>
<start> Whoever secede from us offends not us , perhaps , but certainly our adherents . <end>
<start> After Death . <end>
<start> It is only long after the deathbed missed in the case of really great men , only one dies that he is not much missed , and that the pompous funeral oration is a piece of hypocrisy . <end>
<start> Necessity first teaches the necessaries of an individual , and the proper epitaph is a belated sigh . <end>
<start> Leaving in Hades . <end>
<start> We must leave many things in the Hades of half conscious feeling , and not try to release them from their shadow existence , or else they will become , as thoughts and words , our tyrants , with cruel lust after our blood . <end>
<start> Near to beggars . <end>
<start> Even the richest intellect sometimes displays the key to the room in which his hoarded treasures repose . <end>
<start> He is then like the poorest of the poor , who must beg to get a living . <end>
<start> Chain Thinkers . <end>
<start> To him who has thought a great deal , every new thought that he hears or reads at once assumes the form of a chain . <end>
<start> Pity . <end>
<start> In the gilded sheath of pity is sometimes hidden the dagger of envy . <end>
<start> What is Genius ? <end>
<start> To aspire to a lofty aim and to will the means to that aim . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> Vanity of Combatants . <end>
<start> He who has no hope of victory in a combat , or who is obviously worsted , is all the more desirous that his style of fighting should be admired . <end>
<start> The Philosophic Life Misinterpreted . <end>
<start> At the moment when one is beginning to take philosophy seriously , the whole world fancies that one is doing the reverse . <end>
<start> Imitation . <end>
<start> By imitation , the bad gains , the good loses credit especially in art . <end>
<start> Final Teaching of History . <end>
<start> Oh that I had but lived in those times ! <end>
<start> is the exclamation of foolish and frivolous men . <end>
<start> At every period of history that we seriously review , even if it be the most bearded era of the past , we shall rather cry out at the end , Anything but a return to that ! <end>
<start> The spirit of that age would oppress you with the weight of a hundred atmospheres , the good and beautiful in it you would not enjoy , its evil you could not digest . <end>
<start> Depend upon it , posterity will pass the same verdict on our own epoch , and say that it was unbearable , that life under such conditions was intolerable . <end>
<start> And yet every one can endure his own times ? <end>
<start> Yes , because the spirit of his age not only lies upon him but is in him . <end>
<start> The spirit of the age offers resistance to itself and can bear itself . <end>
<start> Greatness as a Mask . <end>
<start> By greatness in our compartment we emitter our foes by envy that we do not conceal we almost reconcile them to us . <end>
<start> For envy levels and makes equal it is an unconscious , plaintive variety of modesty . <end>
<start> It may be indeed that here and there , for the sake of the above named advantage , envy has been assumed as a mask by those who are not envious . <end>
<start> Certainly , however , greatness in compartment is often used as the mask of envy by ambitious men who would rather suffer drawbacks and emitter their foes than let it be seen that they place them on an equal footing with themselves . <end>
<start> Unpardonable . <end>
<start> You gave him an opportunity of displaying the greatness of his character , and he did not make use of the opportunity . <end>
<start> He will never forgive you for that . <end>
<start> Contrasts . <end>
<start> The most senile thought ever conceived about men lies in the famous saying , The ego is always hateful , the most childish in the still more famous saying , Love thy neighbour as thyself . <end>
<start> With the one knowledge of men has ceased , with the other it has not yet begun . <end>
<start> MISCELLANEOUS MAXIMS AND OPINIONS . <end>
<start> A Defective Ear . <end>
<start> We still belong to the mob so long as we always shift the blame on to others we are on the track of wisdom when we always make ourselves alone responsible but the wise man finds no one to blame , neither himself nor others . <end>
<start> Who said that ? <end>
<start> Epictetus , eighteen hundred years ago . <end>
<start> The world has heard but forgotten the saying . <end>
<start> No , the world has not heard and not forgotten it : everything is not forgotten . <end>
<start> But we had not the necessary ear , the ear of Epictetus . <end>
<start> So he whispered it into his own ear ? <end>
<start> Even so : wisdom is the whispering of the sage to himself in the crowded market place . <end>
<start> A Defect of Standpoint , not of Vision We always stand a few paces too near ourselves and a few paces too far from our neighbour . <end>
<start> Hence we judge him too much in the lump , and ourselves too much by individual , occasional , insignificant features and circumstances . <end>
<start> Ignorance about Weapons . <end>
<start> How little we care whether another knows a subject or not ! <end>
<start> whereas he perhaps sweats blood at the bare idea that he may be considered ignorant on the point . <end>
<start> Yes , there are exquisite fools , who always go about with a of mighty , utterances , ready to shoot down any one who shows freely that there are matters in which their judgment is not taken into account . <end>
<start> At the Drinking Table of Experience . <end>
<start> People whose innate moderation leads them to drink but the half of every glass , will not admit that everything in the world has its lees and sediment . <end>
<start> Singing Birds . <end>
<start> The followers of a great man often put their own eyes out , so that they may be the better able to sing his praise . <end>
<start> Beyond our Ken . <end>
<start> The good generally displeased us when it is beyond our ken . <end>
<start> Rule as Mother or as Child . <end>
<start> There is one condition that gives birth to rules , another to which rules give birth . <end>
<start> Comedy . <end>
<start> We sometimes earn honour or love for actions and achievements which we have long since sloughed as the snake slough his skin . <end>
<start> We are hereby easily seduced into becoming the comic actors of our own past , and into throwing the old skin once more about our shoulders and that not merely from vanity , but from good will towards our admirers . <end>
<start> A Mistake of Biographers . <end>
<start> The small force that is required to launch a boat into the stream must not be confounded with the force of the stream that carries the boat along . <end>
<start> Yet this mistake is made in nearly all biographies . <end>
<start> Not Buying too Dear . <end>
<start> The things that we buy too dear we generally turn to bad use , because we have no love for them but only a painful recollection . <end>
<start> Thus they involve a twofold drawback . <end>
<start> The Philosophy that Society always Needs . <end>
<start> The pillars of the social structure rest upon the fundamental fact that every one cheerfully contemplates all that he is , does , and attempts , his sickness or health , his poverty or affluence , his honour or insignificance , and says to himself , After all , I would not change places with any one ! <end>
<start> Whoever wishes to add a stone to the social structure should always try to implant in mankind this cheerful philosophy of contentment and refusal to change places . <end>
<start> The Mark of a Noble Soul . <end>
<start> A noble soulful that which rises little and falls little , living . <end>
<start> Greatness and its contemplate . <end>
<start> The noblest effect of greatness is that it gives the con template a power of vision that magnifies and embellished . <end>
<start> Being Satisfied . <end>
<start> We show that we have attained maturity of understanding when we no longer go where rare flowers lurk under the thornes hedges of knowledge , but are satisfied with gardens , forests , meadows , and ploughland , remembering that life is too short for the rare and uncommon . <end>
<start> Advantage in Privation . <end>
<start> He who always lives in the warmth and fullness of the heart , and , as it were , in the summer air of the soul , cannon form an idea of that fearful delight which seizes more wintry natures , who for once in a way are kissed by the rays of love and the milder breath of a sunny February day . <end>
<start> Recipe for the Sufferer . <end>
<start> You find the burden of life too heavy ? <end>
<start> Then you must increase the burden of your life . <end>
<start> When the sufferer finally thirsts after and seeks the river of lithe , then he must become a hero to be certain of finding it . <end>
<start> The Judge . <end>
<start> He who has seen another s ideal becomes his inexorable judge , and as it were his evil conscience . <end>
<start> The Utility of Great Renunciation . <end>
<start> The useful thing about great renunciation is that it in vests us with that youthful pride through which we can thenceforth easily demand of ourselves small renunciations . <end>
<start> How Duty Acquires a Glamour . <end>
<start> You can change a brazen duty into gold in the eyes of all by always performing something more than you have promised . <end>
<start> Prayer to Mankind . <end>
<start> Forgive us our virtues so should we pray to mankind . <end>
<start> They that Create and They that Enjoy . <end>
<start> Every one who enjoys thinks that the principal thing to the tree is the fruit , but in point of fact the principal thing to it is the seed . <end>
<start> Herein lies the difference between them that create and them that enjoy . <end>
<start> The Glory of all Great Men . <end>
<start> What is the use of genius if it does not invest him who contemplates and reveres it with such freedom and loftiness of feeling that he no longer has need of genius ? <end>
<start> To make themselves superfluous is the glory of all great men . <end>
<start> The Journey to Hades . <end>
<start> I too have been in the underworld , even as Odysseus , and I shall often be there again . <end>
<start> Not sheep alone have I sacrificed , vol . <end>
<start> M that I might be able to converse with a few dead souls , but not even my own blood have I spared . <end>
<start> There were four pairs who responded to me in my sacrifice : Epicurus and Montaigne , Goethe and Spinoza , Plato and Rousseau , Pascal and Schopenhauer . <end>
<start> With them I have to come to terms . <end>
<start> When I have long wandered alone , I will let them prove me right or wrong to them will I listen , if they prove each other right or wrong . <end>
<start> In all that I say , conclude , or think out for myself and others , I fasten my eyes on those eight and see their eyes fastened on mine . <end>
<start> May the living forgive me if I look upon them at times as shadows , so pale and fretful , so restless and , alas ! <end>
<start> so eager for life . <end>
<start> Those eight , on the other hand , seem to me so living that I feel as if even now , after their death , they could never become weary of life . <end>
<start> But eternal vigour of life is the important point : what matters eternal life , or indeed life at all ? <end>
<start> PART II . <end>
<start> THE WANDERER AND HIS SHADOW . <end>
<start> THE WANDERER AND HIS SHADOW . <end>
<start> The Shadow . <end>
<start> It is so long since I heard you speak that I should like to give you an opportunity of talking . <end>
<start> The Wanderer : I hear a voice where ? <end>
<start> whose ? <end>
<start> I almost fancied that heard myself speaking , but with a voice yet weaker than my own . <end>
<start> The Shadow after a pause : Are you not glad to have an opportunity of speaking ? <end>
<start> The Wanderer : By God and everything else in which disbelieve , it is my shadow that speaks . <end>
<start> I hear it , but I do not believe it . <end>
<start> The Shadow : Let us assume that it exists , and think no more about it . <end>
<start> In another hour all will be over . <end>
<start> The Wanderer : That is just what I thought when in a forest near Pisa I saw first two and then five camels . <end>
<start> The Shadow . <end>
<start> It is all the better if we are both equally forbearing towards each other when for once our reason is silent . <end>
<start> Thus we shall avoid losing our tempers in conversation , and shall not at once apply mutual thumb screws in the event of any word sounding for once unintelligible to us . <end>
<start> If one does not know exactly how to answer , it is enough to say something . <end>
<start> Those are the reasonable terms on which I hold conversation with any person . <end>
<start> During a long talk the wisest of men becomes a fool once and a simpleton thrice . <end>
<start> The Wanderer . <end>
<start> Your moderation is not flattering to those to whom you confess it . <end>
<start> The Wanderer . <end>
<start> I thought a man s shadow was his vanity . <end>
<start> Surely vanity would never say , Am I , then , to flatter ? <end>
<start> The Shadow : Nor does human vanity , so far as I am acquainted with it , ask , as I have done twice , whether it may speak . <end>
<start> It simply speaks . <end>
<start> The Wanderer : Now I see for the first time how rude I am to you , my beloved shadow . <end>
<start> I have not said a word of my supreme delight in hearing and not merely seeing you . <end>
<start> You must know that I love shadows even as I love light . <end>
<start> For the existence of beauty of face , clearness of speech , kindliness and firmness of character , the shadow is as necessary as the light . <end>
<start> They are not opponents rather do they hold each other s hands like good friends and when the light vanishes , the shadow glides after it The Shadow : Yes , and I hate the same thing that you hate night . <end>
<start> I love men because they are rotaries of life . <end>
<start> I rejoice in the gleam of their eyes when they recognise and discover , they who never weary of recognising and discovering . <end>
<start> That shadow which all things cast when the sunshine of knowledge falls upon them that shadow too am I . <end>
<start> The Wanderer : I think I understand you , although you have expressed yourself in somewhat shadowy terms . <end>
<start> You are right . <end>
<start> Good friends give to each other here and there , as a sign of mutual understanding , an obscure phrase which to any third party is meant to be a riddle . <end>
<start> And we are good friends , you and I . <end>
<start> So enough of preambles ! <end>
<start> Some few hundred questions oppress my soul , and the time for you to answer them is perchance but short . <end>
<start> Let us see how we may come to an understanding as quickly and peaceably as possible . <end>
<start> The Shadow . <end>
<start> But shadows are more shy than men . <end>
<start> You will not reveal to any man the manner of our conversation ? <end>
<start> The Wanderer : The manner of our conversation ? <end>
<start> Heaven preserve me from wire drawn , literary dialogues ! <end>
<start> If Plato had found less pleasure in spinning them out , his readers would have found more pleasure in Plato . <end>
<start> A dialogue that in real life is a source of delight , when turned into writing and read , is a picture with nothing but false perspectives . <end>
<start> Everything is too long or too short . <end>
<start> Yet perhaps I may reveal the points on which we have come to an understanding ? <end>
<start> The Shadow . <end>
<start> With that I am content . <end>
<start> For every one will only recognise your views once more , and no one will think of the shadow . <end>
<start> The Wanderer . <end>
<start> Perhaps you are wrong , my friend ! <end>
<start> Hitherto they have observed in my views more of the shadow than of me . <end>
<start> The Shadow : More of the shadow than of the light ? <end>
<start> Is that possible ? <end>
<start> The Wanderer : Be serious , dear fool ! <end>
<start> My very first question demands seriousness . <end>
<start> I . <end>
<start> Of the Tree of Knowledge . <end>
<start> Probability , but no truth the semblance of freedom , but no freedom these are the two fruits by virtue of which the tree of knowledge cannon be confounded with the tree of life . <end>
<start> The World s Reason . <end>
<start> That the world is not the abstract essence of an eternal reasonableness is sufficiently proved by the fact that that bit of the world which we know I mean our human reason is none too reasonable . <end>
<start> And if this is not eternally and wholly wise and reasonable , the rest of the world will not be so either . <end>
<start> Here the conclusion a minor ad maus , a parte ad totem holds good , and that with decisive force . <end>
<start> In the Beginning was . <end>
<start> To glorify the origin that is the metaphysical after shoot which sprouts again at the contemplation of history , and absolutely makes us imagine that in the beginning of things lies all that is most valuable and essential . <end>
<start> Standard for the Value of Truth . <end>
<start> The difficulty of climbing mountains is no gauge of their height . <end>
<start> Yet in the case of science it is different ! <end>
<start> we are told by certain persons who wish to be considered the initiated , the difficulty in finding truth is to determine the value of truth ! <end>
<start> This insane morality originates in the idea that truths are really nothing more than gymnastic appliances , with which we have to exercise ourselves until we are thoroughly tired . <end>
<start> It is a morality for the athletes and gymnasts of the intellect . <end>
<start> Use of Words and Reality . <end>
<start> There exists a simulated contempt for all the things that mankind actually holds most important , for all everyday matters . <end>
<start> For instance , we say we only eat to live an abominable lie , like that which speaks of the procreation of children as the real purpose of all sexual pleasure . <end>
<start> Conversely , the reverence for the most important things is hardly ever quite genuine . <end>
<start> The priests and metaphysician have indeed accustomed us to a hypocritically exaggerated use of words regarding these matters , but they have not altered the feeling that these most important things are not so important as those despised everyday matters . <end>
<start> A fatal consequence of this twofold hypocrisy is that we never make these everyday matters such as eating , housing , clothes , and intercourse the object of a constant unprejudiced and universal reflection and revision , but , as such a process appears degrading , we divert from them our serious intellectual and artistic side . <end>
<start> Hence in such matters habit and frivolity win an easy victory over the thoughtless , especially over inexperienced youth . <end>
<start> On whether hand , our continual transgressions of the simplest laws of body and mind reduce us all , young and old , to a disgraceful state of dependence and servitude I mean to that fundamentally superfluous dependence upon physicians , teachers and clergymen , whose dead weight still lies heavy upon the whole of society . <end>
<start> Earthly Infirmities and their Main Cause . <end>
<start> If we look about us , we are always coming across men who have eaten eggs all their lives without observing that the oblong shaped taste the best whoo not know that a thunder storm is beneficial to the stomach that perfumes are most fragrant in cold , clear air that our sense of taste varies in different parts of our mouths that every meal at which we talk well or listen well does harm to the digestion . <end>
<start> If we are not satisfied with these examples of defective powers of observation , we shall concede all the more readily that the everyday matters are very imperfectly seen and rarely observed by the majority . <end>
<start> Is this a matter of indifference ? <end>
<start> Let us remember , after all , that from this defect are derived nearly all the bodily and spiritual infirmities of the individual . <end>
<start> Ignorance of what is good and bad for us , in the arrangement of our mode of life , the division of our day , the selection of our friends and the time we devote to them , in business and leisure , commanding and obeying , our feeling for nature and for art , our eating , sleeping , and meditation ignorance and lack of keen perceptions in the smallest and most ordinary details this it is that makes the world a vale of tears for so many . <end>
<start> Let us not say that here as everywhere the fault lies with human unreason . <end>
<start> Of reason there is enough and to spare , but it is wrongly directed and artificially diverted from these little intimate things . <end>
<start> Priests and teachers , and the sublime ambition of all idealists , coarser and subtler , din it even into the child s ears that the means of serving mankind at large depend upon altogether different things upon the salvation of the soul , the service of the State , the advancement of science , or even upon social position and property whereas the needs of the individual , his requirements great and small during the twenty four hours of the day , are quite paltry or indifferent . <end>
<start> Even Socrates attacked with all his might this arrogant neglect of the human for the benefit of humanity , and loved to indicate by a quotation from Homer the true sphere and conception of all anxiety and reflection : All that really matters , he said , is the good and evil hap I find at home . <end>
<start> Two Means of Consolation . <end>
<start> Epicurus , the soul comforter of later antiquity , said , with that marvellous insight which to this very day is so rarely to be found , that for the calming of the spirit the solution of the final and ultimate theoretical problems is by no means necessary . <end>
<start> Hence , instead of raising a barren and remote discussion of the final question , whether the Gods existed , it sufficed him to say to those who were tormented by fear of the Gods : If there are Gods , they do not concern themselves with us . <end>
<start> The latter position is far stronger and more favourable , for , by conceding a few points to the other , one makes him readier to listen and to take to heart . <end>
<start> But as soon as he sets about proving the opposite that the Gods do concern themselves with us , into what thorny jungles of error must the poor man fall , quite of his own accord , and without any cunning on the part of his interlocutor ! <end>
<start> The latter must only have enough subtlety and humanity to conceal his sympathy with this tragedy . <end>
<start> Finally , the other comes to feel disgust the strongest argument against any proposition disgust with his own hypothesis . <end>
<start> He becomes cold , and goes away in the same frame of mind as the pure atheist who says , What do the Gods matter to me ? <end>
<start> The devil take them ! <end>
<start> In other cases , especially when a half physical , balmoral assumption had cast a gloom over his spirit , Epicurus did not refute the assumption . <end>
<start> He agreed that it might be true , but that there was a second assumption to explain the same phenomenon , and that it could perhaps be maintained in other ways . <end>
<start> The plurality of hypotheses for example , that concerning the origin of conscientious scruples suffices even in our time to remove from the soul the shadows that arise so easily from pondering over a hypothesis which is isolated , merely visible , and hence overvalued a hundredfold . <end>
<start> Thus whoever wishes to console the unfortunate , the criminal , the hypochondriac , the dying , may call to mind the two soothing suggestions of Epicurus , which can be applied to a great number of problems . <end>
<start> In their simplest form they would run : firstly , granted the thing is so , it does not concern us secondly , the thing may be so , but it may also be otherwise . <end>
<start> THE WANDERER AND HIS SHADOW . <end>
<start> In the Night . <end>
<start> So soon as night begins to fall our sensations concerning everyday matters are altered . <end>
<start> There is the wind , prowling as if on forbidden paths , whispering as if in search of something , fretting because he cannon find it . <end>
<start> There is the lamplight , with its dim red glow , its weary look , unwillingly fighting against night , a sullen slave to wakeful man . <end>
<start> There are the breathing of the sleeper , with their terrible rhythm , to which an ever recurring care seems to blow the trumpet melody we do not hear it , but when the sleeper s bosom heaves we feel our heart strings tighten and when the breath sinks and almost dies away into a deathly stillness , we say to ourselves , Rest awhile , poor troubled spirit ! <end>
<start> All living creatures bear so great a burden that we wish them an eternal rest night invites to death . <end>
<start> If human beings were deprived of the sun and resisted night by means of moonlight and oil-lamps , what a philosophy would cast its veil over them ! <end>
<start> We already see only too plainly how a shadow is thrown over the spiritual and intellectual nature of man by that moiety of darkness and uselessness that envelops life . <end>
<start> Origin of the Doctrine of Free Will . <end>
<start> Necessity sways one man in the shape of his passions , another as a habit of hearing and obeying , a third as a logical conscience , a fourth as a caprice and a mischievous delight in evasions . <end>
<start> These four , go HUMAN , ALL TOO HUMAN . <end>
<start> however , seek the freedom of their will at the very point where they are most securely fettered . <end>
<start> It is as if the silkworm sought freedom of will in spinning . <end>
<start> What is the reason ? <end>
<start> Clearly this , that every one thinks himself most free where his vitality is strongest hence , as I have said , now in passion , now in duty , now in knowledge , now in caprice . <end>
<start> A man unconsciously imagines that where he is strong , where he feels most thoroughly alive , the element of his freedom must lie . <end>
<start> He thinks of dependence and apathy , independence and vivacity as forming inevitable pairs . <end>
<start> Thus an experience that a man has undergone in the social and political sphere is wrongly transferred to the ultimate metaphysical sphere . <end>
<start> There the strong man is also the free man , there the vivid feeling of joy and sorrow , the high hopes , the keen desires , the powerful hates are the attributes of the ruling , independent natures , while the thrall and the slave live in a state of dazed oppression . <end>
<start> The doctrine of free will is an invention of the ruling classes . <end>
<start> Absence of Feeling of New Chains . <end>
<start> So long as we do not feel that we are in some way dependent , we consider ourselves independent a false conclusion that shows how proud man is , how eager for dominion . <end>
<start> For he hereby assumes that he would always be sure to observe and recognise dependence so soon as he suffered it , the preliminary hypothesis being that he generally lives in independence , and that , should he lose that independence for once in a way , he would immediately detect a contrary sense tion . <end>
<start> Suppose , however , the reverse to be true that he is always living in a complex state of dependence , but thinks himself free where , through long habit , he no longer feels the weight of the chain ? <end>
<start> He only suffers from new chains , and free will really means nothing more than an absence of feeling of new chains . <end>
<start> Freedom of the Will and the Isolation of Facts . <end>
<start> Our ordinary inaccurate observation takes a group of phenomena as one and calls them a fact . <end>
<start> Between this fact and another we imagine a vacuum , we isolate each fact . <end>
<start> In reality , however , the sum of our actions and cognitions is no series of facts and intervening value , but a continuous stream . <end>
<start> Now the belief in free will is incompatible with the idea of a continuous , uniform , undivided , indivisible flow . <end>
<start> This belief presupposes that every single action is isolated and indivisible it is an atomic theory as regards volition and cognition . <end>
<start> We misunderstand facts as we misunderstand characters , speaking of similar characters and similar facts , whereas both are non existent . <end>
<start> Further , we bestow praise and blame only on this false hypothesis , that there are similar facts , that a graduated order of species of facts exists , corresponding to a graduated order of values . <end>
<start> Thus we isolate not only the single fact , but the groups of apparently equal facts good , evil , compassionate , envious actions , and so forth . <end>
<start> In both cases we are wrong . <end>
<start> The word and the concept are the most obvious reason for our belief in this isolation of groups of actions . <end>
<start> We do not merely thereby designate the things the thought at the back of our minds is that by the word and the concept we can grasp the essence of the actions . <end>
<start> We are still constantly led astray by words and actions , and are induced to think of things as simpler than they are , as separate , indivisible , existing in the absolute . <end>
<start> Language contains a hidden philosophical mythology , which , however careful we may be , breaks out afresh at every moment . <end>
<start> The belief in free will that is to say , in similar facts and isolated facts finds in language its continual apostle and advocate . <end>
<start> The Fundamental Errors . <end>
<start> A man cannon feel any psychical pleasure or pain unless he is swayed by one of two illusions . <end>
<start> Either he believes in the identity of certain facts , certain sensations , and in that case finds spiritual pleasure and pain in comparing present with past conditions and in noting their similarity or difference as is invariably the case with recollection or he believes in the freedom of the will , perhaps when he reflects , I ought not to have done this , This might have turned out differently , and from these reflections likewise he derives pleasure and pain . <end>
<start> Without the errors that are rife in every psychical pain and pleasure , humanity would never have developed . <end>
<start> For the root idea of humanity is that man is free in a world of bondage man , the eternal wonder worker , whether his deeds be good or evil man , the amazing exception , the super beast , the quasi God , the mind of creation , the indispensable , the key word to the cosmic riddle , the mighty lord of nature and despised of nature , the creature that calls its history the history of the world ! <end>
<start> vanity homo . <end>
<start> Repetition . <end>
<start> It is an excellent thing to express a thing consecutively in two ways , and thus provide it with a right and a left foot . <end>
<start> Truth can stand indeed on one leg , but with two she will walk and complete her journey . <end>
<start> Man as the Comic Actor of the World . <end>
<start> It would require beings more intellectual than men to relish to the full the humorous side of man s view of himself as the goal of all existence and of his serious pronouncement that he is satisfied only with the prospect of fulfilling a world mission . <end>
<start> If a God created the world , he created man to be his ape , as a perpetual source of amusement in the midst of his rather tedious . <end>
<start> The music of the spheres surrounding the world would then presumably be the mocking laughter of all the other creatures around mankind . <end>
<start> God in his boredom uses pain for the tickling of his favourite animal , in order to enjoy his proudly tragic gestures and expressions of suffering , and , in general , the intellectual inventiveness of the finest of his creatures as inventor of this inventor . <end>
<start> For he who invented man as a joke had more intellect and more joy in intellect than has man . <end>
<start> Even here , where our human nature is willing to humble itself , our vanity again plays us a trick , in that we men should like in this vanity at least to be quite marvellous and incomparable . <end>
<start> Our uniqueness in the world ! <end>
<start> Oh , what an improbable thing it is ! <end>
<start> Astronomers , who occasionally acquire a horizon outside our world , give us to understand that the drop of life on the earth is without significance for the total character of the mighty ocean of birth and decay that countless stars present conditions for the generation of life similar to those of the earth and yet these are but a handful in comparison with the endless number that have never known , or have long been cured , of the eruption of life that life on each of these stars , measured by the period of its existence , has been but an instant , a flicker , with long , long intervals afterwards and thus in no way the aim and final purpose of their existence . <end>
<start> Possibly the ant in the forest is quite as firmly convinced that it is the aim and purpose of the existence of the forest , as we are convinced in our imaginations almost unconsciously that the destruction of mankind involves the destruction of the world . <end>
<start> It is even modesty on our part to go no farther than this , and not to arrange a universal twilight of the world and the Gods as the funeral ceremony of the last man . <end>
<start> Even to the eye of the most unbiased astronomer a lifeless world can scarcely appear otherwise than as a shining and swinging star wherein man lies buried . <end>
<start> The Modesty of Man . <end>
<start> How little pleasure is enough for the majority to make them feel that life is good ! <end>
<start> How modest is man ! <end>
<start> Where Indifference is Necessary . <end>
<start> Nothing would be more perverse than to wait for the truths that science will finally establish concerning the first and last things , and until then to think and especially to believe in the traditional way , as one is so often advised to do . <end>
<start> The impulse that bids us seek nothing but certainties in this domain is a religious offshoot , nothing better a hidden and only apparently sceptical variety of the metaphysical need , the underlying idea being that for a long time no view of these ultimate certainties will be obtainable , and that until then the believer has the right not to trouble himself about the whole subject . <end>
<start> We have no need of these certainties about the furthermost horizons in order to live a full and efficient human life , any more than the ant needs them in order to be a good ant . <end>
<start> Rather must we ascertain the origin of that troublesome significance that we have attached to these things for so long . <end>
<start> For this we require the history of ethical and religious sentiments , since it is only under the influence of such sentiments that these most acute problems of knowledge have become so weighty and terrifying . <end>
<start> Into the outermost regions to which the mental eye can penetrate without ever penetrating into them , we have smuggled such concepts as guilt and punishment everlasting punishment , too ! <end>
<start> The darker those regions , the more careless we have been . <end>
<start> For ages men have let their imaginations run riot where they could establish nothing , and have induced posterity to accept these fantasies as something serious and true , with this abominable lie as their final : that faith is worth more than knowledge . <end>
<start> What we need now in regard to these ultimate things is not knowledge as against faith , but indifference as against faith and pretended knowledge in these matters ! <end>
<start> Everything must lie nearer to us than what has hitherto been preached to us as the most important thing , I mean the questions : What end does man serve ? <end>
<start> What is his fate after death ? <end>
<start> How does he make his peace with God ? <end>
<start> and all the rest of that bag of tricks . <end>
<start> The problems of the dogmatic philosophers , be they idealists , materialists , or realists , concern us as little as do these religious questions . <end>
<start> They all have the same object in view to force us to a decision in matters where neither faith nor knowledge is needed . <end>
<start> It is better even for the most ardent lover of knowledge that the territory open to investigation and to reason should be encircled by a belt of fog laden , treacherous marshland , a strip of ever watery , impenetrable , and indeterminate country . <end>
<start> It is just by the comparison with the realm of darkness on the edge of the world of knowledge that the bright , accessible region of that world rises in value . <end>
<start> We must once more become good friends of the everyday matters , and not , as hitherto , despise them and look beyond them at clouds and monsters of the night . <end>
<start> In forests and caverns , in marshy tracts and under dull skies , on the lowest rungs of the ladder of culture , man has lived for aeons , and lived in poverty . <end>
<start> There he has learnt to despise the present , his neighbours , his life , and himself , and we , the inhabitants of the brighter fields of Nature and mind , still inherit in our blood some taint of this contempt for everyday matters . <end>
<start> Profound Interpretations . <end>
<start> He who has interpreted a passage in an author more profoundly than was intended , has not interpreted the author but has obscured him . <end>
<start> Our metaphysician are in the same relation , or even in a worse relation , to the text of Nature . <end>
<start> For , to apply their profound interpretations , they often alter the text to suit their purpose or , in other words , corrupt the text . <end>
<start> A curious example of the corruption and of an author s text is furnished by the ideas of Schopenhauer on the pregnancy of women . <end>
<start> The sign of a continuous will to life in time , he says , is copulation the sign of the light of knowledge which is associated anew with this will and holds the possibility of a deliverance , and that too in the highest degree of clearness , is the renewed incarnation of the will to life . <end>
<start> This incarnation is betoken by pregnancy , which is therefore frank and open , and even proud , whereas copulation hides itself like a criminal . <end>
<start> He declares that every woman , if surprised in the sexual act , would be likely to die of shame , but displays her pregnancy without a trace of shame , nay even with a sort of pride . <end>
<start> Now , firstly , this condition cannon easily be displayed more aggressively than it displays itself , and when Schopenhauer gives prominence only to the intentional character of the display , he is fashioning his text to suit the interpretation . <end>
<start> Moreover , his statement of the universality of the phenomenon is not true . <end>
<start> He speaks of every woman . <end>
<start> Many women , especially the younger , often appear painfully ashamed of their condition , even in the presence of their nearest kinsfolk . <end>
<start> And when women of riper years , especially in the humbler classes , do actually appear proud of their condition , it is because they would give us to understand that they are still desirable to their husbands . <end>
<start> That a neighbour on seeing them or a passing stranger should say or think Can it be possible ? <end>
<start> that is an alms always acceptable to the vanity of women of low mental capacity . <end>
<start> In the reverse instance , to conclude from Schopenhauer s proposition , the cleverest and most intelligent women would tend more than any to exult openly in their condition . <end>
<start> For they have the best prospect of giving birth to an intellectual prodigy , in whom the will can once more negative itself for the universal good . <end>
<start> Stupid women , on the other hand , would have every reason to hide their pregnancy more modestly than anything they hide . <end>
<start> It cannon be said that this view corresponds to reality . <end>
<start> Granted , however , that Schopenhauer was right on the general principle that women show more self satisfaction when pregnant than at any other time , a better explanation than this lies to hand . <end>
<start> One might imagine the clucking of a hen even before she lays an egg , saying , Look ! <end>
<start> look ! <end>
<start> I shall lay an egg ! <end>
<start> I shall lay an egg ! <end>
<start> M THE WANDERER AND HIS SHADOW . <end>
<start> The Modern Diogenes . <end>
<start> Before we look for man , we must have found the lantern . <end>
<start> Will it have to be the Cynic s lantern ? <end>
<start> immorality . <end>
<start> Moralists must now put up with being rated as immorality , because they dissect morals . <end>
<start> He , however , who would dissect must kill , but only in order that we may know more , judge better , live better , not in order that all the world may dissect . <end>
<start> Unfortunately , men still think that every moralist in his every action must be a pattern for others to imitate . <end>
<start> They confound him with the preacher of morality . <end>
<start> The older moralists did not dissect enough and preached too often , whence that confusion and the unpleasant consequences for our latter day moralists are derived . <end>
<start> A Caution against Confusion . <end>
<start> There are moralists who treat the strong , noble , self denying attitude of such beings as the heroes of Plutarch , or the pure , enlightened , warmth giving state of soul peculiar to truly good men and women , as difficult scientific problems . <end>
<start> They investigate the origin of such phenomena , indicating the complex element in the apparent simplicity , and directing their gaze to the tangled skein of motives , the delicate web of conceptual illusions , and the sentiments of individuals or of groups , that are a legacy of ancient days gradually increased . <end>
<start> Such moralists are very different from those with whom they are most commonly confounded , from those petty minds that do not believe at all in these modes of thought and states of soul , and imagine their own poverty to be hidden somewhere behind the glamour of greatness and purity . <end>
<start> The moralists say , Here are problems , and these pitiable creatures say , Here are impostors and deceptions . <end>
<start> Thus the latter deny the existence of the very things which the former are at pains to explain . <end>
<start> Man as the measures . <end>
<start> Perhaps all human morality had its origin in the tremendous excitement that seized primitive man when he discovered measure and measuring , scales and weighing for the word bench man means the measures he wished to name himself after his greatest discovery ! <end>
<start> With these ideas they mounted into regions that are quite beyond all measuring and weighing , but did not appear to be so in the beginning . <end>
<start> The Principle of Equilibrium . <end>
<start> The robber and the man of power who promises to protect a community from robbers are perhaps at bottom beings of the same mould , save that the latter attains his ends by other means than the former that is to say , through regular imports paid to him by the community , and no longer through forced contributions . <end>
<start> The same relation exists between merchant and pirate , who for a long period are one and the same person : where the one function appears to them inadvisable , they exercise the other . <end>
<start> Even to day mercantile morality is really nothing but a refinement on piratical morality buying in the cheapest market , at prime cost if possible , and selling in the dearest . <end>
<start> The essential point is that the man of power promises to maintain the equilibrium against the robber , and herein the weak find a possibility of living . <end>
<start> For either they must group themselves into an equivalent power , or they must subject themselves to some one of equivalent power i . <end>
<start> e . <end>
<start> render service in return for his efforts . <end>
<start> The latter course is generally preferred , because it really keeps two dangerous beings in check the robber through the man of power , and the man of power through the standpoint of advantage for the latter profits by treating his subjects with graciousness and tolerance , in order that they may support not only themselves but their ruler . <end>
<start> As a matter of fact , conditions may still be hard and cruel enough , yet in comparison with the complete annihilation that was formerly always a possibility , men breathe freely . <end>
<start> The community is at first the organisation of the weak to counterbalance menacing forces . <end>
<start> An organisation to outweigh those forces would be more advisable , if its members grew strong enough to destroy the adverse power : and when it is a question of one mighty oppressor , the attempt will certainly be made . <end>
<start> But if the one man is the head of a clan , or if he has a large following , a rapid and decisive annihilation is improbable , and a long or permanent feud is only to be expected . <end>
<start> This feud , however , involves the least desirable condition for the community , for it thereby loses the time to provide for its means of subsistence with the necessary regularity , and sees the product of all work hourly threatened . <end>
<start> Hence the community prefers to raise its power of attack and defence to the exact plane on which the power of its dangerous neighbour stands , and to give him to understand that an equal weight now lies in its own side of the scales so why not be good friends ? <end>
<start> Thus equilibrium is a most important conception for the understanding of the ancient doctrines of law and morals . <end>
<start> Equilibrium is , in fact , the basis of justice . <end>
<start> When justice in ruder ages says , An eye for an eye , a tooth for a tooth , it presupposes the attainment of this equilibrium and tries to maintain it by means of this compensation so that , when crime is committed , the injured party will not take the revenge of blind anger . <end>
<start> By means of the jus talons the equilibrium of the disturbed relations of power is restored , for in such primitive times an eye or an arm more means a bit more power , more weight . <end>
<start> In a community where all consider themselves equal , disgrace and punishment await crime that is , violations of the principle of equilibrium . <end>
<start> Disgrace is thrown into the scale as a counter weight against the encroaching individual , who has gained profit by his encroachment , and now suffers losses through disgrace which annul and outweigh the previous profits . <end>
<start> Punishment , in the same way , sets up a far greater counter weight against the preponderance which every criminal hopes to obtain imprisonment as against a deed of violence , resting tion and fines as against theft . <end>
<start> Thus the sinner is reminded that his action has excluded him from the community and from its moral advantages , since the community treats him as an inferior , a weaker brother , an outsider . <end>
<start> For this reason punishment is not merely retaliation , but has something more , something of the cruelty of the state of nature , and of this it would serve as a reminder . <end>
<start> Whether the Adherents of the Doctrine of Free Will have a Right to Punish ? <end>
<start> Men whose vocation it is to judge and punish try to establish in every case whether an evil doer is really responsible for his act , whether he was able to apply his reasoning powers , whether he acted with motives and not unconsciously or under constraint . <end>
<start> If he is punished , it is because he preferred the worse to the better motives , which he must consequently have known . <end>
<start> Where this knowledge is wanting , man is , according to the prevailing view , not responsible unless his ignorance , e . <end>
<start> g . <end>
<start> his ignorant legis , be the consequence of an intentional neglect to learn what he ought : in that case he already preferred the worse to the better motives at the time when he refused to learn , and must now pay the penalty of his unwise choice . <end>
<start> If , on the other hand , perhaps through stupidity or shortsightedness , he has never seen the better motives , he is generally not punished , for people say that he made a wrong choice , he acted like a brute beast . <end>
<start> The intentional rejection of the better reason is now needed before we treat the offender as fit to be punished . <end>
<start> But how can any one be intentionally more unreasonable than he ought to be ? <end>
<start> Whence comes the decision , if the scales are loaded with good and bad motives ? <end>
<start> So the origin is not error or blindness , not an internal or external constraint ? <end>
<start> It should furthermore be remembered that every so called external constraint is nothing more than the internal constraint of fear and pain . <end>
<start> Whence ? <end>
<start> is the repeated question . <end>
<start> So reason is not to be the cause of action , because reason cannon decide against the better motives ? <end>
<start> Thus we call free will to our aid . <end>
<start> Absolute discretion is to decide , and a moment is to intervene when no motive exercises an influence , when the deed is done as a miracle , resulting from nothing . <end>
<start> This assumed discretion is punished in a case where no discretion should rule . <end>
<start> Reason , which knows law , prohibition , and command , should have left no choice , they say , and should have acted as a constraint and a higher power . <end>
<start> Hence the offender is punished because he makes use of free will in other words , has acted without motive where he should have been guided by motives . <end>
<start> But why did he do it ? <end>
<start> This question must not even be asked the deed was done without a Why ? <end>
<start> without motive , without origin , being a thing purposeless , unreasoned . <end>
<start> However , according to the above named preliminary condition of , such a deed should not be punished at all ! <end>
<start> Moreover , even this reason for punishing should not hold good , that in this case something had not been done , had been omitted , that reason had not been used at all : for at any rate the omission was union tensional , and only intentional omission is considered punishable . <end>
<start> The offender has indeed preferred the worse to the better motives , but without motive and purpose : he has indeed failed to apply his reason , but not exactly with the object of not applying it . <end>
<start> The very assumption made in the case of punishable crime , that the criminal intentionally renounced his reason , is removed by the hypothesis of free will . <end>
<start> According to your own principles , you must not punish , you adherents of the doctrine of free will ! <end>
<start> These principles are , however , nothing but a very marvellous conceptual mythology , and the hen that hatched them has brooded on her eggs far away from all reality . <end>
<start> Judging the Criminal and his Judge . <end>
<start> The criminal , who knows the whole concatenation of circumstances , does not consider his act so far beyond the bounds of order and comprehension as does his judge . <end>
<start> His punishment , however , is measured by the degree of astonishment that seizes the judge when he finds the crime incomprehensible . <end>
<start> If the defending counsel s knowledge of the case and its previous history extends far enough , the so called extenuating circumstances which he duly pleads must end by absolving his client from all guilt . <end>
<start> Or , to put it more plainly , the advocate will , step by step , tone down and finally remove the astonishment of the judge , by forcing every honest listener to the tacit avowal , He was bound to act as he did , and if we punished , we should be punishing eternal Necessity . <end>
<start> Measuring the punishment by the degree of knowledge we possess or can obtain of the previous history of the crime is that not in conflict with all equity ? <end>
<start> Exchange and Equity . <end>
<start> In an exchange , the only just and honest course would be for either party to demand only so much as he considers his commodity to be worth , allowance being made for trouble in acquisition , scarcity , time spent and so forth , besides the subjective value . <end>
<start> As soon as you make your price bear a relation to the other s need , you become a refined sort of robber and extortion . <end>
<start> If money is the sole medium of exchange , we must remember that a shilling is by no means the same thing in the hands of a rich heir , a farm labourer , a merchant , and a university student . <end>
<start> It would be equitable for every one to receive much or little for his money , according as he has done much or little to earn it . <end>
<start> In practice , as we all know , the reverse is the case . <end>
<start> In the world of high finance the shilling of the idle rich man can buy more than that of the poor , industrious man . <end>
<start> Legal Conditions as Means . <end>
<start> Law , where it rests upon contracts between equals , holds good so long as the power of the parties to the contract remains equal or similar . <end>
<start> Wisdom created law to end all feuds and useless expenditure among men on an equal footing . <end>
<start> Quite as definite an end is put to this waste , however , when one party has become decidedly weaker than the other . <end>
<start> Subjection enters and law ceases , but the result is the same as that attained by law . <end>
<start> For now it is the wisdom of the superior which advises to spare the inferior and not uselessly to squander his strength . <end>
<start> Thus the position of the inferior is often more favourable than that of the equal . <end>
<start> Hence legal conditions are temporary means counselled by wisdom , and not ends . <end>
<start> Explanation of Malicious Joy . <end>
<start> Malicious joy arises when a man consciously finds himself in evil plight and feels anxiety or remorse or pain . <end>
<start> The misfortune that overtakes B . <end>
<start> makes him equal to A . <end>
<start> and A . <end>
<start> is reconciled and no longer envious . <end>
<start> If A . <end>
<start> is prosperous , he still hoards up in his memory B . <end>
<start> s misfortune as a capital , so as to throw it in the scale as a counter weight when he himself suffers adversity . <end>
<start> In this case too he feels malicious joy Schadenfreude . <end>
<start> The sentiment of equality thus applies its standard to the domain of luck and chance . <end>
<start> Malicious joy is the commonest expression of victory and restoration of equality , even in a higher state of civilisation . <end>
<start> This emotion has only been in existence since the time when man learnt to look upon another as his equal in other words , since the foundation of society . <end>
<start> The Arbitrary Element in the Award of Punishment . <end>
<start> To most criminals punishment comes just as illegitimate children come to women . <end>
<start> They have done the same thing a hundred times without any bad consequences . <end>
<start> Suddenly comes discovery , and with discovery punishment . <end>
<start> Yet habit should make the deed for which the criminal is punished appear more excusable , for he has developed a propensity that is hard to resist . <end>
<start> Instead of this , the criminal is punished more severely if the suspicion of habitual crime rests on him , and habit is made a valid reason against all attenuation . <end>
<start> On the other hand , a model life , wherein crime shows up in more terrible contrast , should make the guilt appear more heavy ! <end>
<start> But here the custom is to soften the punishment . <end>
<start> Everything is measured not from the standpoint of the criminal but from that of society and its losses and dangers . <end>
<start> The previous utility of an individual is weighed against his one nefarious action , his previous criminality is added to that recently discovered , and punishment is thus meted out as highly as possible . <end>
<start> But if we thus punish or reward a man s past for in the former case the diminution of punishment is a reward we ought to go farther back and punish and reward the cause of his past I mean parents , teachers , society . <end>
<start> In many instances we shall then find the judges somehow or other sharing in the guilt . <end>
<start> It is arbitrary to stop at the criminal himself when we punish his past : if we will not grant the absolute excitability of every crime , we should stop at each individual case and probe no farther into the past in other words , isolate guilt and not connect it with previous actions . <end>
<start> Otherwise we sin against logic . <end>
<start> The teachers of free will should draw the inevitable conclusion from their doctrine of free will and boldly decree : No action has a past . <end>
<start> Envy and her Nobler Sister . <end>
<start> Where equality is really recognised and permanently established , we see the rise of that propensity that is generally considered immoral , and would scarcely be conceivable in a state of nature envy . <end>
<start> The envious man is susceptible to every sign of individual superiority to the common herd , and wishes to depress every one once more to the level or raise himself to the superior plane . <end>
<start> Hence arise two different modes of action , which Hesiod designated good and bad eric . <end>
<start> In the same way , in a condition of equality there arises indignation if A . <end>
<start> is prosperous above and B . <end>
<start> unfortunate beneath their deserts and equality . <end>
<start> These latter , however , are emotions of nobler natures . <end>
<start> They feel the want of justice and equity in things that are independent of the arbitrary choice of men or , in other words , they desire the equality recognised by man to be recognised as well by Nature and chance . <end>
<start> They are angry that men of equal merits should not have equal fortune . <end>
<start> The Envy of the Gods . <end>
<start> The envy of the Gods arises when a despised person sets himself on an equality with his superior like Ajax , or is made equal with him by the favour of fortune like nine , the too favoured mother . <end>
<start> In the social class system this envy demands that no one shall have merits above his station , that his prosperity shall be on a level with his position , and especially that his self consciousness shall not outgrow the limits of his rank . <end>
<start> Often the victorious general , or the pupil who achieves a masterpiece , has experienced the envy of the gods . <end>
<start> Vanity as an Anti Social Aftergrowth . <end>
<start> As men , for the sake of security , have made themselves equal in order to found communities , but as also this conception is imposed by a sort of constraint and is entirely opposed to the instincts of the individual , so , the more universal security is guaranteed , the more do new offshoots of the old instinct for predominance appear . <end>
<start> Such offshoots appear in the setting up of class distinctions , in the demand for professional dignities and privileges , and , generally speaking , in vanity manners , dress , speech , and so forth . <end>
<start> So soon as danger to the community is apparent , the majority , who were unable to assert their preponderance in a time of universal peace , once more bring about the condition of equality , and for the time being the absurd privileges and vanities disappear . <end>
<start> If the community , however , collapses utterly and anarchy reigns supreme , there arises the state of nature : an absolutely ruthless inequality as recounted by Thucydides in the case of Corcyra . <end>
<start> Neither a natural justice nor a natural injustice exists . <end>
<start> Equity . <end>
<start> Equity is a development of justice , and arises among such as do not come into conflict with the communal equality . <end>
<start> This more subtle recognition of the principle of equilibrium is applied to cases where nothing is prescribed by law . <end>
<start> Equity looks forwards and backwards , its maxim being , Do unto others as you would that they should do unto you . <end>
<start> equus means : This principle is conformable to our equality it tones down even our small differences to an appearance of equality , and expects us to be indulgent in cases where we are not compelled to pardon . <end>
<start> Elements of Revenge . <end>
<start> The word revenge is spoken so quickly that it almost seems as if it could not contain more than one conceptual and emotional root . <end>
<start> Hence we are still at pains to find this root . <end>
<start> Our economists , in the same way , have never wearied of scenting a similar unity in the word value , and of hunting after the primitive root idea of value . <end>
<start> As if all words were not pockets , into which this or that or several things have been stuffed at once ! <end>
<start> So revenge is now one thing , now another , and sometimes more composite . <end>
<start> Let us first distinguish that defensive counter blow , which we strike , almost unconsciously , even at inanimate objects such as machinery in motion that have hurt us . <end>
<start> The notion is to set a check to the object that has hurt us , by bringing the machine to a stop . <end>
<start> Sometimes the force of this counter blow , in order to attain its object , will have to be strong enough to shatter the machine . <end>
<start> If the machine be too strong to be disorganised by one man , the latter will all the same strike the most violent blow he can as a sort of last attempt . <end>
<start> We behave similarly towards persons who hurt us , at the immediate sensation of the hurt . <end>
<start> If we like to call this an act of revenge , well and good : but we must remember that here self preservation alone has set its cog wheels of reason in motion , and that after all we do not think of the doer of the injury but only of ourselves . <end>
<start> We act without any idea of doing injury in return , only with a view to getting away safe and sound . <end>
<start> It needs time to pass in thought from oneself to one s adversary and ask oneself at what point he is most vulnerable . <end>
<start> This is done in the second variety of revenge , the preliminary idea of which is to consider the vulnerability and susceptibility of the other . <end>
<start> The intention then is to give pain . <end>
<start> On the other hand , the idea of securing himself against further injury is in this case so entirely outside the avenger s horizon , that he almost regularly brings about his own further injury and often foresees it in cold blood . <end>
<start> If in the first sort of revenge it was the fear of a second blow that made the counter blow as strong as possible , in this case there is an almost complete indifference to what one s adversary will do : the strength of the counter blow is only determined by what he has already done to us . <end>
<start> Then what has he done ? <end>
<start> What profit is it to us if he is now suffering , after we have suffered through him ? <end>
<start> This is a case of readjustment , whereas the first act of revenge only serves the purpose of self-preservation . <end>
<start> It may be that through our adversary we have lost property , rank , friends , children these losses are not recovered by revenge , the readjustment only concerns a subsidiary loss which is added to all the other losses . <end>
<start> The revenge of readjustment does not preserve one from further injury , it does not make good the injury already suffered except in one case . <end>
<start> If our honour has suffered through our adversary , revenge can restore it . <end>
<start> But in any case honour has suffered an injury if intentional harm has been done us , because our adversary proved thereby that he was not afraid of us . <end>
<start> By revenge we prove that we are not afraid of him either , and herein lies the settlement , the readjustment . <end>
<start> The intention of showing their complete lack of fear goes so far in some people that the dangers of revenge loss of health or life or other losses are in their eyes an indispensable condition of every vengeful act . <end>
<start> Hence they practise the duel , although the law also offers them aid in obtaining satisfaction for what they have suffered . <end>
<start> They are not satisfied with a safe means of recovering their honour , because this would not prove their fearlessness . <end>
<start> In the first named variety of revenge it is just fear that strikes the counter blow in the second case it is the absence of fear , which , as has been said , wishes to manifest itself in the counter blow . <end>
<start> Thus nothing appears more different than the motives of the two courses of action which are designated by the one word revenge . <end>
<start> Yet it often happens that the avenger is not precisely certain as to what really prompted his deed : perhaps he struck the counter blow from fear and the instinct of self preservation , but in the background , when he has time to reflect upon the standpoint of wounded honour , he imagines that he has avenged himself for the sake of his honour this motive is in any case more reputable than the other . <end>
<start> An essential point is whether he sees his honour injured in the eyes of others the world or only in the eyes of his offenders : in the latter case he will prefer secret , in the former open revenge . <end>
<start> Accordingly , as he enters strongly or feebly into the soul of the doer and the spectator , his revenge will be more bitter or more tame . <end>
<start> If he is entirely lacking in this sort of imagination , he will not think at all of revenge , as the feeling of honour is not present in him , and accordingly cannon be wounded . <end>
<start> In the same way , he will not think of revenge if he despises the offender and the spectator because as objects of his contempt they cannon give him honour , and accordingly cannon rob him of honour . <end>
<start> Finally , he will forego revenge in the not uncommon case of his loving the offender . <end>
<start> It is true that he then suffers loss of honour in the other s eyes , and will perhaps become less worthy of having his love returned . <end>
<start> But even to renounce all recital of love is a sacrifice that love is ready to make when its only object is to avoid hurting the beloved object : this would mean hurting oneself more than one is hurt by the sacrifice . <end>
<start> Accordingly , every one will avenge himself , unless he be bereft of honour or inspired by contempt or by love for the offender . <end>
<start> Even if he turns to the law courts , he desires revenge as a private individual but also , as a thoughtful , prudent man of society , he desires the revenge of society upon one who does not respect it . <end>
<start> Thus by legal punishment private honour as well as that of society is restored that is to say , punishment is revenge . <end>
<start> Punishment undoubtedly contains the first mentioned element of revenge , in as far as by its means society helps to preserve itself , and strikes a counter blow in self defence . <end>
<start> Punishment desires to prevent further injury , to scare other offenders . <end>
<start> In this way the two elements of revenge , different as they are , are united in punishment , and this may perhaps tend most of all to maintain the above mentioned confusion of ideas , thanks to which the individual avenger generally does not know what he really wants . <end>
<start> The Virtues that Damage Us . <end>
<start> As members of communities we think we have no right to exercise certain virtues which afford us great honour and some pleasure as private individuals for example , indulgence and favour towards miscreants of all kinds in short , every mode of action whereby the advantage of society would suffer through our virtue . <end>
<start> No bench of judges , face to face with its conscience , may permit itself to be gracious . <end>
<start> This privilege is reserved for the king as an individual , and we are glad when he makes use of it , proving that we should like to be gracious individually , but not collectively . <end>
<start> Society recognises only the virtues profitable to her , or at least not injurious to her virtues like justice , which are exercised without loss , or , in fact , at compound interest . <end>
<start> The virtues that damage us cannon have originated in society , because even now opposition to them arises in every small society that is in the making . <end>
<start> Such virtues are therefore those of men of unequal standing , invented by the superior individuals they are the virtues of rulers , and the idea underlying them is : I am mighty enough to put up with an obvious loss that is a proof of my power . <end>
<start> Thus they are virtues closely akin to pride . <end>
<start> The Casuistry of Advantage . <end>
<start> There would be no moral casuistry if there were no casuistry of advantage . <end>
<start> The most free and refined intelligence is often incapable of choosing between two alternatives in such a way that his choice necessarily involves the greater advantage . <end>
<start> In such cases we choose because we must , and afterwards often feel a kind of emotional sea sickness . <end>
<start> Turning Hypocrite . <end>
<start> Every beggar turns hypocrite , like every one who makes his living out of incidence , be it personal or public . <end>
<start> The beggar does not feel want nearly so keenly as he must make others feel it , if he wishes to make a living by mendicant . <end>
<start> A Sort of Cult of the Passions . <end>
<start> yoof the formidable nature of human passions , whole world structure . <end>
<start> As if the passions were always and everywhere formidable ! <end>
<start> As if this sort of terror must always exist in the world ! <end>
<start> Through a carelessness in small matters , through a deficiency in observation of self and of the rising generation , you have yourselves allowed your passions to develop into such unruly monsters that you are frightened now at the mere mention of the word passion ! <end>
<start> It rests with you and it rests with us to divest the passions of their formidable features and so to dam them that they do not become devastating floods . <end>
<start> We must not exalt our errors into eternal fatalities . <end>
<start> Rather shall we honestly endeavour to convert all the passions of humanity into sources of joy . <end>
<start> The Sting of Conscience . <end>
<start> The sting of conscience , like the gnawing of a dog at a stone , is mere foolishness . <end>
<start> Origin of Rights . <end>
<start> Rights may be traced to traditions , traditions to momentary agreements . <end>
<start> At some time or other men were mutually content with the consequences of making an agreement , and , again , too indolent formally to renew it . <end>
<start> Thus they went on living as if it had constantly been renewed , and gradually , when oblivion cast its The play on Freudenschaften i . <end>
<start> e . <end>
<start> pleasure giving passions and Leidenschaften i . <end>
<start> e . <end>
<start> pain giving passions is often used by Nietzsche , and is untranslatable . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> veil over the origin , they thought they possessed a sacred , unalterable foundation on which every generation would be compelled to build . <end>
<start> Tradition was now a constraint , even if it no more involved the profit originally derived from making the agreement . <end>
<start> Here the weak have always found their strong fortress . <end>
<start> They are inclined to immortalise the momentary agreement , the single act of favour shown towards them . <end>
<start> The Significance of Oblivion in Moral Sentiment . <end>
<start> The same actions that in primitive society first aimed at the common advantage were later on performed from other motives : from fear or reverence of those who demanded and recommended them or from habit , because men had seen them done about them from childhood upwards or from kindness , because the practising of them caused delight and approving looks on all sides or from vanity , because they were praised . <end>
<start> Such actions , in which the fundamental motive , that of utility , has been forgotten , are then called moral not , indeed , because they are done from those other motives , but because they are not done with a conscious purpose of utility . <end>
<start> Whence the hatred of utility that suddenly manifests itself here , and by which all praiseworthy actions formally exclude all actions for the sake of utility ? <end>
<start> Clearly society , the rallying point of all morality and of all maxims in praise of moral action , has had to battle too long and too fiercely with the selfishness and obstinacy of the individual not to rate every motive morally higher than utility . <end>
<start> Hence it looks as if morals had not sprung from utility , whereas in fact morals are originally the public utility , which had great difficulty in prevailing over the interests of the unit and securing a loftier reputation . <end>
<start> The Heirs to the Wealth of Morality . <end>
<start> Even in the domain of morals there is an inherited wealth , which is owned by the gentle , the good-tempered , the compassionate , the indulgent . <end>
<start> They have inherited from their forefathers their gentle mode of action , but not common sense the source of that mode of action . <end>
<start> The pleasant thing about this wealth is that one must always bestow and communicate a portion of it , if its presence is to be felt at all . <end>
