<start> Thus this wealth unconsciously aims at bridging the gulf between the morally rich and the morally poor , and , what is its best and most remarkable feature , not for the sake of a future mean between rich and poor , but for the sake of a universal prosperity and superfluity . <end>
<start> Such may be the prevailing view of inherited moral wealth , but it seems to me that this view is maintained more in major gloria of morality than in honour of truth . <end>
<start> Experience at least establishes a maxim which must serve , if not as a refutation , at any rate as an important check upon that generalisation . <end>
<start> Without the most exquisite intelligence , says experience , without the most refined capacity for choice and a strong propensity to observe the mean , the morally rich will become spendthrifts of morality . <end>
<start> For by abandoning themselves without restraint to their compassionate , gentle , conciliatory , harmonising instincts , they make all about them more careless , more covetous , and more sentimental . <end>
<start> The children of these highly moral spendthrifts easily and sad to relate at best become pleasant but futile wasters . <end>
<start> The Judge and Extenuating Circumstances . <end>
<start> One should behave as a man of honour even towards the devil and pay his debts , said an old soldier , when the story of Faust had been related to him in rather fuller detail . <end>
<start> Hell is the right place for Faust ! <end>
<start> You are terrible , you men ! <end>
<start> cried his wife how can that be ? <end>
<start> After all , his only fault was having no ink in his ink stand ! <end>
<start> It is indeed a sin to write with blood , but surely for that such a handsome man ought not to burn in Hell fire ? <end>
<start> Problem of the Duty of Truth . <end>
<start> Duty is an imperious sentiment that forces us to action . <end>
<start> We call it good , and consider it outside the pale of discussion . <end>
<start> The origin , limits , and justification of duty we will not debate or allow to be debated . <end>
<start> But the thinker considers everything an evolution and every evolution a subject for discussion , and is accordingly without duty so long as he is merely a thinker . <end>
<start> As such , he would not recognise the duty of seeing and speaking the truth he would not feel the sentiment at all . <end>
<start> He asks , whence comes it and whither will it go ? <end>
<start> But even this questioning appears to him questionable . <end>
<start> Surely , however , the consequence would be that the thinker s machinery would no longer work properly if he could really feel himself unencumbered by duty in the search for knowledge ? <end>
<start> It would appear , then , that for fuel the same element is necessary as must be investigated by means of the machine . <end>
<start> Perhaps the formula will be : granted there were a duty of recognising truth , what is then the truth in regard to every other kind of duty ? <end>
<start> But is not a hypothetical sense of duty a contradiction in terms ? <end>
<start> Grades of Morals . <end>
<start> Morality is primarily a means of preserving the community and saving it from destruction . <end>
<start> Next it is a means of maintaining the community on a certain plane and in a certain degree of benevolence . <end>
<start> Its motives are fear and hope , and these in a more coarse , rough , and powerful form , the more the propensity towards the perverse , one sided , and personal still persists . <end>
<start> The most terrible means of intimidation must be brought into play so long as milder forms have no effect and that twofold species of preservation cannon be attained . <end>
<start> The strongest intimidation , by the way , is the invention of a hereafter with a hell everlasting . <end>
<start> For this purpose we must have racks and torturers of the soul . <end>
<start> Further grades of morality , and accordingly means to the end referred to , are the commandments of a God as in the Mosaic law . <end>
<start> Still further and higher are the commandments of an absolute sense of duty with a Thou shalt all rather roughly hewn yet broad steps , because on the finer , narrower steps men cannon yet set their feet . <end>
<start> Then comes a morality of inclination , of taste , finally of insight which is beyond all the illusory motives of morality , but has convinced itself that humanity for long periods could be allowed no other . <end>
<start> The Morality of Pity in the Mouths of the Intemperate . <end>
<start> All those who are not sufficiently masters of themselves and do not know morality as a self control and self conquest continuously exercised in things great and small , unconsciously come to glorify the good , compassionate , benevolent impulses of that instinctive morality which has no head , but seems merely to consist of a heart and helpful hands . <end>
<start> It is to their interest even to cast suspicion upon a morality of reason and to set up the other as the sole morality . <end>
<start> Sewers of the Soul . <end>
<start> Even the soul must have its definite sewers , through which it can allow its filth to flow off : for this purpose it may use persons , relations , social classes , its native country , or the world , or finally for the wholly arrogant I mean our modern pessimists le bon Dieu . <end>
<start> A Kind of Rest and Contemplation . <end>
<start> Beware lest your rest and contemplation resemble that of a dog before a butcher s stall , prevented by fear from advancing and by greed from retiring , and opening its eyes wide as though they were mouths . <end>
<start> Prohibitions without Reasons . <end>
<start> A prohibition , the reason of which we do not understand or admit , is almost a command , not only for the stiff-necked but for the thirst after knowledge . <end>
<start> We at once make an experiment in order to learn why the prohibition was made . <end>
<start> Moral prohibitions , like those of the dialogue , are only suited to ages when reason lies vanquished . <end>
<start> Nowadays a prohibition like Thou shalt not kill , Thou shalt not commit adultery , laid down without reasons , would have an injurious rather than a beneficial effect . <end>
<start> Character Portrait . <end>
<start> What sort of a man is it that can say of himself : I despise very easily , but never hate . <end>
<start> I at once find out in every man something which can be honoured and for which I honour him : the so called amiable qualities attract me but little ? <end>
<start> Pity and Contempt . <end>
<start> The expression of pity is regarded as a sign of contempt , because one has clearly ceased to be an object offer as soon as one becomes an object of pity . <end>
<start> One has sunk below the level of the equilibrium . <end>
<start> For this equilibrium does not satisfy human vanity , which is only satis find by the feeling that one is imposing respect and awe . <end>
<start> Hence it is difficult to explain why pity is so highly prized , just as we need to explain why the unselfish man , who is originally despised or feared as being artful , is praised . <end>
<start> the Capacity of Being Small . <end>
<start> We must be as near to flowers , grasses , and butterflies as a child , that is , not much bigger than they . <end>
<start> We adults have grown up beyond them and have to stoop to them . <end>
<start> I think the grasses hate us when we confess our love for them . <end>
<start> He who would have a share in all good things must understand at times how to be small . <end>
<start> The Sum Total of Conscience . <end>
<start> The subtotal of our conscience is all that has regularly been demanded of us , without reason , in the days of our childhood , by people whom we respected or feared . <end>
<start> From conscience comes that feeling of obligation This I must do , this omit which does not ask , Why must I ? <end>
<start> In all cases where a thing is done with because and why , man acts without conscience , but not necessarily on that account against conscience . <end>
<start> The belief in authority is the source of conscience which is therefore not the voice of God in the heart of man , but the voice of some men in man . <end>
<start> Conquest of the Passions . <end>
<start> The man who has overcome his passions has entered into possession of the most fruitful soil , like the colonist who has become lord over bogs and forests . <end>
<start> To sow the seed of spiritual good works on the soil of the vanquished passions is the next and most urgent task . <end>
<start> The conquest itself is a means , not an end : if it be not so regarded , all kind of weeds and devil s crop quickly spring up upon the fertile soil that has been cleared , and soon the growth is all wilder and more luxuriant than before . <end>
<start> Skill in Service . <end>
<start> All so called practical men have skill in service , whether it be serving others or themselves this is what makes them practical . <end>
<start> Robinson owned a servant even better than Friday his name was Crusoe . <end>
<start> Intellect and Boredom . <end>
<start> The proverb , The Hungarian is far too lazy to feel bored , gives food for thought . <end>
<start> Only the highest and most active animals are capable of being bored . <end>
<start> The boredom of God on the seventh day of Creation would be a subject for a great poet . <end>
<start> Intercourse with Animals . <end>
<start> The origin of our morality may still be observed in our animals . <end>
<start> Where advantage or the reverse do not come into play , we have a feeling of complete irresponsibility . <end>
<start> For example , we kill or wound insects or let them live , and as a rule think no more about it . <end>
<start> We are so clumsy that even our gracious acts towards flowers and small animals are almost always murderous : this does not in the least detract from our pleasure in them . <end>
<start> To day is the festival of the small animals , the most sultry day of the year . <end>
<start> There is a swarming and crawling around us , and we , without intention , but also without reflection , crush here and there a little fly or winged beetle . <end>
<start> If animals do us harm , we strive to annihilate them in every possible way . <end>
<start> The means are often cruel enough , even without our really intending them to be so it is the cruelty of thoughtlessness . <end>
<start> If they are useful , we turn them to advantage , until a more refined wisdom teaches us that certain animals amply reward a different mode of treatment , that of tending and breeding . <end>
<start> Here responsibility first arises . <end>
<start> Torturing is avoided in the case of the domestic animal . <end>
<start> One man is indignant if another is cruel to his cow , quite in accordance with the primitive communal morality , which sees the commonwealth in danger whenever an individual does wrong . <end>
<start> He who perceives any transgression in the community fears indirect harm to himself . <end>
<start> Thus we fear in this case for the quality of meat , agriculture , and means of communication if we see the domestic animals ill treated . <end>
<start> Moreover , he who is harsh to animals awakens a suspicion that he is also harsh to men who are weak , inferior , and incapable of revenge . <end>
<start> He is held to be ignoble and deficient in the finer form of pride . <end>
<start> Thus arises a foundation of moral judgments and sentiments , but the greatest contribution is made by superstition . <end>
<start> Many animals incite men by glances , tones , and gestures to transfer themselves into them in imagination , and some religions teach us , under certain circumstances , to see in animals the dwelling-places of human and divine souls : whence they recommend a nobler caution or even a reverential awe in intercourse with animals . <end>
<start> Even after the disappearance of this superstition the sentiments awakened by it continue to exercise their influence , to ripen and to blossom . <end>
<start> Christianity , as is well known , has shown itself in this respect a poor and retrograde religion . <end>
<start> New Actors . <end>
<start> Among human beings there is no greater banality than death . <end>
<start> Second in order , because it is possible to die without being born , comes birth , and next comes marriage . <end>
<start> But these hackneyed little tragic comedies are always presented , at each of their unnumbered and innumerable performances , by new actors , and accordingly do not cease to find interested spectators : whereas we might well believe that the whole audience of the world theatre had long since hanged themselves to every tree from sheer boredom at these performances . <end>
<start> So much depends on new actors , so little on the piece . <end>
<start> What is Being Obstinate ? <end>
<start> The shortest way is not the straightest possible , but that wherein favourable winds swell our sails . <end>
<start> So says the wisdom of seamen . <end>
<start> Not to follow his course is obstinate , firmness of character being then unadulterated by stupidity . <end>
<start> The Word Vanity . <end>
<start> It is annoying that certain words , with which we moralists positively cannon dispense , involve in themselves a kind of censorship of morals , dating from the times when the most ordinary and natural impulses were denounced . <end>
<start> Thus that fundamental conviction that on the waves of society we either find navigable waters or suffer shipwreck far more through what we appear than through what we are a conviction that must act as guiding principle of all action in relation to society is branded with the general word vanity . <end>
<start> In other words , one of the most weighty and significant of qualities is branded with an expression which denotes it as essentially empty and negative : a great thing is designated by a diminutive , ay , even slandered by the strokes of caricature . <end>
<start> There is no help for it we must use such words , but then we must shut our ears to the insinuations of ancient habits . <end>
<start> The Fatalism of the Turk . <end>
<start> The fatalism of the Turk has this fundamental defect , that it contrasts man and fate as two distinct things . <end>
<start> Man , says this doctrine , may struggle against fate and try to baffle it , but in the end fate will always gain the victory . <end>
<start> Hence the most rational course is to resign oneself or to live as one pleases . <end>
<start> As a matter of fact , every man is himself a piece of fate . <end>
<start> When he thinks that he is struggling against fate in this way , fate is accomplishing its ends even in that struggle . <end>
<start> The combat is a fantasy , but so is the resignation in fate all these fantasies are included in fate . <end>
<start> The fear felt by most people of the doctrine that denies the freedom of the will is a fear of the fatalism of the Turk . <end>
<start> They imagine that man will become weakly resigned and will stand before the future with folded hands , because he cannon alter anything of the future . <end>
<start> Or that he will give a free rein to his caprices , because the predestined cannon be made worse by that course . <end>
<start> The follies of men are as much a piece of fate as are his wise actions , and even that fear of belief in fate is a fatality . <end>
<start> You yourself , you poor timid creature , are that indomitable Moira , which rules even the Gods whatever may happen , you are a curse or a blessing , and in any case the fetters wherein the strongest lies bound : in you the whole future of the human world is predestined , and it is no use for you to be frightened of yourself . <end>
<start> The Advocate of the Devil . <end>
<start> Only by our own suffering do we become wise , only by others suffering do we become good so runs that strange philosophy which derives all morality from pity and all intellectuality from the isolation of the individual . <end>
<start> Herein this philosophy is the unconscious leader for all human deterioration . <end>
<start> For pity needs suffering , and isolation contempt of others . <end>
<start> The Moral Character Masks . <end>
<start> In ages when the character masks of different classes are definitely fixed , like the classes themselves , moralists will be seduced into holding the moral , too , as absolute , and in delineating them accordingly . <end>
<start> Thus Moliere is intelligible as the contemporary of the society of Louis XIV . <end>
<start> in our society of transitions and intermediate stages he would seem an inspired pedant . <end>
<start> The Most Noble Virtue . <end>
<start> In the first era of the higher humanity courage is accounted the most noble virtue , in the next justice , in the third temperance , in the fourth wisdom . <end>
<start> In which era do we live ? <end>
<start> In which do you live ? <end>
<start> A Necessary Preliminary . <end>
<start> A man who will not become master of his irritability , his venomous and vengeful feelings , and his lust , and attempts to become master in anything else , is as stupid as the farmer who lays out his field beside a torrent without guarding against that torrent . <end>
<start> What is Truth ? <end>
<start> Schwarzert Melanchthon : We often preach our faith when we have lost it , and leave not a stone unturned to find it and then we often do not preach worst ! <end>
<start> Luther : Brother , you are really speaking like an angel to day . <end>
<start> Schwarzert . <end>
<start> But that is the idea of your enemies , and they apply it to you . <end>
<start> Luther . <end>
<start> Then it would be a lie from the devil s hind quarters . <end>
<start> The Habit of Contrasts . <end>
<start> Superficial , inexact observation sees contrasts everywhere in nature for instance , hot and cold , where there are no contrasts , only differences of degree . <end>
<start> This bad habit has induced us to try to understand and interpret even the inner nature , the intellectual and moral world , in accordance with such contrasts . <end>
<start> An infinite amount of cruelty , arrogance , harshness , estrangement , and coldness has entered into human emotion , because men imagined they saw contrasts where there were only transitions . <end>
<start> Can We Forgive ? <end>
<start> How can we forgive them at all , if they know not what they do ? <end>
<start> We have nothing to forgive . <end>
<start> But does a man ever fully know what he is doing ? <end>
<start> And if this point at least remains always debatable , men never have anything to forgive each other , and indulgence is for the reasonable man an impossible thing . <end>
<start> Finally , if the evil doers had really known what they did , we should still only have a right to forgive if we had a right to accuse and to punish . <end>
<start> But we have not that right . <end>
<start> Habitual Shame . <end>
<start> Why do we feel shame when some virtue or merit is attributed to us which , as the saying goes , we have not deserved ? <end>
<start> Because we appear to have intruded upon a territory to which we do not belong , from which we should be excluded , as from a holy place or holy of holies , which ought not to be trodden by our foot . <end>
<start> Through the errors of others we have , nevertheless , penetrated to it , and we are now swayed partly by fear , partly by reverence , partly by surprise we do not know whether we ought to fly or to enjoy the blissful moment with all its gracious advantages . <end>
<start> In all shame there is a mystery , which seems desecrated or in danger of desecration through us . <end>
<start> All favour begets shame . <end>
<start> But if it be remembered that we have never really deserved anything , this feeling of shame , provided that we surrender ourselves to this point of view in a spirit of Christian contemplation , becomes habitual , because upon such a one God seems continually to be conferring his blessing and his favours . <end>
<start> Apart from this Christian interpretation , the state of habitual shame will be possible even to the entirely godless sage , who clings firmly to the basic non responsibility and of all action and being . <end>
<start> If he be treated as if he had deserved this or that , he will seem to have won his way into a higher order of beings , who do actually deserve something , who are free and can really bear the burden of responsibility for their own volition and capacity . <end>
<start> Whoever says to him , You have deserved it , appears to cry out to him , You are not a human being , but a God . <end>
<start> The Most Unskilful Teacher . <end>
<start> In one man all his real virtues are implanted on the soil of his spirit of contradiction , in another on his incapacity to say no in other words , on his spirit of acquiescence . <end>
<start> A third has made all his morality grow out of his pride as a solitary , a fourth from his strong social instinct . <end>
<start> Now , supposing that theses of the virtues in these four cases , owing to mischance or unskilful teachers , were not sown on the soil of their nature , which provides them with the richest and most abundant mould , they would become weak , unsatisfactory men devoid of morality . <end>
<start> And who would have been the most unskilful of teachers , the evil genius of these men ? <end>
<start> The moral fanatic , who thinks that the good can only grow out of the good and on the soil of the good . <end>

<start> Divine Missionaries . <end>
<start> Even Socrates feels himself to be a divine missionary , but I am not sure whether we should not here detect a tincture of that Attic irony and fondness for jesting whereby this odious , arrogant conception would be toned down . <end>
<start> He talks of the fact without unction his images of the gadfly and the horse are simple and not sacerdotal . <end>
<start> The real religious task which he has set himself to test God in a hundred ways and see whether he spoke the truth betrays a bold and free attitude , in which the missionary walked by the side of his God . <end>
<start> This testing of God is one of the most subtle compromises between piety and free thinking that has ever been devised . <end>
<start> Nowadays we do not even need this compromise any longer . <end>
<start> Honesty in Painting . <end>
<start> Raphael , who cared a great deal for the Church so far as she could pay him , but , like the best men of his time , cared little for the objects of the Church s belief , did not advance one step to meet the exacting , ecstatic piety of many of his patrons . <end>
<start> He remained honest even in that exceptional picture which was originally intended for a banner in a procession the Sistine Madonna . <end>
<start> Here for once he wished to paint a vision , but such a vision as even noble youths without faith may and will have the vision of the future wife , a wise , high soiled , silent , and very beautiful woman , carrying her first born in her arms . <end>
<start> Let men of an older generation , accustomed to prayer and devotion , find here , like the worthy elder on the left , something superhuman to revere . <end>
<start> We younger men so Raphael seems to call to us are occupied with the beautiful maiden on the right , who says to the spectator of the picture , with her challenging and by no means devout look , The mother and her child is not that a pleasant , inviting sight ? <end>
<start> The face and the look are reflected in the joy in the faces of the beholder . <end>
<start> The artist who devised all this enjoys himself in this way , and adds his own delight to the delight of the art lover . <end>
<start> As regards the messianic expression in the face of the child , Raphael , honest man , who would not paint any state of soul in which he did not believe , has amiably cheated his religious admirers . <end>
<start> He painted that freak of nature which is very often found , the man s eye in the child s face , and that , too , the eye of a brave , helpful man who sees distress . <end>
<start> This eye should be accompanied by a beard . <end>
<start> The fact that a beard is wanting , and that two different ages are seen in one countenance , is the pleasing paradox which believers have interpreted in accordance with their faith in miracles . <end>
<start> The artist could only expect as much from their art of exposition and interpretation . <end>
<start> Prayer . <end>
<start> On two hypotheses alone is there any sense in prayer , that not quite extinct custom of olden times . <end>
<start> It would have to be possible either to fix or alter the will of the godhead , and the devotee would have to know best himself what he needs and should really desire . <end>
<start> Both hypotheses , axiomatic and traditional in all other religions , are denied by Christianity . <end>
<start> If Christianity nevertheless maintained prayer side by side with its belief in the all wise and all provident divine reason a belief that makes prayer really senseless and even blasphemous , it showed here once more its admirable wisdom of the serpent . <end>
<start> For an outspoken command , Thou shalt not pray , would have led Christians by way of boredom to the denial of Christianity . <end>
<start> In the Christian ora et labor ora plays the role of pleasure . <end>
<start> Without ora what could those unlucky saints who renounced labor have done ? <end>
<start> But to have a chat with God , to ask him for all kinds of pleasant things , to feel a slight amusement at one s own folly in still having any wishes at all , in spite of so excellent a father all that was an admirable invention for saints . <end>
<start> A HOLY Lie . <end>
<start> The lie that was on aria s lips when she died pete , non dole obscures all the truths that have ever been uttered by the dying . <end>
<start> It is the only holy lie that has become famous , whereas elsewhere the odour of sanctity has clung only to errors . <end>
<start> The Most Necessary Apostle . <end>
<start> Among twelve apostles one must always be hard as stone , in order that upon him the new church may be built . <end>
<start> The wife of the Stoic phrase pactus , when their complicity in the great conspiracy of a . <end>
<start> d . <end>
<start> against Nero was discovered , is reported to have said as she committed suicide , It does t hurt , pactus . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> Which is more Transitory , the Body or the Spirit ? <end>
<start> In legal , moral , and religious institutions the external and concrete elements in other words , rites , gestures , and ceremonies are the most permanent . <end>
<start> They are the body to which a new spirit is constantly being superadded . <end>
<start> The cult , like an unchangeable text , is ever interpreted anew . <end>
<start> Concepts and emotions are fluid , customs are solid . <end>
<start> The Belief in Disease qua Disease . <end>
<start> Christianity first painted the devil on the wall of the world . <end>
<start> Christianity first brought the idea of sin into the world . <end>
<start> The belief in the remedies , which is offered as an antidote , has gradually been shaken to its very foundations . <end>
<start> But the belief in the disease , which Christianity has taught and propagated , still exists . <end>
<start> Speech and Writings of Religious Men . <end>
<start> If the priest s style and general expression , both in speaking and writing , do not clearly betray the religious man , we need no longer take his views upon religion and his pleading for religion seriously . <end>
<start> These opinions have become powerless for him if , judging by his style , he has at command irony , arrogance , malice , hatred , and all the changing eddies of mood , just like the most irreligious of men how far more powerless will they be for his hearers and readers ! <end>
<start> In short , he will serve to make the latter still more irreligious . <end>
<start> The Danger in Personality . <end>
<start> The more God has been regarded as a personality in himself , the less loyal have we been to him . <end>
<start> Men are far more attached to their thought images than to their best beloved . <end>
<start> That is why they sacrifice themselves for State , Church , and even for God so far as he remains their creation , their thought , and is not too much looked upon as a personality . <end>
<start> In the latter case they almost always quarrel with him . <end>
<start> After all , it was the most pious of men who let slip that bitter cry : My God , why hast thou forsaken me ? <end>
<start> Worldly Justice . <end>
<start> It is possible to unhinge worldly justice with the doctrine of the complete non responsibility and innocence of every man . <end>
<start> An attempt has been made in the same direction on the basis of the opposite doctrine of the full responsibility and guilt of every man . <end>
<start> It was the founder of Christianity who wished to abolish worldly justice and banish judgment and punishment from the world . <end>
<start> For he understood all guilt as sin that is , an outrage against God and not against the world . <end>
<start> On the other hand , he considered every man in a broad sense , and almost in every sense , a sinner . <end>
<start> The guilty , however , are not to be the judges of their peers so his rules of equity decided . <end>
<start> Thus all dispensers of worldly justice were in his eyes as culpable as those they condemned , and their air of appeared to him hypocritical and pharisaic . <end>
<start> Moreover , he looked to the motives and not to the results of actions , and thought that only one was keen sighted enough to give a verdict on motives himself or , as he expressed it , God . <end>
<start> An Affectation in Parting . <end>
<start> He who wishes to sever his connection with a party or a creed thinks it necessary for him to refute it . <end>
<start> This is a most arrogant notion . <end>
<start> The only thing necessary is that he should clearly see what tentacles hitherto held him to this party or creed and no longer hold him , what views impelled him to it and now impel him in some other directions . <end>
<start> We have not joined the party or creed on strict grounds of knowledge . <end>
<start> We should not affect this attitude on parting from it either . <end>
<start> Saviour and Physician . <end>
<start> In his knowledge of the human soul the founder of Christianity was , as is natural , not without many great deficiencies and prejudices , and , as physician of the soul , was addicted to that disreputable , local belief in a universal medicine . <end>
<start> In his methods he sometimes resembles that dentist who wishes to heal all pain by extracting the tooth . <end>
<start> Thus , for example , he assail sensuality with the advice : If thine eye offend thee , pluck it out . <end>
<start> Yet there still remains the distinction that the dentist at least attains his object pointlessness for the patient although in so clumsy a fashion that he becomes ridiculous whereas the Christian who follows that advice and thinks he has killed his sensuality , is wrong , for his sensuality still lives in an uncanny , vampire form , and torments him in hideous disguises . <end>
<start> Prisoners . <end>
<start> One morning the prisoners entered the yard for work , but the warder was not there . <end>
<start> Some , as their manner was , set to work at once others stood idle and gazed defiantly around . <end>
<start> Then one of them strode forward and cried , Work as much as you will or do nothing , it all comes to the same . <end>
<start> Your secret machinations have come to light the warder has been keeping his eye on you of late , and will cause a terrible judgment to be passed upon you in a few days time . <end>
<start> You know him he is of a cruel and resentful disposition . <end>
<start> But now , listen : you have mistaken me hitherto . <end>
<start> I am not what I seem , but far more I am the son of the warder , and can get anything I like out of him . <end>
<start> I can save you nay , I will save you . <end>
<start> But remember this : I will only save those of you who believe that I am the son of the prison warder . <end>
<start> The rest may reap the fruits of their unbelief . <end>
<start> Well , said an old prisoner after an interval of silence , what can it matter to you whether we believe you or not ? <end>
<start> If you are really the son , and can do what you say , then put in a good word for us all . <end>
<start> That would be a real kindness on your part . <end>
<start> But have done with all talk of belief and unbelief ! <end>
<start> What is more , cried a younger man , I don t believe him : he has only got a bee in his bonnet . <end>
<start> I ll wager that in a week s time we shall find ourselves in the same place as we are to day , and the warder will know nothing . <end>
<start> And if the warder ever knew anything , he knows it no longer , said the last of the prisoners , coming down into the yard at that moment , for he has just died suddenly . <end>
<start> Ah ha ! <end>
<start> cried several in confusion , ah ha ! <end>
<start> Sir Son , Sir Son , how stands it now with your title ? <end>
<start> Are we by any chance your prisoners now ? <end>
<start> I told you , answered the man gently , I will set free all who believe in me , as surely as my father still lives . <end>
<start> The prisoners did not laugh , but shrugged their shoulders and left him to himself . <end>
<start> The Persecutors of God . <end>
<start> Paul conceived and Calvin followed up the idea that countless creatures have been predestined to damnation from time immemorial , and that this fair world was made in order that the glory of God might be manifested therein . <end>
<start> So heaven and hell and mankind merely exist to satisfy the vanity of God ! <end>
<start> What cruel , insatiable vanity must have smouldered in the soul of the first or second thinker of such a thought ! <end>
<start> Paul , then , after all , remained Saul the persecutor of God . <end>
<start> Socrates . <end>
<start> If all goes well , the time will come when , in order to advance themselves on the path of moral reason , men will rather take up the Memorabilia of Socrates than the Bible , and when Montaigne and Horace will be used as pioneers and guides for the understanding of Socrates , the simplest and most enduring of interpretative sages . <end>
<start> In him converge the roads of the most different philosophic modes of life , which are in truth the modes of the different temperaments , crystallised by reason and habit and all ultimately directed towards the delight in life and in self . <end>
<start> The apparent conclusion is that the most peculiar thing about Socrates was his share in all the temperaments . <end>
<start> Socrates excels the founder of Christianity by virtue of his merry style of seriousness and by that wisdom of sheer roguish pranks which constitutes the best state of soul in a man . <end>
<start> Moreover , he had a superior intelligence . <end>
<start> Learning to Write Well . <end>
<start> The age of good speaking is over , because the age of city state culture is over . <end>
<start> The limit allowed by Aristotle to the great city in which the town crier must be able to make himself heard by the whole assembled community troubles us as little as do any , us who even wish to be understood beyond the boundaries of nations . <end>
<start> Therefore every one who is of a good European turn of mind must learn to write well , and to write better and better . <end>
<start> He cannon help himself , he must learn that : even if he was born in Germany , where bad writing is looked upon as a national privilege . <end>
<start> Better writing means better thinking always to discover matter more worthy of communication to be able to communicate it properly to be translatable into the tongues of neighbouring nations to make oneself comprehensible to foreigners who learn our language to work with the view of making all that is good common property , and of giving free access everywhere to the free finally , to pave the way for that still remote state of things , when the great task shall come for good Europeans guidance and guardianship of the universal . <end>
<start> Whoever preaches the opposite doctrine of not troubling about good writing and good reading both virtues grow together and decline together is really showing the peoples a way of becoming more and more national . <end>
<start> He is intensifying the malady of this century , and is a foe to good Europeans , a foe to free spirits . <end>
<start> The Theory of the Best Style . <end>
<start> The theory of the best style may at one time be the theory of finding the expression by which we transfer every mood of ours to the reader and the listener . <end>
<start> At another , it may be the theory of finding expressions for the more desirable human moods , the communication and transference of which one desires most for the mood of a man moved from the depth of his heart , intellectually cheerful , bright , and sincere , who has conquered his passions . <end>
<start> This will be the theory of the best style , a theory that corresponds to the good man . <end>
<start> Paying Attention to Movement . <end>
<start> The movement of the sentences shows whether the author be tired . <end>
<start> Individual expressions may nevertheless be still strong and good , because they were invented earlier and for their own sake , when the thought first flashed across the author s mind . <end>
<start> This is frequently the case with Goethe , who too often dictated when he was tired . <end>
<start> Already and Still . <end>
<start> A . <end>
<start> German prose is still very young . <end>
<start> Goethe declares that Wieland is its father . <end>
<start> C . <end>
<start> But , so far as I am aware , Bishop fulfils already wrote German prose , which must therefore be fifteen hundred years old . <end>
<start> Original German . <end>
<start> German prose , which is really not fashioned on any pattern and must be considered an original creation of German taste , should give the eager advocate of a future original German culture an indication of how real German dress , German society , German furniture , German meals would look without the imitation of models . <end>
<start> Some one who had long reflected on these vistas finally cried in great horror , But , Heaven help us , perhaps we already have that original culture only we don t like to talk about it ! <end>
<start> THE WANDERER AND HIS SHADOW . <end>
<start> Forbidden Books . <end>
<start> One should never read anything written by those arrogant and puzzle brains who have the detestable vice of logical paradox . <end>
<start> They apply logical formulae just where everything is really improvised at random and built in the air . <end>
<start> Therefore with them means , You idiot of a reader , this therefore does not exist for you , but only for me . <end>
<start> The answer to this is : You idiot of a writer , then why do you write ? <end>
<start> Displaying One s Wit . <end>
<start> Every one who wishes to display his wit thereby proclaims that he has also a plentiful lack of wife That vice which clever Frenchmen have of adding a touch to their best ideas arises from a desire to be considered richer than they really are . <end>
<start> They wish to be carelessly generous , as if weary of continual spending from overfull treasuries . <end>
<start> French and German Literature . <end>
<start> The misfortune of the French and German literature of the last hundred years is that the Germans ran away too early from the French school , and the French , later on , went too early to the German school . <end>
<start> OUR PROSE . <end>
<start> None of the present day cultured nations has so bad a prose as the German . <end>
<start> WhenIclever , blast Frenchmen say , There is no German prose , we ought really not to be angry , for this criticism is more polite than we deserve . <end>
<start> If we look for reasons , we come at last to the strange phenomenon that the German knows only improvised prose and has no conception of any other . <end>
<start> He simply cannon understand the Italian , who says that prose is as much harder than poetry as the representation of naked beauty is harder to the sculptor than that of draped beauty . <end>
<start> Verse , images , rhythm , and rhyme need honest effort that even the German realises , and he is not inclined to set a very high value on extempore poetry . <end>
<start> But the notion of working at a page of prose as at a statue sounds to him like a tale from fairyland . <end>
<start> The Grand Style . <end>
<start> The grand style comes into being when the beautiful wins a victory over the monstrous . <end>
<start> DODGING . <end>
<start> We do not realise , in the case of distinguished minds , wherein lies the excellence of their expression , their turn of phrase , until we can say what word every mediocre writer would inevitably have hit upon in expressing the same idea . <end>
<start> All great artists , in steering their car , show themselves prone to dodge and leave the track , but never to fall over . <end>
<start> Something like Bread . <end>
<start> Bread neutralises and takes out the taste of other food , and is there fore necessary to every long meal . <end>
<start> In all works of art there must be something like bread , in order that they may produce divers effects . <end>
<start> If these effects followed one another without occasional pauses and intervals , they would soon make us weary and provoke disgust in fact , a long meal of art would then be impossible . <end>
<start> Jean Paul . <end>
<start> Jean Paul knew a great deal , but had no science understood all manner of tricks of art , but had no art found almost everything enjoyable , but had no taste possessed feeling and seriousness , but in dispensing them poured over them a nauseous sauce of tears had even wit , but , unfortunately for his ardent desire for it , far too little whence he drives the reader to despair by his very lack of wit . <end>
<start> In short , he was the bright , rank : smelling weed that shot up overnight in the fair pleasance of Schiller and Goethe . <end>
<start> He was a good , comfortable man , and yet a destiny , a destiny in a dressing gown . <end>
<start> Palate for Opposites . <end>
<start> In order to enjoy a work of the past as its contemporaries enjoyed it , one must have a palate for the prevailing taste of the age which it attacked . <end>
<start> It is interesting to compare this judgment with Carlyle s praise of Jean Paul . <end>
<start> The dressing gown is an allusion to Jean Paul s favourite costume . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> Spirits OF Wine Authors . <end>
<start> Many writers are neither spirit nor wine , but spirits of wine . <end>
<start> They can flare up , and then they give warmth . <end>
<start> The Interpretative Sense . <end>
<start> The sense of taste , as the true interpretative sense , often talks the other senses over to its point of view and imposes upon them its laws and customs . <end>
<start> At table one can receive disclosures about the most subtle secrets of the arts it suffices to observe what tastes good and when and after what and how long it tastes good . <end>
<start> LESSING . <end>
<start> Lessing had a genuine French talent , and , as writer , went most assiduously to the French school . <end>
<start> He knows well how to arrange and display his wares in his shop window . <end>
<start> Without this true art his thoughts , like the objects of them , would have remained rather in the dark , nor would the general loss be great . <end>
<start> His art , however , has taught many especially the last generation of German scholars and has given enjoyment to a countless number . <end>
<start> It is true his disciples had no need to learn from him , as they often did , his unpleasant tone with its mingling of petulance and candour . <end>
<start> Opinion is now unanimous on Lessing as lyric poet , and will some day be unanimous on Lessing as dramatic poet . <end>
<start> Undesirable Readers . <end>
<start> How an author is vexed by those stolid , awkward readers who always fall at every place where they stumble , and always hurt themselves when they fall ! <end>
<start> Poets Thoughts . <end>
<start> Real thoughts of real poets always go about with a veil on , like Egyptian women only the deep eye of thought looks out freely through the veil . <end>
<start> Poets thoughts are as a rule not of such value as is supposed . <end>
<start> We have to pay for the veil and for our own curiosity into the bargain . <end>
<start> Write Simply and Usefully . <end>
<start> Transitions , details , colour in depicting the passions we make a present of all these to the author because we bring them with us and set them down to the credit of his book , provided he makes us some compensation . <end>
<start> leland . <end>
<start> Wieland wrote German better than any one else , and had the genuine inadequacies and inadequacies of the master . <end>
<start> His translations of the letters of Cicero and Lucian are the best in the language . <end>
<start> His ideas , however , add nothing to our store of thought . <end>
<start> We can endure his cheerful moralities as little as his cheerful immoralities , for both are very closely connected . <end>
<start> The men who enjoyed them were at bottom better men than we are , but also a good deal heavier . <end>
<start> They needed an author of this sort . <end>
<start> The Germans did not need Goethe , and therefore cannon make proper use of him . <end>
<start> We have only to consider the best of our statesmen and artists in this light . <end>
<start> None of them had or could have had Goethe as their teacher . <end>
<start> Rare Festivals . <end>
<start> Pithyconciseness , repose , and maturity where you find these qualities in an author , cry halt and celebrate a great festival in the desert . <end>
<start> It will be long before you have such a treat again . <end>
<start> The Treasure of German Prose . <end>
<start> Apart from Goethe s writings and especially Goethe s conversations with ackermann the best German book in existence , what German prose literature remains that is worth reading over and over again ? <end>
<start> Lichtenberg s Aphorisms , the first book of Jung Stilling s Story of My Life , Adalbert stiffer s St . <end>
<start> Martin s Summer and Gottfried Keller s People of Seldwyla and there , for the time being , it comes to an end . <end>
<start> Literary and Colloquial Style . <end>
<start> The art of writing demands , first and foremost , substitutions for the means of expression which speech alone possesses in other words , for gestures , accent , intonation , and look . <end>
<start> Hence literary style is quite different from colloquial style , and far more difficult , because it has to make itself as intelligible as the latter with fewer accessories . <end>
<start> Demosthenes delivered his speeches differently from what we read he worked them up for reading purposes . <end>
<start> Cicero s speeches ought to be with the same object , for at present they contain more of the Roman Forum than we can endure . <end>
<start> Caution in Quotation . <end>
<start> Young authors do not know that a good expression or idea only looks well among its peers that an excellent quotation may spoil whole pages , nay the whole book for it seems to cry warningly to the reader , Mark you , I am the precious stone , and round about me is lead pale , worthless lead ! <end>
<start> Every word , every idea only desires to live in its own company that is the moral of a choice style . <end>
<start> How should Errors be Enunciated ? <end>
<start> We may dispute whether it be more injurious for errors to be enunciated badly or as well as the best truths . <end>
<start> It is certain that in the former case they are doubly harmful to the brain and are less easily removed from it . <end>
<start> But , on the other hand , they are not so certain of effect as in the latter case . <end>
<start> They are , in fact , less contagious . <end>
<start> Limiting and Widening . <end>
<start> Homer limited and diminished the horizon of his subject , but allowed individual scenes to expand and blossom out . <end>
<start> Later , the tragedians are constantly renewing this process . <end>
<start> Each takes his material in ever smaller and smaller fragments than his predecessor did , but each attains a greater wealth of blooms within the narrow hedges of these sequestered garden enclosures . <end>
<start> Literature and Morality Mutually Explanatory . <end>
<start> We can show from Greek literature by what forces the Greek spirit developed , how it entered upon different channels , and where it became enfeebled . <end>
<start> All this also depicts to us how Greek morality proceeded , and how all morality will proceed : how it was at first a constraint and displayed cruelty , then became gradually milder how a pleasure in certain actions , in certain forms and conventions arose , and from this again a propensity for solitary exercise , for solitary possession how the track becomes crowded and overcrowded with competitors how satiety enters in , new objects of struggle and ambition are sought , and forgotten aims are awakened to life how the drama is repeated , and the spectators become altogether weary of looking on , because the whole gamut seems to have been run through and then comes a stoppage , an expiration , and the rivulets are lost in the sand . <end>
<start> The end , or at any rate an end , has come . <end>
<start> What Landscapes give Permanent Delight . <end>
<start> Such and such a landscape has features eminently suited for painting , but I cannon find the formula for it it remains beyond my grasp as a whole . <end>
<start> I notice that all landscapes which please me permanently have a simple geometrical scheme of lines underneath all their complexity . <end>
<start> Without such a mathematical substratum no scenery becomes artistically pleasing . <end>
<start> Perhaps this rule may be applied symbolically to human beings . <end>
<start> Reading Aloud . <end>
<start> The ability to read aloud involves of necessity the ability to declaim . <end>
<start> Everywhere we must apply pale tints , but we must determine the degree of pallor in close relation to the richly and deeply coloured background , that always hovers before our eyes and acts as our guide in other words , in accordance with the way in which we should declaim the same passages . <end>
<start> That is why we must be able to declaim . <end>
<start> The Dramatic Sense . <end>
<start> He who has not the four subtler senses of art tries to understand everything with the fifth sense , which is the coarsest of all the dramatic sense . <end>
<start> Herder . <end>
<start> Herder fails to be all that he made people think he was and himself wished to think he was . <end>
<start> He was no great thinker or discoverer , no newly fertile soil with the exhausted strength of a virgin forest . <end>
<start> But he possessed in the highest degree the power of scenting the future , he saw and picked the first fruits of the seasons earlier than all others , and they then believed that he had made them grow . <end>
<start> Between darkness and light , youth and age , his mind was like a hunter on the watch , looking everywhere for transitions , depressions , convulsions , the outward and visible signs of internal growth . <end>
<start> The unrest of spring drove him to and fro , but he was himself not the spring . <end>
<start> At times , indeed , he had some inkling of this , and yet would fain not have believed it he , the ambitious priest , who would have so gladly been the intellectual pope of his epoch ! <end>
<start> This is his despair . <end>
<start> He seems to have lived long as a pretender to several kingdoms or even to a universal monarchy . <end>
<start> He had his following which believed in him , among others the young Goethe . <end>
<start> But whenever crowns were really distributed , he was passed over . <end>
<start> Kant , Goethe , and then the first true German historians and scholars robbed him of what he thought he had reserved for himself although in silence and secret he often thought the reverse . <end>
<start> Just when he doubted in himself , he gladly clothed himself in dignity and enthusiasm : these were often in him mere garments , which had to hide a great deal and also to deceive and comfort him . <end>
<start> He really had fire and enthusiasm , but his ambition was far greater ! <end>
<start> It blew impatiently at the fire , which flickered , crackled , and smoked his style flickers , crackles , and smokes but he yearned for the great flame which never broke out . <end>
<start> He did not sit at the table of the genuine creators , and his ambition did not admit of his sitting modestly among those who simply enjoy . <end>
<start> Thus he was a restless spirit , the taster of all intellectual dishes , which were collected by the Germans from every quarter and every age in the course of half a century . <end>
<start> Never really happy and satisfied , Herder was also too often ill , and then at times envy sat by his bed , and hypocrisy paid her visit as well . <end>
<start> He always had an air of being scarred and crippled , and he lacked simple , stalwart manliness more completely than any of the so called classical writers . <end>
<start> Scent of Words . <end>
<start> Every word has its scent there is a harmony and discord of scents , and so too of words . <end>
<start> The Far Fetched Style . <end>
<start> The natural style is an offence to the lover of the far fetched style . <end>
<start> A Vow . <end>
<start> I will never again read an author of whom one can suspect that he wanted to make a book , but only those writers whose thoughts unexpectedly became a book . <end>
<start> The Artistic Convention . <end>
<start> Three fourths of Homer is convention , and the same is the case with all the Greek artists , who had no reason for falling into the modern craze for originality . <end>
<start> They had no fear of convention , for after all convention was a link between them and their public . <end>
<start> Conventions are the artistic means acquired for the understanding of the hearer the common speech , learnt with much toil , whereby the artist can really communicate his ideas . <end>
<start> All the more when he wishes , like the Greek poets and musicians , to conquer at once with each of his works since he is accustomed to compete publicly with one or two rivals , the first condition is that he must be understood at once , and this is only possible by means of convention . <end>
<start> What the artist devises beyond convention he offers of his own free will and takes a risk , his success at best resulting in the setting up of a new convention . <end>
<start> As a rule originality is marvelled at , sometimes even worshipped , but seldom understood . <end>
<start> A stubborn avoidance of convention means a desire not to be understood . <end>
<start> What , then , is the object of the modern craze for originality ? <end>
<start> Artists Affectation of Scientific Method . <end>
<start> Schiller , like other German artists , fancied that if a man had intellect he was entitled to improvise even with the pen on all difficult subjects . <end>
<start> So there we see his prose essays in every way a model of how not to attack scientific questions of aesthetics and ethics , and a danger for young readers who , in their admiration for Schiller the poet , have not the courage to think meanly of Schiller the thinker and author . <end>
<start> The temptation to traverse for once the forbidden paths , and to have his say in science as well , is easy and pardonable in the artist . <end>
<start> For even the ablest artist from time to time finds his handicraft and his workshop unendurable . <end>
<start> This temptation is so strong that it makes the artist show all the world what no one wishes to see , that his little chamber of thought is cramped and untidy . <end>
<start> Why not , indeed ? <end>
<start> He does not live there . <end>
<start> He proceeds to show that the storeroom of his knowledge is partly empty , partly filled with lumber . <end>
<start> Why not , indeed ? <end>
<start> This condition does not really become the artist child badly . <end>
<start> In particular , the artist shows that for the very easiest exercises of scientific method , which are accessible even to beginners , his joints are too stiff and untrained . <end>
<start> Even of that he need not really be ashamed ! <end>
<start> On the other hand , he often develops no mean art in imitating all the mistakes , vices , and base pantries that are practised in the scientific community , in the belief that these belong to the appearance of the thing , if not to the thing itself . <end>
<start> This is the very point that is so amusing in artists writing , that the artist involuntarily acts as his vocation demands : he parodies the scientific and inartistic natures . <end>
<start> Towards science he should show no attitude but that of parody , in so far as he is an artist and only an artist . <end>
<start> The Faust Idea . <end>
<start> A little temptress is seduced and plunged into despair : a great scholar of all the four Faculties is the evil doer . <end>
<start> That cannon have happened in the ordinary course , surely ? <end>
<start> No , certainly not ! <end>
<start> Without the aid of the devil incarnate , the great scholar would never have achieved the deed . <end>
<start> Is this really destined to be the greatest German tragic idea , as one hears it said among Germans ? <end>
<start> But for Goethe even this idea was too terrible . <end>
<start> His kind heart could not avoid placing the little temptress , the good soul that but once , near to the saints , after her involuntary death . <end>
<start> Even the great scholar , the good man with the dark impulse , is brought into heaven in the nick of time , by a trick which is played upon the devil at the decisive moment . <end>
<start> In heaven the lovers find themselves again . <end>
<start> Goethe once said that his nature was too conciliatory for really tragic subjects . <end>
<start> Are there German Classics ? <end>
<start> SainteBeuve observes somewhere that the word classic does not suit the genius of certain literatures . <end>
<start> For instance , nobody could talk seriously of German classics . <end>
<start> What do our German publishers , who are about to add fifty more to the fifty German classics we are told to accept , say to that ? <end>
<start> Does it not almost seem as if one need only have been dead for the last thirty years , and lie a lawful prey to the public , in order to hear suddenly and unexpectedly the trumpet of resurrection as a Classic ? <end>
<start> And this in an age and a nation where at least five out of the six great fathers of its literature are undoubtedly antiquated without there being any need for the age or the nation to be ashamed of this . <end>
<start> For those writers have given way before the strength of our time let that be considered in all fairness ! <end>
<start> Goethe , as I have indicated , I do not include . <end>
<start> He belongs to a higher species than national literatures : hence life , revival . <end>
<start> The German copyright expires thirty years after publication . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> and decay do not enter into the reckoning in his relations with his countrymen . <end>
<start> He lived and now lives but for the few for the majority he is nothing but a flourish of vanity which is trumpeted from time to time across the border into foreign ears . <end>
<start> Goethe , not merely a great and good man , but a culture , is in German history an interlude without a sequel . <end>
<start> Who , for instance , would be able to point to any trace of Goethe s influence in German politics of the last seventy years whereas the influence , certainly of Schiller , and perhaps of Lessing , can be traced in the political world ? <end>
<start> But what of those five others ? <end>
<start> lostock , in a most honourable way , became out of date even in his own lifetime , and so completely that the meditative book of his later years , The Republic of Learning , has never been taken seriously from that day to this . <end>
<start> Herder s misfortune was that his writings were always either new or antiquated . <end>
<start> Thus for stronger and more subtle minds like Lichtenberg even Herder s masterpiece , his Ideas for the History of Mankind , was in a way antiquated at the very moment of its appearance . <end>
<start> Wieland , who lived to the full and made others live likewise , was clever enough to anticipate by death the waning of his influence . <end>
<start> Lessing , perhaps , still lives to day but among a young and ever younger band of scholars . <end>
<start> Schiller has fallen from the hands of young men into those of boys , of all German boys . <end>
<start> It is a well known sign of obsolescence when a book descends to people of less and less mature age . <end>
<start> Well , what is it that has thrust these five into the background , so that well educated men of affairs no longer read them ? <end>
<start> A better taste , a riper knowledge , a higher reverence for the real and the true : in other words , the very virtues which these five and ten or twenty others of lesser repute first re planted in Germany , and which now , like a mighty forest , cast over their graves not only the shadow of awe , but something of the shadow of oblivion . <end>
<start> But classical writers are not planters of intellectual and literary virtues . <end>
<start> They bring those virtues to perfection and are their highest luminous peaks , and being brighter , freer , and purer than all that surrounds them , they remain shining above the nations when the nations themselves perish . <end>
<start> There may come an elevated stage of humanity , in which the Europe of the peoples is a dark , forgotten thing , but Europe lives on in thirty books , very old but never antiquated in the classics . <end>
<start> Interesting , but not Beautiful . <end>
<start> This countryside conceals its meaning , but it has one that we should like to guess . <end>
<start> Everywhere that I look , I read words and hints of words , but I do not know where begins the sentence that solves the riddle of all these hints . <end>
<start> So I get a stiff neck in trying to discover whether I should start reading from this or that point . <end>
<start> Against Innovators in Language . <end>
<start> The use of neologisms or archaism , the preference for the rare and the bizarre , the attempt to enrich rather than to limit the vocabulary , are always signs either of an immature or of a corrupted taste . <end>
<start> A noble poverty but a masterly freedom within the limits of that modest wealth distinguishes the Greek artists in oratory . <end>
<start> They wish to have less than the people has for the people is richest in old and new but they wish to have that little better . <end>
<start> The reckoning up of their archaic and exotic forms is soon done , but we never cease marvelling if we have an eye for their light and delicate manner in handling the commonplace and apparently elements in word and phrase . <end>
<start> Gloomy and Serious Authors . <end>
<start> He who commits his sufferings to paper becomes a gloomy author , but he becomes a serious one if he tells us what he has suffered and why he is now enjoying a pleasurable repose . <end>
<start> Healthiness of Taste . <end>
<start> How is it that health is less contagious than disease generally , and particularly in matters of taste ? <end>
<start> Or are there epidemics of health ? <end>
<start> A Resolution . <end>
<start> Never again to read a book that is born and christened with ink at the same moment . <end>
<start> Improving our Ideas . <end>
<start> Improving our style means improving our ideas , and nothing else . <end>
<start> He who does not at once concede this can never be convinced of the point . <end>
<start> Classical Books . <end>
<start> The weakest point in every classical book is that it is written too much in the mother tongue of its author . <end>
<start> BAD BOOKS . <end>
<start> The book should demand pen , ink , and desk , but usually it is pen , ink , and desk that demand the book . <end>
<start> That is why books are of so little account at present . <end>
<start> Presence of Sense . <end>
<start> When the public reflects on paintings , it becomes a poet when on poems , an investigator . <end>
<start> At the moment when the artist summons it it is always lacking in the right sense , and accordingly in presence of sense , not in presence of mind . <end>
<start> Choice Ideas . <end>
<start> The choice style of a momentous period does not only select its words but its ideas and both from the customary and prevailing usage . <end>
<start> Venturesome ideas , that smell too fresh , are to the maturer taste no less repugnant than new and reckless images and phrases . <end>
<start> Later on both choice ideas and choice words soon smack of mediocrity , because the scent of the choice vanishes quickly , and then nothing but the customary and commonplace element is tasted . <end>
<start> THE WANDERER AND HIS SHADOW . <end>
<start> Main Reason for Corruption of Style . <end>
<start> The desire to display more sentiment than one really feels for a thing corrupts style , in language and in all art . <end>
<start> All great art shows rather the opposite tendency . <end>
<start> Like every man of moral significance , it loves to check emotion on its way and not let it run its course to the very end . <end>
<start> This modesty of letting emotion but half appear is most charly to be observed , for example , in Sophocles . <end>
<start> The features of sentiment seem to become men sentiment reigns to be more shy than it really is . <end>
<start> An Excuse for the Heavy Style . <end>
<start> The lightly uttered phrase seldom falls on the ear with the full weight of the subject . <end>
<start> This is , however , due to the bad training of the ear , which by education must pass from what has hitherto been called music to the school of the higher harmony in other words , to conversation . <end>
<start> Bird s Eye Views . <end>
<start> Here torrents rush from every side into a ravine : their movement is so swift and stormy , and carries the eye along so quickly , that the bare or wooded mountain slopes around seem not to sink down but to fly down . <end>
<start> We are in an agonised tension at the sight , as if behind all this were hidden some hostile element , before which all must fly , and against which the abyss alone gave protection . <end>
<start> This landscape cannon be painted , un less we hover above it like a bird in the open air . <end>
<start> Here for once the so called bird s eye view is not an artistic caprice , but the sole possibility . <end>
<start> Rash Comparisons . <end>
<start> If rash comparisons are not proofs of the wantonness of the writer , they are proofs of the exhaustion of his imagination . <end>
<start> In any case they bear witness to his bad taste . <end>
<start> Dancing in Chains . <end>
<start> In the case of ever Greek artist , poet , or writer we must ask : What is the new constraint which he imposes upon himself and makes attractive to his contemporaries , so as to find imitators ? <end>
<start> For the thing called invention in metre , for example is always a self imposed fetter of this kind . <end>
<start> Dancing in chains to make that hard for themselves and then to spread a false notion that it is easy that is the trick that they wish to show us . <end>
<start> Even in Homer we may perceive a wealth of inherited formulae and laws of epic narration , within the circle of which he had to dance , and he himself created new conventions for them that came after . <end>
<start> This was the discipline of the Greek poets : first to impose upon themselves a manifold constraint by means of the earlier poets then to invent in addition a new constraint , to impose it upon themselves and cheerfully to overcome it , so that constraint and victory are perceived and admired . <end>
<start> THE WANDERER AND HIS SHADOW . <end>
<start> Authors Copiousness . <end>
<start> The last quality that a good author acquires is : whoever has it to begin with will never become a good author . <end>
<start> The noblest racehorses are lean until they are permitted to rest from their victories . <end>
<start> Wheezing Heroes . <end>
<start> Poets and artists who suffer from a narrow chest of the emotions generally make their heroes wheeze . <end>
<start> They do not know what easy breathing means . <end>
<start> The Short Sighted . <end>
<start> The short sighted are the deadly foes of all authors who let themselves go . <end>
<start> These authors should know the wrath with which these people shut the book in which they observe that its creator needs fifty pages to express five ideas . <end>
<start> And the cause of their wrath is that they have endangered what remains of their vision almost without compensation . <end>
<start> A short sighted person said , All authors let themselves go . <end>
<start> Even the HolyGhost ? <end>
<start> Even the Holy Ghost . <end>
<start> But he had a right to , for he wrote for those who had lost their sight altogether . <end>
<start> The Style of Immortality . <end>
<start> Thucydides and Tacitus both imagined immortal , life for their works when they executed them . <end>
<start> That might be guessed if not known otherwise from their style . <end>
<start> The one thought to give permanence to his ideas by salting them , the other by boiling them down and neither , it seems , made a miscalculation . <end>
<start> Against Images and Similes . <end>
<start> By images and similes we convince , but we do not prove . <end>
<start> That is why science has such a horror of images and similes . <end>
<start> Science does not want to convince or make plausible , and rather seeks to provoke cold distrust by its mode of expression , by the bareness of its walls . <end>
<start> For distrust is the touchstone for the gold of certainty . <end>
<start> CAUTION . <end>
<start> In Germany , he who lacks thorough knowledge should beware of writing . <end>
<start> The good German does not say in that case he is ignorant , but he is of doubtful character . <end>
<start> This hasty conclusion , by the way , does great credit to the Germans . <end>
<start> Painted Skeletons . <end>
<start> Painted skeletons are those authors who try to make up for their want of flesh by artistic colourings . <end>
<start> The Grand Style and Something Better . <end>
<start> It is easier to learn how to write the grand style than how to write easily and simply . <end>
<start> The reasons for this are inextricably bound up with morality . <end>

<start> Sebastian Bach . <end>
<start> In so far as we do not hear Bach s music as perfect and experienced connoisseurs of counterpoint and all the varieties of style and accordingly must dispense with real artistic enjoyment , we shall feel in listening to his music in Goethe s magnificent phrase as if we were present at God s creation of the world . <end>
<start> In other words , we feel here that something great is in the making-up not yet made our mighty modern music , which by conquering nationalities , the Church , and counterpoint has conquered the world . <end>
<start> In Bach there is still too much crude Christianity , crude germanium , crude scholasticism . <end>
<start> He stands on the threshold of modern European music , but turns from thence to look at the Middle Ages . <end>
<start> HANDEL . <end>
<start> Handel , who in the invention of his music was bold , original , truthful , powerful , inclined to and akin to all the heroism of which a nation is capable , often proved stiff , cold , nay even weary of himself in composition . <end>
<start> He applied a few well tried methods of execution , wrote copiously and quickly , and was glad when he had finished but that joy was not the joy of God and other creators in the eventide of their working day . <end>
<start> Haydn . <end>
<start> So far as genius can exist in a man who is merely good , Haydn had genius . <end>
<start> He went just as far as the limit which morality sets to intellect , and only wrote music that has no past . <end>
<start> Beethoven and Mozart . <end>
<start> Beethoven s music often appears like a deeply emotional meditation on unexpectedly hearing once more a piece long thought to be forgotten , Tonal Innocence : it is music about music . <end>
<start> In the song of the beggar and child in the street , in the monotonous airs of vagrant Italians , in the dance of the village inn or in carnival nights he discovers his melodies . <end>
<start> He stores them together like a bee , snatching here and there some notes or a short phrase . <end>
<start> To him these are hallowed memories of the better world , like the ideas of Plato . <end>
<start> Mozart stands in quite a different relation to his melodies . <end>
<start> He finds his inspiration not in hearing music but in gazing at life , at the most stirring life of southern lands . <end>
<start> He was always dreaming of Italy , when he was not there . <end>
<start> Recitative . <end>
<start> Formerly recitative was dry , but now we live in the age of moist recitative . <end>
<start> It has fallen into the water , and the waves carry it they list . <end>
<start> Cheerful Music . <end>
<start> If for a long time southern wine all too quickly into the blood and leaves behind it a soul dazed with narcotics , half awake , longing for sleep . <end>
<start> This is particularly the case with cheerful music , which inspires in us bitterness and pain , satiety and home sickness together , and forces us to sip again and again as at a sweetened draught of poison . <end>
<start> The hall of gay , noisy merriment then seems to grow narrow , the light to lose its brightness and become browner . <end>
<start> At last we feel as if this music were penetrating to a prison where a poor wretch cannon sleep for home sickness . <end>
<start> Franz Schubert . <end>
<start> Franz Schubert , inferior as an artist to the other great musicians , had nevertheless the largest share of inherited musical wealth . <end>
<start> He spent it with a free hand and a kind heart , so that for a few centuries musicians will continue to nibble at his ideas and inspirations . <end>
<start> In his works we find a store of unused inventions the greatness of others will lie in making use of those inventions . <end>
<start> If Beethoven may be called the ideal listener for a troubadour , Schubert has a right to be called the ideal troubadour . <end>
<start> Modern Musical Execution . <end>
<start> Great tragic or dramatic execution of music acquires its character by imitating the gesture of the great sinner , such as Christianity conceives and desires him : the , passionately brooding man , distracted by the agonies of conscience , now flying in terror , now clutching with delight , now standing still in despair and all the other marks of great sinful ness . <end>
<start> Only on the Christian assumption that all men are great sinners and do nothing but sin could we justify the application of this style of execution to all music . <end>
<start> So far , music would be the reflection of all the actions and impulses of man , and would continually have to express by gestures the language of the great sinner . <end>
<start> At such a performance , a listener who was not enough of a Christian to understand this logic might indeed cry out in horror , For the love of Heaven , how did sin find its way into music ? <end>
<start> Felix Mendelssohn . <end>
<start> Felix Mendelssohn s music is the music of the good taste that enjoys all the good things that have ever existed . <end>
<start> It always points behind . <end>
<start> How could it have much in front , much of a future ? <end>
<start> But did he want it to have a future ? <end>
<start> He possessed a virtue rare among artists , that of gratitude without april expense e . <end>
<start> This virtue , too , always points behind . <end>
<start> A Mother of Arts . <end>
<start> In our sceptical age , real devotion requires almost a brutal heroism of ambition . <end>
<start> Fanatical shutting of the eyes and bending of the knee no longer suffice . <end>
<start> Would it not be possible for ambition in its eagerness to be the last devotee of all the ages to become the begetter of a final church music , as it has been the begetter of the final church architecture ? <end>
<start> They call it the Jesuit style . <end>
