<start> Freedom in Fetters a Princely Freedom . <end>
<start> Chopin , the last of the modern musicians , who gazed at and worshipped beauty , like leopard Chopin , the Pole , the inimitable none that came before or after him has a right to this name Chopin had the same princely punctilious in convention that Raphael shows in the use of the simplest traditional colours . <end>
<start> The only difference is that Chopin applies them not to colour but to melodic and rhythmic traditions . <end>
<start> He admitted the validity of these traditions because he was born under the sway of etiquette . <end>
<start> But in these fetters he plays and dances as the freest and faintest of spirits , and , be it observed , he does not spurn the chain . <end>
<start> Chopin s Barcarolle . <end>
<start> Almost all states and modes of life have a moment of rapture , and good artists know how to discover that moment . <end>
<start> Such a moment there is even in life by the seashore that dreary , sordid , unhealthy existence , dragged out in the neighbourhood of a noisy and covetous rabble . <end>
<start> This moment of rapture Chopin in his Barcarolle expressed in sound so supremely that Gods themselves , when they heard it , might yearn to lie long summer evenings in a boat . <end>
<start> Robert Schumann . <end>
<start> The stripping , as the romantic songsters of Germany and France of the first three decades of this century imagined him this stripping was completely translated into song and melody by Robert Schumann , the eternal youth , so long as he felt himself in full possession of his powers . <end>
<start> There are indeed moments when his music reminds one of the eternal old maid . <end>
<start> Dramatic Singers . <end>
<start> Why does this beggar sing ? <end>
<start> Probably he does not know how to wail . <end>
<start> Then he does right . <end>
<start> But our dramatic singers , who wail because they do not know how to sing are they also in the right ? <end>
<start> Dramatic Music . <end>
<start> For him who does not see what is happening on the stage , dramatic music is a monstrosity , just as the running commentary to a lost text is a monstrosity . <end>
<start> Such music requires us to have ears where our eyes are . <end>
<start> This , however , is doing violence to eaterie , who , poor Muse , wants to have her eyes and ears where the other Muses have theirs . <end>
<start> Victory and Reasonableness . <end>
<start> Unfortunately in the aesthetic wars , which artists provoke by their works and apologies for their works , just as is the case in real war , it is might and not reason that decides . <end>
<start> All the world now assumes as a historical fact that , in his dispute with puccini , gluckman in the right . <end>
<start> At any rate , he was victorious , and had might on his side . <end>
<start> THE WANDERER AND HIS SHADOW . <end>
<start> Of the Principle of Musical Execution . <end>
<start> Do the modern musical performers really believe that the supreme law of their art is to give every piece as much high relief as is possible , and to make it speak at all costs a dramatic language ? <end>
<start> Is not this principle , when applied for example to Mozart , a veritable sin against the spirit the gay , sunny , airy , delicate spirit of Mozart , whose seriousness was of a kindly and not awe inspiring order , whose pictures do not try to leap from the wall and drive away the beholder in panic ? <end>
<start> Or do you think that all Mozart s music is identical with the statue music in Don Juan And not only Mozart s , but all music ? <end>
<start> You reply that the advantage of your principle lies in its greater effect . <end>
<start> You would be right if there did not remain the counter question , On whom has the effect operated , and on whom should an artist of the first rank desire to produce his effect ? <end>
<start> Never on the populace ! <end>
<start> Never on the immature ! <end>
<start> Never on the morbidly sensitive ! <end>
<start> Never on the diseased ! <end>
<start> And above all never on the blase The Music of To Day . <end>
<start> This ultra modern music , with its strong lungs and weak nerves , is frightened above all things of itself . <end>
<start> Where Music is at Home . <end>
<start> Music reaches its high water mark only among men who have not the vol . <end>
<start> S ability or the right to argue . <end>
<start> Accordingly , its chief promoters are princes , whose aim is that there should be not much criticism nor even much thought in their neighbourhood . <end>
<start> Next come societies which , under some pressure or other political or religious , are forced to become habituated to silence , and so feel all the greater need of spells to charm away emotional ennui these spells being generally eternal love making and eternal music . <end>
<start> Thirdly , we must reckon whole nations in which there is no society , but all the greater number of individuals with a bent towards solitude , mystical thinking , and a reverence for all that is inexpressible these are the genuine musical souls . <end>
<start> The Greeks , as a nation delighting in talking and argument , accordingly put up with music only as an hors d oeuvre to those arts which really admit of discussion and dispute . <end>
<start> About music one can hardly even think clearly . <end>
<start> The Pythagoreans , who in so many respects were exceptional Greeks , are said to have been great musicians . <end>
<start> This was the school that invented a five years silence , but did not invent a dialectic . <end>
<start> Sentimentality in Music . <end>
<start> We may be ever so much in sympathy with serious and profound music , yet nevertheless , or perhaps all the more for that reason , we shall at occasional moments be overpowered , entranced , and almost melted away by its In the sixth century B . <end>
<start> C . <end>
<start> Pythagoras founded at Croton a school somewhat resembling a monastic order . <end>
<start> Among the ordeals for novitiate was enforced silence for five years . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> opposite I mean , by those simple Italian operatic airs which , in spite of all their monotony of rhythm and childishness of harmony , seem at times to sing to us like the very soul of music . <end>
<start> Admit this or not as you please , you Pharisees of good taste , it is so , and it is my present task to propound the riddle that it is so , and to nibble a little myself at the solution . <end>
<start> In childhood s days we tasted the honey of many things for the first time . <end>
<start> Never was honey so good as then it seduced us to life , into abundant life , in the guise of the first spring , the first flower , the first butterfly , the first friendship . <end>
<start> Then perhaps in our ninth year or so we heard our first music , and this was the first that we understood thus the simplest and most childish tunes , that were not much more than a sequel to the nurse s lullaby and the strolling fiddler s tune , were our first experience . <end>
<start> For even the most trifling revelations of art need preparation and study there is no immediate effect of art , whatever charming fables the philosophers may tell . <end>
<start> Our sensation on hearing these Italian airs is associated with those first musical raptures , the strongest of our lives . <end>
<start> The bliss of childhood and its flight , the feeling that our most precious possession can never be brought back , all this moves the chords of the soul more strongly than the most serious and profound music can move them . <end>
<start> This mingling of aesthetic pleasure with moral pain , which nowadays it is customary to call rather too haughtily , I think sentimentality it is the mood of Faust at the end of the first scene this sentimentality of the listener is all to the advantage of Italian music . <end>
<start> It is a feeling which the ex experienced connoisseurs in art , the pure aesthetes , like to ignore . <end>
<start> Moreover , almost all music has a magical effect only when we hear it speak the language of our own past . <end>
<start> Accordingly , it seems to the layman that all the old music is continually growing better , and that all the latest is of little value . <end>
<start> For the latter arouses no sentimentality , that most essential element of happiness , as aforesaid , for every man who cannon approach this art with pure aesthetic enjoyment . <end>
<start> As Friends of Music . <end>
<start> Ultimately we are and remain good friends with music , as we are with the light of the moon . <end>
<start> Neither , after all , tries to supplant the sun : they only want to illumine our nights to the best of their powers . <end>
<start> Yet we may jest and laugh at them , may we not ? <end>
<start> Just a little , at least , and from time to time ? <end>
<start> At the man in the moon , at the woman in the music ? <end>
<start> Art in an Age of Work . <end>
<start> We have the conscience of an industrious epoch . <end>
<start> This dears us from devoting our best hours and the best part of our days to art , even though that art be the greatest and worthies . <end>
<start> Art is for us a matter of leisure , of recreation , and we consecrate to it the residue of our time and strength . <end>
<start> This is the cardinal fact that has altered the relation of art to life . <end>
<start> When art makes its great demands of time and strength upon its recipients , it has to battle against the conscience of the industrious and efficient , it is relegated to the idle and , who , by their very nature , are not exactly suited to great art , and consider its claims arrogant . <end>
<start> It might , therefore , be all over with art , since it lacks air and the power to breathe . <end>
<start> But perhaps the great art attempts , by a sort of worsening and disguising , to make itself at home in that other atmosphere , or at least to put up with it an atmosphere which is really a natural element only for petty art , the art of recreation , of pleasant distraction . <end>
<start> This happens nowadays almost everywhere . <end>
<start> Even the exponents of great art promise recreation and distraction even they address themselves to the exhausted even they demand from him the evening hours of his just like the artists of the entertaining school , who are content to smooth the furrowed brow and brighten the lack lustre eye . <end>
<start> What , then , are the devices of their mightier brethren ? <end>
<start> These have in their medicine chests the most powerful executants , which might give a shock even to a man half dead : they can deafen you , intoxicated you , make you shudder , or bring tears to your eyes . <end>
<start> By this means they overpower the exhausted man and stimulate him for one night to an over lively condition , to an ecstasy of terror and delight . <end>
<start> This great art , as it now lives in opera , tragedy , and music have we a right to be angry with it , because of its perilous fascination , as we should be angry with a cunning courtesan ? <end>
<start> Certainly not . <end>
<start> It would far rather live in the pure element of morning calm , and would far rather make its appeal to the fresh , expectant , vigorous morning soul of the beholder or listener . <end>
<start> Let us be thankful that it prefers living thus to vanishing altogether . <end>
<start> But let us also confess that an era that once more introduces free and complete high days and holidays into life will have no use for our great art . <end>
<start> The Employees of Science and the Others . <end>
<start> Really efficient and successful men of science might be collectively called The Employees . <end>
<start> If in youth is sufficiently practised , their memory full , and hand and eye have acquired sureness , they are appointed by an older fellow craftsman to a scientific position where their qualities may prove useful . <end>
<start> Later on , when they have themselves gained an eye for the gaps and defects in their science , they place themselves in whatever position they are needed . <end>
<start> These persons all exist for the sake of science . <end>
<start> But there are rarer spirits , spirits that seldom succeed or fully mature for whose sake science exists at least , in their view . <end>
<start> They are often unpleasant , conceited , or cross grained men , but almost always prodigies to a certain extent . <end>
<start> They are neither employees nor employers they make use of what those others have worked out and established , with a certain princely carelessness and with little and rare praise just as if the others belonged to a lower order of beings . <end>
<start> Yet they possess the same qualities as their fellow workers , and that sometimes in a less developed form . <end>
<start> Moreover , they have a peculiar limitation , from which the others are free this makes it impossible to put them into a place and to see in them useful tools . <end>
<start> They can only live in their own air and on their own soil . <end>
<start> This limitation suggests to them what elements of a science are theirs n in other words , what they can carry home into their house and atmosphere : they think that they are always collecting their scattered property . <end>
<start> If they are prevented from building at their own nest , they perish like birds . <end>
<start> The loss of freedom causes them to wilt away . <end>
<start> If they show , like their colleagues , a fondness for certain regions of science , it is always only regions where the fruits and seeds necessary to them can thrive . <end>
<start> What do they care whether science , taken as a whole , has untitled or badly tilled regions ? <end>
<start> They lack all impersonal interest in a scientific problem . <end>
<start> As they are themselves personal through and through , all their knowledge and ideas are moulded into a person , into a living complexity , with its parts interdependent , overlapping , jointly nurtured , and with a peculiar atmosphere and scent as a whole . <end>
<start> Such natures , with their system of personal knowledge , produce the illusion that a science or even the whole of philosophy is finished and has reached its goal . <end>
<start> The life in their system works this magic , which at times has been fatal to science and deceptive to the really efficient workers above described , and at other times , when drought and exhaustion prevailed , has acted as a kind of restorative , as if it were the air of a cool , refreshing resting place . <end>
<start> These men are usually called philosophers . <end>

<start> The Talk of Invalids . <end>
<start> Just as in spiritual grief we tear our hair , strike our foreheads , lacerated our cheeks or even like oedipus gouge our eyes out , so against violent physical pain we call to our aid a bitter , violent emotion , through the recollection of slanderous and malignant people , through the denigration of our future , through the sword pricks and acts of malice which we mentally direct against the absent . <end>
<start> And at times it is true that one devil drives out another but then we have the other . <end>
<start> Hence a different sort of talk , tending to alleviate pain , should be recommended invalids : reflections upon the kindnesses and courtesies that can be performed towards friend and foe . <end>

<start> The Great Danger of Savants . <end>
<start> It is just the most thorough and profound savants who are in peril of seeing their life s goal set ever lower and lower , and , with a feeling of this in their minds , to become ever more discouraged and more unendurable in the latter half of their lives . <end>
<start> At first they plunge into their science with spacious hopes and set themselves daring tasks , the ends of which are already anticipated by their imaginations . <end>
<start> Then there are moments as in the lives of the great maritime discoverers knowledge , presentiment , and power raise each other higher and higher , until a new shore first dawns upon the eye in the far distance . <end>
<start> But now the stern man recognises more and more how important it is that the individual task of the inquirer should be limited as far as possible , so that it may be entirely accomplished and the intolerable waste of force from which earlier periods of science suffered may be avoided . <end>
<start> In those days everything was done ten times over , and then the eleventh always had the last and best word . <end>
<start> Yet the more the savant learns and practises this art of solving riddles in their entirety , the more pleasure he finds in so doing . <end>
<start> But at the same time his demands upon what is here called entirety grow more exacting . <end>
<start> He sets aside everything that must remain in this sense incomplete , he acquires a disgust and an acute scent for the half soluble for all that can only give a kind of certainty in a general and indefinite form . <end>
<start> His youthful plans crumble away before his eyes . <end>
<start> There remains scarcely anything but a few little knots , in untying which the master now takes his pleasure and shows his strength . <end>
<start> Then , in the midst of all this useful , restless activity , he , now grown old , is suddenly then often overcome by a deep misgiving , a sort of torment of conscience . <end>
<start> He looks upon himself as one changed , as if he were diminished , humbled , transformed into a dexterous dwarf he grows anxious as to whether mastery in small matters be not a convenience , an escape from the summons to greatness in ligand form . <end>
<start> But he cannon pass beyond any longer the time for that has gone by . <end>
<start> Teachers in the Age of Books . <end>
<start> Now that self education and mutual education are becoming more widespread , the teacher in his usual form must become almost unnecessary . <end>

<start> Vanity as the Greatest Utility . <end>
<start> Originally the strong individual uses not only Nature but even societies and weaker individuals as objects of raping . <end>
<start> He exploits them , so far as he can , and then passes on . <end>
<start> As he lives from hand to mouth , alternating between hunger and superfluity , he kills more animals than he can eat , and robs and maltreated men more than is necessary . <end>
<start> His manifestation of power is at the same time one of revenge against his cramped and worried existence . <end>
<start> Furthermore , he wishes to be held more powerful than he is , and thus misuses opportunities the accretion of fear that he begets being an accretion of power . <end>
<start> He soon observes that he stands or falls not by what he is but by what he is thought to be . <end>
<start> Herein lies the origin of vanity . <end>
<start> The man of power seeks by every means to increase others faith in his power . <end>
<start> The thrills who tremble before him and serve him know , for their part , that they are worth just much as they appear to him to be worth , and so they work with an eye to this valuation rather than to their own self satisfaction . <end>
<start> We know vanity only in its most weakened forms , in its idealisation and its small doses , because we live in a late and very emasculated state of society . <end>
<start> Originally vanity is the great utility , the strongest means of preservation . <end>
<start> And indeed vanity will be greater , the cleverer the individual , because an increase in the belief in power is easier than an increase in the power itself , but only for him who has intellect or as must be the case under primitive conditions who is cunning and crafty . <end>
<start> Weather Signs of Culture . <end>

<start> Herein lies ideas that men can have , the idea of a progress of all progresses . <end>

<start> There is still reserved for mankind a great deal of joy , the very scent of which has not yet been wafted to the men of our day ! <end>
<start> Indeed , we may promise ourselves this joy , nay summon and conjure it up as a necessary thing , so long as the development of human reason does not stand still . <end>

<start> Origin of Pessimists . <end>
<start> A snack of good food often decides whether we are to look to the future with hollow eye or in hopeful mood . <end>
<start> The same influence extends to the very highest and most intellectual states . <end>
<start> Discontent and reviling of the world are for the present generation an inheritance from starveling ancestors . <end>
<start> Even in our artists and poets we often notice that , however exuberant their life , they are not of good birth , and have often , from oppressed and ill nourished ancestors , inherited in their blood and brain much that comes out as the subject and even the conscious colouring of their work . <end>
<start> The culture of the Greeks is a culture of men of wealth , in fact , inherited wealth . <end>
<start> For a few centuries they lived better than we do better in every sense , in particular far more simply in food and drink . <end>
<start> Then the brain finally became so well stored and subtle , and the blood flowed so quickly , like a joyous , clear wine , that the best in them came to light no longer as gloomy , distorted , and violent , but full of beauty and sunshine . <end>
<start> Of Reasonable Death . <end>
<start> Which is more reasonable , to stop the machine when the works have done the task demanded of them , or to let it run on until it stands still of its own accord in other words , is destroyed ? <end>
<start> Is not the latter a waste of the cost of upkeep , a misuse of the strength and care of those who serve ? <end>
<start> Are men not here throwing away that which would be sorely needed elsewhere ? <end>
<start> Is not a kind of contempt of the machines propagated , in that many of them are so uselessly tended and kept up ? <end>
<start> I am speaking of involuntary natural and voluntary reasonable death . <end>
<start> Natural death is independent of all reason and is really an irrational death , in which the pitiable substance of the shell determines how long the kernel is to exist or not in which , accordingly , the stunted , diseased and dull fitted jailer is lord , and indicates the moment at which his distinguished prisoner shall die . <end>
<start> Natural death is the suicide of nature in other words , the annihilation of the most rational being through the most irrational element that is attached thereto . <end>
<start> Only through religious illumination can the reverse appear for then , as is equitable , the higher reason God issues its orders , which the lower reason has to obey . <end>
<start> Outside religious thought natural death is not worth glorifying . <end>
<start> The wise dispensation and disposal of death belongs to that now quite incomprehensible and immoral sounding morality of the future , the dawn of which it will be an ineffable delight to behold . <end>
<start> Retrograde Influences . <end>
<start> All criminals force society back to earlier stages of culture than that in which they are placed for the time being . <end>
<start> Their influence is retrograde . <end>
<start> Let us consider the tools that society must forge and maintain for its defence : the cunning detectives , the jailers , the hangmen . <end>
<start> Nor should we forget the public counsel for prosecution and defence . <end>
<start> Finally we may ask ourselves whether the judge himself and punishment and the whole legal procedure are not oppressive rather than elevating in their reaction upon all who are not law breakers . <end>
<start> For we shall never succeed in carrying self defence and revenge in the garb of innocence , and so long as men are used and sacrificed as a means to the end of society , all loftier humanity will deplore this necessity . <end>

<start> War as a Remedy . <end>
<start> For nations that are growing weak and contemptible war may be prescribed as a remedy , if indeed they really want to go on living . <end>
<start> National consumption as well as individual admits of a brutal cure . <end>
<start> The eternal will to live and inability to die is , however , in itself already a sign of senility of emotion . <end>
<start> The more fully and thoroughly we live , the more ready we are to sacrifice life for a single pleasurable emotion . <end>
<start> A people that lives and feels in this wise has no need of war . <end>
<start> Intellectual and Physical Transplantation as Remedies . <end>
<start> The different cultures are so many intellectual climates , every one of which is peculiarly harmful or beneficial to this or that organism . <end>
<start> History as a whole , as the knowledge of different cultures , is the science of remedies , but not the science of the healing art itself . <end>
<start> We still need a physician who can make use of these remedies , in order to send every one temporarily or permanently to the climate that just suits him . <end>
<start> To live in the present , within the limits of a single culture , is insufficient as a universal remedy : too many highly useful kinds of men , who cannon breathe freely in this atmosphere , would perish . <end>
<start> With the aid of history we must give them air and try to preserve them : even men of lower cultures have their value . <end>
<start> Add to this cure of intellects that humanity , on considerations of bodily health , must strive to discover by means of a medical geography what kinds of degeneration and disease are caused by each region of the earth , and conversely , what ingredients of health the earth affords : and then , gradually , nations , families , and individuals must be transplanted long and permanently enough for them to become masters of their inherited physical infirmities . <end>
<start> The whole world will finally be a series of anatolia . <end>
<start> Reason and the Tree of Mankind . <end>
<start> What you all fear in your senile short , regarding the over population of the world , gives the more hopeful a mighty task . <end>
<start> Man is some day to become a tree overshadowing the whole earth , with millions upon millions of buds that shall all grow to fruits side by side , and the earth itself shall be prepared for the nourishment of this tree . <end>
<start> That the shoot , tiny as yet , may increase in sap and strength that the sap may flow in countless channels for the nutrition of the whole and the parts from these and similar tasks we must derive our standard for measuring whether a man of to day is useful or worthless . <end>
<start> The task is unspeakably great and adventurous : let us all contribute our share to prevent the tree from rotting before its time ! <end>
<start> The historically trained mind will no doubt succeed in calling up the human activities of all the ages before its eyes , as the community of ants with its cunningly wrought mounds stands before our eyes . <end>
<start> Superficially judged , mankind as a whole , like ant kind , might admit of our speaking of instinct . <end>
<start> On a closer examination we observe how whole nations , vol . <end>
<start> T nay whole centuries , take pains to discover and test new means of benefiting the great mass of humanity , and thus finally the great common fruit tree of the world . <end>
<start> Whatever injury the individual nations or periods may suffer in this testing process , they have each become wise through this injury , and from them the tide of wisdom slowly pours over the principles of whole races and whole epochs . <end>
<start> Ants too go astray and make blunders . <end>
<start> Through the folly of its remedies , mankind may well go to rack and ruin before the proper time . <end>
<start> There is no sure guiding instinct for the former or the latter . <end>
<start> Rather must we boldly face the great task of preparing the earth for a plant of the most ample and joyous fruitfulness a task set by reason to reason ! <end>
<start> The Praise of Disinterestedness and its ORIGIN . <end>
<start> Between two neighbouring chieftains there was a long standing quarrel : they laid waste each other s territories , stole cattle , and burnt down houses , with an indecisive result on the whole , because their power was fairly equal . <end>
<start> A third , who from the distant situation of his property was able to keep aloof from these feuds , yet had reason to dread the day when one of the two neighbours should gain a decisive preponderance , at last intervened between the combatants with ceremonial goodwill . <end>
<start> Secretly he lent a heavy weight to his peace proposal by giving either to understand that he would henceforth join forces with the other against the one who strove to break the peace . <end>
<start> They met in his presence , they hesitatingly placed into his hand the hands that had hitherto been the tools and only too often the causes of hatred and then they really and seriously tried to keep the peace . <end>
<start> Either saw with astonishment how suddenly his prosperity and his comfort increased how he now had as neighbour a dealer ready to buy and sell instead of a treacherous or openly scornful evil doer how even , in unforeseen troubles , they could reciprocally save each other from distress , instead of , as before , making capital out of this distress of his neighbour and enhancing it to the highest degree . <end>
<start> It even seemed as if the human type had improved in both countries , for the eyes had become brighter , the forehead had lost its wrinkles all now felt confidence in the future and nothing is more advantageous for the souls and bodies of men than this confidence . <end>
<start> They saw each other every year on the anniversary of the alliance , the chieftains as well as their retinue , and indeed before the eyes of the mediator , whose mode of action they admired and revered more and more , the greater the profit that they owed to him became . <end>
<start> Then his mode of action was called disinterested . <end>
<start> They had looked far too fixedly at the profit they had reaped themselves hitherto to see anything more of their neighbour s method of dealing than that his condition in consequence of this had not altered so much as their own he had rather remained the same : and thus it appeared that the former had not had his profit in view . <end>
<start> For the first time people said to themselves that disinterestedness was a virtue . <end>
<start> It is true that in minor private matters similar circumstances had arisen , but men only had eyes for this virtue when it was depicted on the walls in a large script that was legible to the whole community . <end>
<start> Moral qualities are not recognised as virtues , endowed with names , held in esteem , and recommended as worthy of acquisition until the moment when they have visibly decided the happiness and destiny of whole societies . <end>
<start> For then the loftiness of sentiment and the excitation of the inner creative forces is in many so great , that offerings are brought to this quality , offerings from the best of what each possesses . <end>
<start> At its feet the serious man lays his seriousness , the dignified man his dignity , women their gentleness , the young all the wealth of hope and futurity that in them lies the poet lends it words and names , sets it marching in the procession of similar beings , gives it a pedigree , and finally , as is the way of artists , adores the picture of his fancy as a new godhead he even teaches others to adore . <end>
<start> Thus in the end , with the co operation of universal love and gratitude , a virtue becomes , like a statue , a repository of all that is good and honourable , a sort of temple and divine personage combined . <end>
<start> It appears thenceforward as an individual virtue , as an absolute entity , which it was not before , and exercises the power and privileges of a sanctified super humanity . <end>
<start> In the later days of Greece the cities were full of such deified human abstractions if one may so call them . <end>
<start> The nation , in its own fashion , had set up a Platonic Heaven of Ideas on earth , and I do not think that its inhabitants were felt to be less alive than any of the old Homeric divinities . <end>

<start> Nature and Science . <end>
<start> As in nature , so in science the worse and less fertile soils are first cultivated because the means that science in its early stages has at command are fairly sufficient for this purpose . <end>
<start> The working of the most fertile soils requires an enormous , carefully developed , persevering method , tangible individual results , and an organised body of well trained workers . <end>
<start> All these are found together only at a late stage . <end>
<start> Impatience and ambition often grasp too early at these most fertile soils , but the results are then from the first null and void . <end>
<start> In nature such losses would usually be avenged by the starvation of the settlers . <end>

<start> Neutrality of Nature on a Grand Scale . <end>
<start> The neutrality of Nature on a grand scale in mountain , sea , forest , and desert is pleasing , but only for a brief space . <end>
<start> Afterwards we become impatient . <end>
<start> Have they all nothing to say to us Do we not exist so far as they are concerned ? <end>
<start> There arises a feeling that a less majesty is committed against humanity . <end>
<start> Forgetting our Purpose . <end>
<start> In a journey we commonly forget its goal . <end>
<start> Almost every vocation is chosen and entered upon as means to an end , but is continued as the ultimate end . <end>
<start> Forgetting our purpose is the most frequent form of folly . <end>
<start> Solar Orbit of an Idea . <end>
<start> When an idea is just rising on the horizon , the soul s temperature is usually very low . <end>
<start> Gradually the idea develops in warmth , and is hottest that is to say , exerts its greatest influence when belief in the idea is already on the wane . <end>
<start> HOW TO HAVE EVERY MAN AGAINST YOU . <end>
<start> If some one now dared to say , He that is not for me is against me , he would at once have all against him . <end>
<start> This sentiment does credit to our era . <end>
<start> Being Ashamed of Wealth . <end>
<start> Our age endures only a single species of rich men those who are ashamed of their wealth . <end>
<start> If we hear it said of any one that he is very rich , we at once feel a similar sentiment to that experienced at the sight of a repulsive swollen invalid , one suffering from diabetes or dropsy . <end>
<start> We must with an effort remember our humanity , in order to go about with this rich man in such a way that he does not notice our feeling of disgust . <end>
<start> But as soon as he prides himself at all on his wealth , our feelings are mingled with an almost compassionate surprise at such a high degree of human unreason . <end>
<start> We would fain raise our hands to heaven and cry , Poor deformed and overburdened creature , fettered a hundredfold , to whom every hour brings or may bring something unpleasant , in whose frame twitches every event that occurs in scores of countries , how can you make us believe that you feel at ease in your position ? <end>
<start> If you appear anywhere in public , we know that it is a sort of running the gauntlet amid countless glances that have for you only cold hate or opportunity or silent scorn . <end>
<start> You may earn more easily than others , but it is only a superfluous earning , which brings little joy , and the guarding of what you have earned is now , at any rate , a more troublesome business than any noisome process of earning . <end>
<start> You are continually suffering , because you are continually losing . <end>
<start> What avail it you that they are always injecting you with fresh artificial blood ? <end>
<start> That does not relieve the pain of those that are fixed , for ever fixed , on your neck ! <end>
<start> But , to be quite fair to you , it is difficult or perhaps impossible for you not to be rich . <end>
<start> You must guard , you must earn more the inherited bent of your character is the yoke fastened upon you . <end>
<start> But do not on that account deceive us be honestly and visibly ashamed of the yoke you wear , as in your soul you are weary and unwilling to wear it . <end>
<start> This shame is no disgrace . <end>
<start> Extravagant Presumptions . <end>
<start> There are men so presumptuous that they can only praise a greatness which they publicly admire by representing it as steps and bridges that lead to themselves . <end>
<start> On the Soil of Insult . <end>
<start> He who wishes to deprive men of a conception is generally not satisfied with refuting it and drawing out of it the illogical worm that resides within . <end>
<start> Rather , when the worm has been killed , does he throw the whole fruit as well into the mire , in order to make it ignoble in men s sight and to inspire disgust . <end>
<start> Thus he thinks that he has found a means of making the usual third day resurrection of conceptions an impossibility . <end>
<start> He is wrong , for on the very soil of insult , in the midst of the filth , the kernel of the conception soon produces new seeds . <end>
<start> The right thing then , is not to scorn and spatter what one wishes finally to remove , but to lay it tenderly on ice again and again , having regard to the fact that conceptions are very tenacious of life . <end>
<start> Here we must act according to the maxim : One refutation is no refutation . <end>
<start> The Lot of Morality . <end>
<start> Since spiritual bondage is being relaxed , morality the inherited , traditional , instinctive mode of action in accordance with moral sentiments is surely also on the decline . <end>
<start> This , however , is not the case with the individual virtues , moderation , justice , repose for the greatest freedom of the conscious intellect leads at some time , even unconsciously , back to these virtues , and then enjoins their practice as expedient . <end>
<start> The Fanatic of Distrust and his Surety . <end>
<start> The Elder . <end>
<start> You wish to make the tremendous venture and instruct mankind in the great things ? <end>
<start> What is your surety ? <end>
<start> myrrh : It is this : I intend to warn men against myself I intend to confess all the defects of my character quite openly , and reveal to the world my hasty conclusions , my contradictions , and my foolish blunders . <end>
<start> Do not listen to me , I will say to them , until I have become equal to the meanest among you , nay am even less than he . <end>
<start> Struggle against truth as long as you can , from your disgust with her advocate . <end>
<start> I shall be your seducer and betrayer if you find in me the slightest glimmering of respectability and dignity . <end>
<start> The Elder : You promise too much you cannon bear this burden . <end>
<start> myrrh : Then I will tell men even that , and say that I am too weak , and cannon keep my promise . <end>
<start> The greater my unworthiness , the more will they mistrust the truth , when it passes through my lips . <end>
<start> The Elder : You propose to teach distrust of truth ? <end>
<start> myrrh : Yes distrust as it never was yet on earth , distrust of anything and everything . <end>
<start> This is the only road to truth . <end>
<start> The right eye must not trust the left eye , and for some time light must be called darkness : this is the path that you must tread . <end>
<start> Do not imagine that it will lead you to fruit trees and fair pastures . <end>
<start> You will find on this road little hard grains these are truths . <end>
<start> For years and years you will have to swallow handfuls of lies , so as not to die of hunger , although you know that they are lies . <end>
<start> But those grains will be sown and planted , and perhaps , perhaps some day will come the harvest . <end>
<start> No one may promise that day , unless he be a fanatic . <end>
<start> The Elder : Friend , friend ! <end>
<start> Your words too are those of a fanatic ! <end>
<start> myrrh : You are right ! <end>
<start> I will be distrustful of all words . <end>
<start> myrrh : I shall tell men that I have to be silent , and that they are to mistrust my silence . <end>
<start> myrrh : On the contrary you have shown me the door through which I must pass . <end>
<start> The Elder : I don t know whether we yet completely understand each other ? <end>
<start> The Elder : If only you understand yourself ! <end>
<start> myrrh turns round and laughs . <end>
<start> The Elder : Ah , friend ! <end>
<start> Silence and laughter is that now your whole philosophy ? <end>
<start> myrrh : There might be a worse . <end>
<start> EUROPEAN BOOKS . <end>
<start> In reading Montaigne , La Rochefoucauld , La buyer , Fontenelle especially the Dialogues des sorts , Vauvenargues , and comfort we are nearer to antiquity than in any group of six authors of other nations . <end>
<start> Through these six the spirit of the last centuries before Christ has once more come into being , and they collectively form an important link in the great and still continuous chain of the Renaissance . <end>
<start> Their books are raised above all changes of national taste and philosophical nuances from which as a rule every book takes and must take its hue in order to become famous . <end>
<start> They contain more real ideas than all the books of German philosophers am at a loss how to define to the end : enough to say that they appear to me writers who wrote neither for children nor for visionaries , neither for virgins nor for Christians , neither for Germans north praise them in plain terms , I may say that had they been written in Greek , they would have been understood by Greeks . <end>
<start> How much , on the other hand , would even a Plato have understood of the writings of our best German thinkers Goethe and Schopenhauer , for instance to say nothing of the repugnance that he would have felt to their style , particularly to its obscure , exaggerated , and occasionally dry as dust elements ? <end>
<start> And these are defects from which these two among German thinkers suffer least and yet far too much Goethe as thinker was fonder than he should have been of embracing the cloud , and Schopenhauer almost constantly wanders , not with impunity , among symbols of objects rather than among the objects themselves . <end>
<start> On the other hand , what clearness and graceful precision there is in these Frenchmen ! <end>
<start> The Greeks , whose ears were most refined , could not but have approved of this art , and one quality they would even have admired and reverence the French verbal wit : they were extremely fond of this quality , without being particularly strong in it themselves . <end>
<start> Fashion and Modernity . <end>
<start> Wherever ignorance , uncleanness , and superstition are still rife , where communication is backward , agriculture poor , and the priesthood powerful , national costumes are still worn . <end>
<start> Fashion , on the other hand , rules where the opposite conditions prevail . <end>
<start> Fashion is accordingly to be found next to the virtues in modern Europe . <end>
<start> Are we to call it their seamy side ? <end>
<start> Masculine dress that is fashionable and no longer national proclaims of its wearer : firstly , that he does not wish to appear as an individual or as member of a class or race that he has made an intentional suppression of these kinds of vanity a law unto himself : secondly , that he is a worker , and has little time for dressing and self adornment , and moreover regards anything expensive or luxurious in material and cut as out of harmony with his work : lastly , that by his clothes he indicates the more learned and intellectual callings as those to which he stands or would like to stand nearest as a European whereas such national costumes as still exist would exhibit the occupations of brigand , shepherd , and soldier as the most desirable and distinguished . <end>
<start> Within this general character of masculine fashion exist the slight fluctuations demanded by the vanity of young men , the dandies and handlers of our great cities in other words , Europeans who have not yet reached maturity . <end>
<start> European women are as yet far less mature , and for this reason the fluctuations with them are much greater . <end>
<start> They also will not have the national costume , and hate to be recognised by their dress as German , French , or Russian . <end>
<start> They are , however , very desirous of creating an impression as individuals . <end>
<start> Then , too , their dress must leave no one in doubt that they belong to one of the more reputable classes of society to good or high or great society , and on this score their pretensions are all the greater if they belong scarcely or not at all to that class . <end>
<start> Above all , the young woman does not want to wear what an older woman wears , because she thinks she loses her market value if she is suspected of being somewhat advanced in years . <end>
<start> The older woman , on the other hand , would like to deceive the world as long as possible by a youthful garb . <end>
<start> From this competition must continually arise temporary fashions , in which the youthful element is unmistakably and inimitable apparent . <end>
<start> But after the inventive genius of the young female artists has run riot for some time in such indiscreet revelations of youth or rather , after the inventive genius of older , courtly civilisations and of still existing peoples in fact , of the whole world of dress has been pressed into the service , and , say , the Spaniards , Turks , and ancient Greeks have been yoked together for the glorification of fair flesh , then they at last discover , time and again , that they have not been good judges of their own interest that if they wish to have power over men , the game of hide and seek with the beautiful body is more likely to win than naked or half naked honesty . <end>
<start> And then the wheel of taste and vanity turns once more in an opposite direction . <end>
<start> The rather older young women find that their kingdom has come , and the competition of the dear , absurd creatures rages again from the beginning . <end>
<start> But the more women advance mentally , and no longer among themselves concede the pre eminence to an unripe age , the smaller their fluctuations of costume grow and the less elaborate their adornment . <end>
<start> A just verdict in this respect must not be based on ancient models in other words , not on the standard of the dress of women who dwell on the shores of the Mediterranean but must have an eye to the climatic conditions of the central and northern regions , where the intellectual and creative spirit of Europe now finds its most natural home . <end>
<start> Generally speaking , therefore , it is not change that will be the characteristic mark of fashion and modernity , for change is retrograde , and betokens vol . <end>
<start> the still unripened men and women of Europe rather the repudiation of national , social , and individual vanity . <end>
<start> Accordingly , it is commendable , because involving a saving of time and strength , if certain cities and districts of Europe think and invent for all the rest in the matter of dress , in view of the fact that a sense of form does not seem to have been bestowed upon all . <end>
<start> Nor is it really an excessive ambition , so long as these fluctuations still exist , for Paris , for example , to claim to be the sole inventor and innovator in this sphere . <end>
<start> If a German , from hatred of these claims on the part of a French city , wishes to dress differently , as , for example , in the dinner style , let him reflect that he then has a costume which the Germans of olden times wore , but which the Germans have not in the slightest degree invented . <end>
<start> For there has never been a style of dress that characterised the German as a German . <end>
<start> Moreover , let him observe how he looks in his costume , and whether his altogether modern face , with all its hues and wrinkles , does not raise a protest against a dinner fashion of dress . <end>
<start> Here , where the concepts modern and European are almost identical , we understand by Europe a far wider region than is embraced by the Europe of geography , the little peninsula of Asia . <end>
<start> In particular , we must include America , in so far as America is the daughter of our civilisation . <end>
<start> On the other hand , not all Europe falls under the heading of cultured Europe , but only those nations and divisions of nations which have their common past in Greece , Rome , Judaism , and Christianity . <end>
<start> buttle WANDERER AND HIS SHADOW . <end>
<start> l . <end>
<start> German Virtue . <end>
<start> There is no denying that from the end of the eighteenth century a current of moral awakening flowed through Europe . <end>
<start> Then only Virtue found again the power of speech . <end>
<start> She learnt to discover the unrestrained gestures of exaltation and emotion , she was no longer ashamed of herself , and she created philosophies and poems for her own glorification . <end>
<start> If we look for the sources of this current , we come upon Rousseau , but the mythical Rousseau , the phantom formed from the impression left by his writings one might almost say again , his mythical interpreted writings and by the indications that he provided himself . <end>
<start> He and his public constantly worked at the fashioning of this ideal figure . <end>
<start> The other origin lies in the resurrection of the Stoical side of Rome s greatness , whereby the French so nobly carried on the task of the Renaissance . <end>
<start> With striking success they proceeded from the reproduction of antique forms tithe reproduction of antique characters . <end>
<start> Thus they may always claim a title to the highest honours , as the nation which has hitherto given the modern world its best books and its best men . <end>
<start> How this twofold archetype , the mythical Rousseau and the resurrected spirit of Rome , affected France s weaker neighbours , is particularly noticeable in Germany , which , in consequence of her novel and quite unwonted impulse to seriousness and loftiness in will and self control , finally came to feel astonishment at her own newfound virtue , and launched into the world the concept German virtue , as if this were the most original and hereditary of her possessions . <end>
<start> The first great men who transfused into their own blood that French impulse towards greatness and consciousness of the moral will were more honest , and more grateful . <end>
<start> Whence comes the moralism of Kant ? <end>
<start> He is continually reminding us : from Rousseau and the revival of Stoic Rome . <end>
<start> The moralism of Schiller has the same source and the same glorification of the source . <end>
<start> The moralism of Beethoven in notes is a continual song in praise of Rousseau , the antique French , and Schiller . <end>
<start> Young Germany was the first to forget its gratitude , because in the meantime people had listened to the preachers of hatred of the French . <end>
<start> The young German came to the fore with more consciousness than is generally allowed to youths . <end>
<start> When he investigated his paternity , he might well think of the proximity of Schiller , Schleiermacher , and Fichte . <end>
<start> But he should have looked for his grandfathers in Paris and Geneva , and it was very short sighted of him to believe what he believed : that virtue was not more than thirty years old . <end>
<start> People became used to demanding that the word German should connote virtue , and this process has not been wholly forgotten to this day . <end>
<start> Be it observed further that this moral awakening , as may almost be guessed , has resulted only in drawbacks and obstacles to the recognition of moral phenomena . <end>
<start> What is the entire German philosophy , starting from Kant , with all its French , English , and Italian offshoots and by products ? <end>
<start> A semi theological attack upon helvetica , a rejection of the slowly and laboriously acquired views and signposts of the right road , which in the end he collected and expressed so well . <end>
<start> To this day helvetica is the best abused of all good moralists and good men in Germany . <end>
<start> Classic and Romantic . <end>
<start> Both classically and romantically minded spirits two species that always exist cherish a vision of the future but the former derive their vision from the strength of their time , the latter from its weakness . <end>
<start> The Machine as Teacher . <end>
<start> Machinery teaches in itself the dovetailed working of masses of men , in activities where each has but one thing to do . <end>
<start> It is the model of party organisations and of warfare . <end>
<start> On the other hand , it does not teach individual , for it makes of the many a machine , and of each individual a tool for one purpose . <end>
<start> Its most general effect is to teach the advantage of centralisation . <end>
<start> Unable to Settle . <end>
<start> One likes to live in a small town . <end>
<start> But from time to time just this small town drives us out into bare and lonely Nature , especially when we think we know it too well . <end>
<start> Finally , in order to refresh ourselves from Nature , we go to the big town . <end>
<start> A few draughts from this cup and we see its dregs , and the circle begins afresh , with the small town as starting point . <end>
<start> So the moderns live they are in all things rather too thorough to be able to settle like the men of other days . <end>
<start> Reaction against the Civilisation of Machinery . <end>
<start> The machine , itself a product of the highest mental powers , sets in motion hardly any but the lower , unthinking forces of the men who serve it . <end>
<start> True , it unfettered a vast quantity of force which would otherwise lie dormant But it does not communicate the impulse to climb higher , to improve , to become artistic . <end>
<start> It creates activity and monotony , but this in the long run produces a counter effect , a despairing ennui of the soul , which through machinery has learnt to hanker after the variety of leisure . <end>
<start> The Danger of Enlightenment . <end>
<start> All the half insane , theatrical , bestial cruel , licentious , and especially sentimental and self intoxicating elements which go to form the true revolutionary substance , and became flesh and spirit , before the revolution , in Rousseau all this composite being , with factitious enthusiasm , finally set even enlightenment upon its fanatical head , which thereby began itself to shine as in an illuminating halo . <end>
<start> Yet , enlightenment is essentially foreign to that phenomenon , and , if left to itself , would have pierced silently through the clouds like a shaft of light , long content to transfigured individuals alone , and thus only slowly national customs and institutions as well . <end>
<start> But now , bound hand and foot to a violent and abrupt monster , enlightenment itself became violent and abrupt . <end>
<start> Its danger has therefore become almost greater than its useful quality of liberation and illumination , which it introduced into the great revolutionary movement . <end>
<start> Whoever grasps this will also know from what confusion it has to be extricated , from what impurities to be cleansed , in order that it may then by itself continue the work of enlightenment and also nip the revolution in the bud and nullify its effects . <end>
<start> Passion in the Middle Ages . <end>
<start> The Middle Ages are the period of great passions . <end>
<start> Neither antiquity nor our period possesses this widening of the soul . <end>
<start> Never was the capacity of the soul greater or measured by larger standards . <end>
<start> The physical , primeval sensuality of the barbarian races and the , over vigilant , over brilliant eyes of Christian mystics , the most childish and youthful and the most over ripe and world weary , the of the beast of prey and the effeminacy and excessive refinement of the late antique spirit all these elements were then not seldom united in one and the same person . <end>
<start> Thus , if a man was seized by a passion , the rapidity of the torrent must have been greater , the whirl more confused , the fall deeper than ever before . <end>
<start> We modern men may be content to feel that we have suffered a loss here . <end>
<start> Robbing and Saving . <end>
<start> All intellectual movements whereby the great may hope to rob and thermal to save are sure to prosper . <end>
<start> That is why instance , the German Reformation made progress . <end>
<start> gladstone Souls . <end>
<start> When even a remote hint of drink , drunkenness , and an evil smelling kind of jocularity was given , the souls of the old Germans waxed gladstone . <end>
<start> Otherwise they were depressed , but here they found something they really understood . <end>
<start> Debauchery at Athens . <end>
<start> Even when the of Athens acquired its thinkers and poets , Greek debauchery had a more idyllic and refined appearance than Roman or German debauchery ever had . <end>
<start> The voice of Juvenal would have sounded there like a hollow trumpet , and would have been answered by a good natured and almost childish outburst of laughter . <end>
<start> Cleverness of the Greek . <end>
<start> As the desire for victory and pre eminence is an ineradicable trait of human nature , older and more primitive than any respect of or joy in equality , the Greek State sanctioned gymnastic and artistic competitions among equals . <end>
<start> In other words , it marked out an arena where this impulse to conquer would find a vent without jeopardising the political order . <end>
<start> With the final decline of gymnastic and artistic contests the Greek State fell into a condition of profound unrest and dissolution . <end>
<start> f , forth WANDERER AND HIS SHADOW . <end>
<start> The Eternal Epicurus . <end>
<start> Epicurus has lived in all periods , and lives yet , unbeknown to those who called and still call themselves epicurean , and without repute among philosophers . <end>
<start> He has himself even forgotten his own name that was the heaviest luggage that he ever cast off . <end>
<start> The Style of Superiority . <end>
<start> University slang , the speech of the German students , has its origin among the students who do not study . <end>
<start> The latter know how to acquire a preponderance over their more serious fellows by exposing all the farcical elements of culture , respectability , erudition , order , and moderation , and by having words taken from these realms always on their lips , like the better and more learned students , but with malice in their glance and an accompanying grimace . <end>
<start> This language of superiority the only one that is original in Germany is nowadays unconsciously used by statesmen and newspaper critics as well . <end>
<start> It is a continual process of ironical quotation , a restless , cantankerous squinting of the eye right and left , a language of inverted commas and grimaces . <end>
<start> The Recluse . <end>
<start> We retire into seclusion , but not from personal misgivings , as if the political and social conditions of the day did not satisfy us rather because by our retirement we try to save and collect forces which will some day be urgently needed by culture , the more this present is this present y and , as such , fulfils its task . <end>
<start> We form a capital and try to make it secure , but , as in times of real danger , our method is to bury our hoard . <end>
<start> Tyrants of the Intellect . <end>
<start> In our times , any one who expressed a single moral trait so thoroughly as the characters of Theophrastus and Moliere do , would be considered ill , and be spoken of as possessing a fixed idea . <end>
<start> The Athens of the third century , if we could visit it , would appear to us populated by fools . <end>
<start> Nowadays the democracy of ideas rules in every brain there the multitude collectively is lord . <end>
<start> A single idea that tried to be lord is now called , as above stated , a fixed idea . <end>
<start> This is our method of murdering tyrants we hint at the madhouse . <end>
<start> A Most Dangerous Emigration . <end>
<start> In Russia there is an emigration of the intelligence . <end>
<start> People cross the frontier in order to read and write good books . <end>
<start> Thus , however , they are working towards turning their country , abandoned by the intellect , into a gaping Asiatic maw , which would fain swallow our little Europe . <end>
<start> Political Fools . <end>
<start> The almost religious love of the king was transferred by the Greeks , when the monarchy was abolished , to the polls . <end>
<start> An idea can be loved more than a person , and does not thwart the lover so often as a beloved human being for the more men know themselves to be loved , the less considerate they usually become , until they are no longer worthy of love , and a rift really arises . <end>
<start> Hence the reverence for State and poles was greater than the reverence for princes had ever been . <end>
<start> The Greeks are the political fools of ancient history today other nations boast that distinction . <end>
<start> Against Neglect of the Eyes . <end>
<start> Might one not find among the cultured classes of England , who read the titles , a decline in their powers of sight every ten years ? <end>
<start> Great Works and Great Faith . <end>
<start> One man had great works , but his comrade had great faith in these works . <end>
<start> They were inseparable , but obviously the former was entirely dependent upon the latter . <end>
<start> The Sociable Man . <end>
<start> I don t get on well with myself , said some one in explanation of his fondness for society . <end>
<start> Society has a stronger digestion than I have , and can put up with me . <end>
<start> Shutting the Mind s Eyes . <end>
<start> If we are practised and accustomed to reflect upon our actions , we must nevertheless close the inner eye while performing an action be this even only writing letters or eating or drinking . <end>
<start> Even in conversation with average people we must know how to obscure our own mental vision in order to attain and grasp average thinking . <end>
<start> This shutting of the eyes is a conscious act and can be achieved by the will . <end>
<start> The Most Terrible Revenge . <end>
<start> If we wish to take a thorough revenge upon an opponent , we must wait until we have our hand quite full of truths and equities , and can calmly use the whole lot against him . <end>
<start> Hence the exercise of revenge may be identified with the exercise of equity . <end>
<start> It is the most terrible kind of revenge , for there is no higher court to which an appeal can be made . <end>
<start> Thus did Voltaire revenge himself on iron , with five lines that sum up iron s whole life , work , and character : every word is a truth . <end>
<start> So too he revenged himself upon Frederick the Great in a letter to him from verney . <end>
<start> Taxes of Luxury . <end>
<start> In shops we buy the most necessary and urgent things , and have to pay very dear , because we pay as well for what is also to be had there cheap , but seldom finds a customer articles of luxury that minister to pleasure . <end>
<start> Thus luxury lays a constant tax upon the man of simple life who does without luxuries . <end>
<start> THE WANDERER AND HIS SHADOW . <end>
<start> Why Beggars still Live . <end>
<start> If all alms were given only out of compassion , the whole tribe of beggars would long since have died of starvation . <end>
<start> Why Beggars still Live . <end>
<start> The greatest of is cowardice . <end>
<start> How the Thinker Makes Use of a Conversation . <end>
<start> Without being eavesdroppers , we can hear a good deal if we are able to see well , and at the same time to let ourselves occasionally get out of our own sight . <end>
<start> But people do not know how to make use of a conversation . <end>
<start> They pay far too much attention to what they want to say and reply , whereas the true listener is often contented to make a provisional answer and to say something merely as a payment on account of politeness , but on the other hand , with his memory lurking in ambush , carries away with him all that the other said , together with his tones and gestures in speaking . <end>
<start> In ordinary conversation every one thinks he is the leader , just as if two ships , sailing side by side and giving each other a slight push here and there , were each firmly convinced that the other ship was following or even being towed . <end>
<start> The Art of Excusing Oneself . <end>
<start> If some one excuses himself to us , he has to make out a very good case , otherwise we readily come to feel ourselves the culprits , and experience an unpleasant emotion . <end>
<start> Impossible Intercourse . <end>
<start> The ship of your thoughts goes too deep for you to be able to travel with it in the waters of these friendly , decorous , obliging people . <end>
<start> There are too many shallows and sandbanks : you would have to tack and turn , and would find yourself continually at your wits end , and they would soon also be in perplexity as to your perplexity , the reason for which they cannon divine . <end>
<start> The Fox of Foxes . <end>
<start> A true fox not only calls sour the grapes he cannon reach , but also those he has reached and snatched from the grasp of others . <end>
<start> In Intimate Intercourse . <end>
<start> However closely men are connected , there are still all the four quarters of the heavens in their common horizon , and at times they become aware of this fact . <end>
<start> The Silence of Disgust . <end>
<start> Behold ! <end>
<start> some one undergoes a thorough and painful transformation as thinker and human being , and makes a public avowal of the change . <end>
<start> And those who hear him see nothing , and still believe he is the same as before ! <end>
<start> This common experience has already dis gusted many writers . <end>
<start> They had rated the intellectuality of mankind too highly , and made a vow to be silent as soon as they became aware of their mistake . <end>
<start> Business Seriousness . <end>
<start> The business of many rich and eminent men is their form of recreation from too long periods of habitual leisure . <end>
<start> They then become as serious and impassioned as other people do in their rare moments of leisure and amusement . <end>
<start> The Eye s Double Sense . <end>
<start> Just as a sudden scaly ripple runs over the waters at your feet , so there are similar sudden uncertainties and ambiguities in the human eye . <end>
<start> They lead to the question : is it a shudder , or a smile , or both ? <end>
<start> Positive and Negative . <end>
<start> This thinker needs no one to refute him he is quite capable of doing that himself . <end>
<start> The Revenge of the Empty Nets . <end>
<start> Above all we should beware of those who have the bitter feeling of the fisherman who after a hard day s work comes home in the evening with nets empty . <end>

<start> Bearers of Light . <end>

<start> When a man has been highly honoured and has eaten a little , he is most benevolent . <end>
