<start> The Hypochondriac . <end>
<start> The hypochondriac is a man who has just enough intellect and pleasure in the intellect to take his sorrows , his losses , and his mistakes seriously . <end>
<start> But the field on which he grazes is too small : he crops it so close that in the end he has to look for single stalks . <end>
<start> Thus he finally becomes envious and avaricious and only then is he unbearable . <end>
<start> Giving in Return . <end>
<start> Hesiod advises us to give the neighbour who has helped us good measure and , if possible , fuller measure in return , as soon as we have the power . <end>
<start> For this is where the neighbour s pleasure comes in , since his former benevolence brings him interest . <end>
<start> Moreover , he who gives in return also has his pleasure , inasmuch as , by giving a little more than he got , he redeems the slight humiliation of being compelled to seek aid . <end>
<start> More Subtle than is Necessary . <end>
<start> Our sense of observation for how far others perceive our weaknesses is far more subtle than our sense of observation for the weaknesses of others . <end>
<start> It follows that the first named sense is more subtle than is necessary . <end>
<start> A Kind of Bright Shadows . <end>
<start> Close to the nocturnal type of man we almost regularly find , as if bound up with him , a bright soul . <end>
<start> This is , as it were , the negative shadow cast by the former . <end>
<start> Not to take Revenge . <end>
<start> There are so many subtle sorts of revenge that one who has occasion to take revenge can really do or omit to do what he likes . <end>
<start> In any case , the whole world will agree , after a time , that he has avenged himself . <end>
<start> Hence the avoidance of revenge is hardly within man s power . <end>
<start> He must not even so much as say that he does not want to do so , since the contempt for revenge is interpreted and felt as a sublime and exquisite form of revenge . <end>
<start> It follows that we must do nothing superfluous . <end>
<start> The Mistake of Those who Pay Homage . <end>
<start> Every one thinks he is paying a most agreeable compliment to a thinker when he says that he himself hit upon exactly the same idea and even upon the same expression . <end>
<start> The thinker , however , is seldom delighted at hearing such news , nay , rather , he often becomes distrustful of his own thoughts and expressions . <end>
<start> He silently resolves to revise both some day . <end>
<start> If we wish to pay homage to any one , we must beware of expressing our agreement , for this puts us on the same level . <end>
<start> Often it is a matter of social tact to listen to an opinion as if it were not ours or even travelled beyond the limits of our own horizon as , for example , when an old man once in a while opens the storehouse of his acquired knowledge . <end>
<start> Letters . <end>
<start> A letter is an unannounced visit , and the postman is the intermediary of impolite surprises . <end>
<start> Every week we ought to have one hour for receiving letters , and then go and take a bath . <end>
<start> Prejudiced . <end>
<start> Some one said : I have been prejudiced against myself from childhood upwards , and hence I find some truth in every censure and some absurdity in every eulogy . <end>
<start> Praise I generally value too low and blame too high . <end>
<start> THE WANDERER AND HIS SHADOW . <end>
<start> The Path to Equality . <end>
<start> A few hours of mountain climbing make a blackguard and a saint two rather similar creatures . <end>
<start> Weariness is the shortest path to equality and fraternity and finally liberty is bestowed by sleep . <end>
<start> Calumny . <end>
<start> If we begin to trace to its source a real scandalous misrepresentation , we shall rarely look for its origin in our honourable and straightforward enemies for if they invented anything of the sort about us , they , as being our enemies , would gain no credence . <end>
<start> Those , however , to whom for a time we have been most useful , but who , from some reason or other , may be secretly sure that they will obtain no more from us such persons are in a position to start the ball of slander rolling . <end>
<start> They gain credence , firstly , because it is assumed that they would invent nothing likely to do them damage secondly , because they have learnt to know us intimately . <end>
<start> As a consolation , the much slandered man may say to himself : Calumnies are diseases of others that break out in your body . <end>
<start> They prove that Society is a moral organism , so that you can prescribe to yourself the cure that will in the end be useful to others . <end>
<start> The Child s Kingdom of Heaven . <end>
<start> The happiness of a child is as much of a myth as the happiness of the Hyperboreans of whom the Greeksfabled . <end>
<start> The Greeks supposed that , if indeed happy ness dwells anywhere on our earth , it must certainly dwell as far as possible from us , perhaps over yonder at the edge of the world . <end>
<start> Old people have the same thought if man is at all capable of being happy , he must be happy as far as possible from our age , at the frontiers and beginnings of life . <end>
<start> For many a man the sight of children , through the veil of this myth , is the greatest happiness that he can feel . <end>
<start> He enters himself into the forecourt of heaven when he says , Suffer the little children to come unto me , for of them is the kingdom of heaven . <end>
<start> The myth of the child s kingdom of heaven holds good , in some way or other , wherever in the modern world some sentimentality exists . <end>
<start> The Impatient . <end>
<start> It is just the growing man who does not want things in the growing stage . <end>
<start> He is too impatient for that . <end>
<start> The youth will not wait until , after long study , suffering , and privation , his picture of men and things is complete . <end>
<start> Accordingly , he confidently accepts another picture that lies ready to his hand and is recommended to him , and pins his faith to that , as if it must give him at once the lines and colours of his own painting . <end>
<start> He presses a philosopher or a poet to his bosom , and must from that time forth perform long stretches of forced labour and renounce his own self . <end>
<start> He learns much in the process , but he often forgets what is most worth learning and knowing his self . <end>
<start> He remains all his life a partisan . <end>
<start> ldl Ah , a vast amount of tedious work has to be done before you find your own colours , your own brush , your own canvas ! <end>
<start> Even then you are very far from being a master in the art of life , but at least you are the boss in your own workshop . <end>
<start> There are no Teachers . <end>
<start> As thinkers we ought only to speak of self teaching . <end>
<start> The instruction of the young by others is either an experiment performed upon something as yet unknown and unknowable , or else a thorough levelling process , in order to make the new member of society conform to the customs and manners that prevail for the time being . <end>
<start> In both cases the result is accordingly unworthy of a thinker the handiwork of parents and teachers , whom some valiantly honest person has called u nos enemies natures . <end>
<start> One day , when , as the world thinks , we have long since finished our education , we discover ourselves . <end>
<start> Then begins the task of the thinker , and then is the time to summon him to our aid not as a teacher , but as a self taught man who has experience . <end>
<start> Sympathy with Youth . <end>
<start> We are sorry when we hear that some one who is still young is losing his teeth or growing blind . <end>
<start> If we knew all the irrevocable and hopeless feelings hidden in his whole being , how great our sorrow would be ! <end>
<start> Why down really suffer on this account ? <end>
<start> Because youth has to continue the work we have undertaken , and every flaw and failing in its strength is likely to injure our work , that will fall into its hands . <end>
<start> It is the sorrow at the imperfect guarantee of our immortality : or , if we only feel ourselves as executors of the human mission , it is the sorrow that this mission must pass to weaker hands than ours . <end>
<start> The Ages OF Life . <end>
<start> The comparison of the four ages of life with the four seasons of the year is a venerable piece of folly . <end>
<start> Neither the first twenty nor the last twenty years of a life correspond to a season of the year , assuming that we are not satisfied with drawing a parallel between white hair and snow and similar colour analogies . <end>
<start> The first twenty years are a preparation for life in general , for the whole year of life , a sort of long New Year s Day . <end>
<start> The last twenty review , assimilate , bring into union and harmony all that has been experienced till then : as , in a small degree , we do on every New Year s Eve with the whole past year . <end>
<start> But in between there really lies an interval which suggests a comparison with the seasons the time from the twentieth to the fiftieth year to speak here of decades in the lump , while it is an understood thing that every one must refine for himself these rough outlines . <end>
<start> Those three decades correspond to three seasons summer , spring , and autumn . <end>
<start> Winter human life has none , unless we like to call the unfortunately often intervening hard , cold , lonely , hopeless , unfruitful periods of disease the winters of man . <end>
<start> The twenties , hot , oppressive , stormy , impetuous , exhausting years , when we praise the day in the evening , when it is over , as we wipe the sweat from our foreheads years in which work seems to us cruel but necessary these twenties are the summer of life . <end>
<start> The thirties , on the other hand , are its spring time , with the air now too warm , now too cold , ever restless and stimulating , bubbling sap , bloom of leaves , fragrance of buds everywhere , many delightful mornings and evenings , work to which the song of birds awakens us , a true work of the heart , a kind of joy in our own robustness , strengthened by the savour of hopeful anticipation . <end>
<start> Lastly the forties , mysterious like all that is stationary , like a high , broad plateau , traversed by a fresh breeze , with a clear , cloudless sky above it , which always has the same gentle look all day and half the night the time of harvest and cordial gaiety that is the autumn of life . <end>
<start> Women s Intellect in Modern Society . <end>
<start> What women nowadays think of men s intellect may be divined from the fact that in their art of adornment they think of anything but of emphasising the intellectual side of their faces or their single intellectual features . <end>
<start> On the contrary , they conceal such traits , and understand , for example by an arrangement of their hair over their forehead , how to give themselves an appearance of vivid , eager sensuality and materialism , just when they but slightly possess those qualities . <end>
<start> Their convict tion that intellect in women frightens men goes so far that they even gladly deny the keenness of the most intellectual sense and purposely invite the reputation of short . <end>
<start> They think they will thereby make men more confiding . <end>
<start> It is as if a soft , attractive twilight were spreading itself around them . <end>
<start> Great and Transitory . <end>
<start> What moves the observer to tears is the rapturous look of happiness with which a fair young bride gazes upon her husband . <end>
<start> We feel all the melancholy of autumn in thinking of the greatness and of the transitoriness of human happiness . <end>
<start> Sense and Sacrifice . <end>
<start> Many a woman has the intellect del and no longer enjoys life when her husband refuses to sacrifice her . <end>
<start> With all her wit , she then no longer knows whither ? <end>
<start> and without perceiving it , is changed from sacrificial victim to sacrificial priest . <end>
<start> The Unfeminine . <end>
<start> Stupid as a man , say the women Cowardly as a woman , say the men . <end>
<start> Stupidity in a woman is unfeminine . <end>
<start> Masculine and Feminine Temperament and Mortality . <end>
<start> That the male sex has a worse A transposition of help intellect , the Jesuit maxim . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> temperament than the female follows from the fact that male children have a greater mortality than female , clearly because they leap out of their skins more easily . <end>
<start> Their wildness and soon make all the bad stuff in them deadly . <end>
<start> The Age of Cyclopean Building . <end>
<start> The democratisation of Europe is a restless force . <end>
<start> Even he who would stem the tide uses those very means that democratic thought first put into men s hands , and he makes these means more handy and workable . <end>
<start> The most inveterate enemies of democracy I mean the spirits of upheaval seem only to exist in order , by the fear that they inspire , to drive forward the different parties faster and faster on the democratic course . <end>
<start> Now we may well feel sorry for those who are working consciously and honourably for this future . <end>
<start> There is something dreary and monotonous in their faces , and the grey dust seems to have been wafted into their very brains . <end>
<start> Nevertheless , posterity may possibly some day laugh at our anxiety , and seen the of several generations what we see in the building of stone dams and walls an activity that necessarily covers clothes and face with a great deal of dust , and perhaps unavoidably makes the workmen , too , a little dull fitted but who would on that account desire such work undone ? <end>
<start> It seems that the democratisation of Europe is a link in the chain of those mighty prophylactic principles which are the thought of the modern era , and whereby we rise up in revolt against the Middle Ages . <end>
<start> Now , and now only , is the age of Cyclopean building ! <end>
<start> A final security in the foundations , that the future may build on them without danger ! <end>
<start> Henceforth , an impossibility of the orchards of culture being once more destroyed overnight by wild , senseless mountain torrents ! <end>
<start> Dams and walls against barbarians , against plagues , against physical and spiritual serfdom ! <end>
<start> And all this understood at first roughly and literally , but gradually in an ever higher and more spiritual sense , so that all the principles here indicated may appear as the intellectual preparation of the highest artist in horticulture , who can only apply himself to his own task when the other is fully accomplished ! <end>
<start> True , if we consider the long intervals of time that here lie between means and end , the great , supreme labour , straining the powers and brains of centuries , that is necessary in order to create or to provide each individual means , we must not bear too hardly upon the workers of the present when they loudly proclaim that the wall and the fence are already the end and the final goal . <end>
<start> After all , no one yet sees the gardener and the fruit , for whose sake the fence exists . <end>
<start> The Right of Universal Suffrage . <end>
<start> The people has not granted itself universal suffrage but , wherever this is now in force , it has received and accepted it as a temporary measure . <end>
<start> But in any case the people has the right to restore the gift , if it does not satisfy its anticipations . <end>
<start> This faction seems universal nowadays , for when , at any occasion where the vote is exercised , scarce two-thirds , nay perhaps not even the majority of all voters , go to the polls , that very fact is a vote against the whole suffrage system . <end>
<start> On this point , in fact , we must pronounce a much sterner verdict . <end>
<start> A law that enacts that the majority shall decide as to the welfare of all cannon be built up on the foundation that it alone has provided , for it is bound to require a far broader foundation , namely the unanimity of all . <end>
<start> Universal suffrage must not only be the expression of the will of a majority , but of the whole country . <end>
<start> Thus the dissent of a very small minority is already enough to set aside the system as impracticable and the abstention from voting is in fact a dissent of this kind , which ruins the whole institution . <end>
<start> The absolute veto of the individual , or not to be too minute the veto of a few thousands , hangs over the system as the consequence of justice . <end>
<start> On every occasion when it is employed , the system must , according to the variety of the division , first prove that it has still a right to exist . <end>
<start> False Conclusions . <end>
<start> What false conclusions are drawn in spheres where we are not at home , even by those of us who are accustomed as men of science to draw right conclusions ! <end>
<start> It is humiliating ! <end>
<start> Now it is clear that in the great turmoil of worldly doings , in political affairs , in all sudden and urgent matters such as almost every day brings up , these false conclusions must decide . <end>
<start> For no one feels at home with novelties that have sprung up in the night . <end>
<start> All political work , even with great statesmen , is an improvisation that trusts to luck . <end>
<start> Premisses of the Age of Machinery . <end>
<start> The press , the machine , the railway , the telegraph are premisses of which no one has yet dared to draw the conclusions that will follow in a thousand years . <end>
<start> A Drag upon Culture . <end>
<start> When we are told that here men have no time for productive occupations , because military manoeuvres and processions take up their days , and the rest of the population must feed and clothe them , their dress , however , being striking , often gay and full of absurdities that there only a few distinguished qualities are recognised , individuals resemble each other more than elsewhere , or at any rate are treated as equals , yet obedience is exacted and yielded without reasoning , for men command and make no attempt to convince that here punishments are few , but these few cruel and likely to become the final and most terrible that there treason ranks as the capital offence , and even the criticism of evils is only ventured on by the most audacious that there , again , human life is cheap , and ambition often takes the form of setting life in danger when we hear all this , we at once say , This is a picture of a barbarous society that rests on a hazardous footing . <end>
<start> One man perhaps will add , It is a portrait of Sparta . <end>
<start> But another will become meditative and declare that this is a description of our modern military system , as it exists in the midst of our altogether different culture and society , a living anachronism , the picture , as above said , of a community resting on a hazardous footing a posthumous work of the past , which can only act as a drag upon the wheels of the present . <end>
<start> Yet at times even a drag upon culture is vitally necessary that is to say , when culture is advancing too rapidly downhill or as perhaps in this case uphill . <end>
<start> More Reverence for Them that Know . <end>
<start> In the competition of production and sale the public is made judge of the product . <end>
<start> But the public has no special knowledge , and judges by the appearance of the wares . <end>
<start> In consequence , the art of appearance and perhaps the taste for it must increase under the dominance of competition , while on the other hand the quality of every product must deteriorate . <end>
<start> The result will be so far as reason does not fall in value that one day an end will be put to that competition , and a new principle will win the day . <end>
<start> Only the master of the craft should pronounce a verdict on the work , and the public should be dependent on the belief in the personality of the judge and his honesty . <end>
<start> Accordingly , no anonymous work ! <end>
<start> At least an expert should be there as guarantor and pledge his name if the name of the creator is lacking or is unknown . <end>
<start> The cheapness of an article is for the layman another kind of illusion and deceit , since only durability can decide that a thing is cheap and to what an extent . <end>
<start> But it is difficult , and for a layman impossible , to judge of its durability . <end>
<start> Hence that which produces an effect on the eye and costs little at present gains the advantage this being naturally machine made work . <end>
<start> Again , machinery that is to say , the cause of the greatest rapidity and facility in production favours the most saleable kind of article . <end>
<start> Otherwise it involves no tangible profit it would be too little used and too often stand idle . <end>
<start> But as to what is most saleable , the public , as above said , decides : it must be the most exchangeable in other words , the thing that appears good and also appears cheap . <end>
<start> Thus in the domain of labour our motto must also hold good : More respect for them that know ! <end>
<start> The Danger of Kings . <end>
<start> Democracy has it in its power , without any violent means , and only by a lawful pressure steadily exerted , to make kingship and emperorship hollow , until only a zero remains , perhaps with the significance of every zero in that , while nothing in itself , it multiplies a number tenfold if placed on the right side . <end>
<start> Kingship and emperorship would remain a gorgeous ornament upon the simple and appropriate dress of democracy , a beautiful superfluity that democracy allows itself , a relic of all the historically venerable , primitive ornaments , nay the symbol of history itself , and in this unique position a highly effective thing if , as above said , it does not stand alone , but is put on the right side . <end>
<start> In order to avoid the danger of this qualification , kings hold by their teeth to their dignity as war lords . <end>
<start> To this end they need wars , or in other words exceptional circumstances , in which that slow , lawful pressure of the democratic forces is relaxed . <end>
<start> The Teacher a Necessary Evil . <end>
<start> Let us have as few people as possible between the productive minds and the hungry and recipient minds ! <end>
<start> The middlemen almost unconsciously the food which they supply . <end>
<start> For their work as middlemen they want too high a fee for themselves , and this is drawn from the original , productive spirits namely , interest , admiration , leisure , money , and other advantages . <end>
<start> Accordingly , we should always look upon the teacher as a necessary evil , just like the merchant as an evil that we should make as small as possible . <end>
<start> Perhaps the prevailing distress in Germany has its main cause in the fact that too many wish to live and live well by trade in other words , desiring as far as possible to diminish prices for the producer and raise prices for the consumer , and thus to profit by the greatest possible loss to both . <end>
<start> In the same way , we may certainly trace a main cause of the prevailing intellectual poverty in the superabundance of teachers . <end>
<start> It is because of teachers that so little is learnt , and that so badly . <end>
<start> The Tax of Homage . <end>
<start> Him whom we know and honour , be he physician , artist , or artisan , who does and produces something for us , we gladly pay as highly as we can , often a fee beyond our means . <end>
<start> On the other hand , we pay the unknown as low a price as possible here is a contest in which every one struggles and makes others struggle for a foot s breadth of land . <end>
<start> In the work of the known there is something that cannon be bought , the sentiment and ingenuity put into his work for our own sake . <end>
<start> We think we cannon better express our sense of obligation than by a sort of sacrifice on our part . <end>
<start> The heaviest tax is the tax of homage . <end>
<start> The more competition prevails , the more we buy for the unknown and work for the unknown , the lower does this tax become , whereas it is really the standard for the loftiness of man s spiritual intercourse . <end>
<start> The Means towards Genuine Peace . <end>
<start> No government will nowadays admit that it maintains an army in order to satisfy occasionally its passion for conquest . <end>
<start> The army is said to serve only defensive purposes . <end>
<start> This morality , which justifies self defence , is called in as the government s advocate . <end>
<start> This means , however , reserving morality for ourselves and immorality for our neighbour , because he must be thought eager for attack and conquest if our state is forced to consider means of self-defence . <end>
<start> At the same time , by our explanation of our need of an army because he denies the lust of attack just as our state does , and ostensibly also maintains his army for defensive reasons , we proclaim him a hypocrite and cunning criminal , who would fain seize by surprise , without any fighting , a harmless and unwary victim . <end>
<start> In this attitude all states face each other to day . <end>
<start> They presuppose evil intentions on their neighbour s part and good intentions on their own . <end>
<start> This hypothesis , however , is an inhuman notion , as bad as and worse than war . <end>
<start> Nay , at bottom it is a challenge and motive to war , foisting as it does upon the neighbouring state the charge of immorality , and thus provoking hostile intentions and acts . <end>
<start> The doctrine of the army as a means of self defence must be abjured as completely as the lust of conquest . <end>
<start> Perhaps a memorable day will come when a nation renowned in wars and victories , distinguished by the highest development of military order and intelligence , and accustomed to make the heaviest sacrifice to these objects , will voluntarily exclaim , We will break our swords , and will destroy its whole military system , lock , stock , and barrel . <end>
<start> Making ourselves defenceless after having been the most strongly defended from a loftiness of sentiment that is the means towards genuine peace , which must always rest upon a pacific disposition . <end>
<start> The so called armed peace that prevails at present in all v countries is a sign of a bellicose disposition , of a disposition that trusts neither itself nor its neighbour , and , partly from hate , partly from fear , refuses to lay down its weapons . <end>
<start> Better to perish than to hate and fear , and twice as far better to perish than to make oneself hated and feared this must some day become the supreme maxim of every political community ! <end>
<start> Our liberal representatives of the people , as is well known , have not the time for reflection on the nature of humanity , or else they would know that they are working in vain when they work for a gradual diminution of the military burdens . <end>
<start> ofthe contrary , when the distress of these burdens is greatest , the sort of God who alone can help here will be nearest . <end>
<start> The tree of military glory can only be destroyed at one swoop , with one stroke of lightning . <end>
<start> But , as you know , lightning comes from the cloud and from above . <end>
<start> Whether Property can be squared with JUSTICE . <end>
<start> When the injustice of property is strongly felt and the hand of the great clock is once more at this place , we formulate two methods of relieving this injustice : either an equal distribution , or an abolition of private possession and a return to State ownership . <end>
<start> The latter method is especially dear to the hearts of our Socialists , who are angry with that primitive Jew for saying , Thou shalt not steal . <end>
<start> In their view the eighth commandment should rather run , Thou shalt not possess . <end>
<start> The former method was frequently tried in antiquity , always indeed on a small scale , and yet with poor success . <end>
<start> From this failure we too may learn . <end>
<start> Equal plots of land is easily enough said , but how much bitterness is aroused by the necessary division and separation , by the loss of time honoured possessions , how much piety is wounded and sacrificed ! <end>
<start> We uproot the foundation of morality when we uproot boundary stones . <end>
<start> Again , how much fresh bitterness among the new owners , how much envy and looking askance ! <end>
<start> For there have never been two The original , by a curious slip , has seventh . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> really equal plots of land , and if there were , man s envy of his neighbour would prevent him from believing in their equality . <end>
<start> And how long would this equality , unhealthy and poisoned at the very roots , endure ? <end>
<start> In a few generations , by inheritance , here one plot would come to five owners , there five plots to one . <end>
<start> Even supposing that men acquiesced in such abuses through the enactment of stern laws of inheritance , the same equal plots would indeed exist , but there would also be needy malcontents , owning nothing but dislike of their kinsmen and neighbours , and longing for a general upheaval . <end>
<start> If , however , by the second method we try to restore ownership to the community and make the individual but a temporary tenant , we interfere with agriculture . <end>
<start> For man is opposed to all that is only a transitory possession , blessed with his own care and sacrifice . <end>
<start> With such property he behaves in fashion , as robber or as worthless spendthrift . <end>
<start> When Plato declares that self seeking would be removed with the abolition of property , we may answer him that , if self seeking be taken away , man will no longer possess the four cardinal virtues either as we must say that the most deadly plague could not injure mankind so terribly as if vanity were one day to disappear . <end>
<start> Without vanity and self seeking what are human virtues ? <end>
<start> By this I am far from meaning that these virtues are but varied names and masks for these two qualities . <end>
<start> Plato s Utopian refrain , which is still sung by Socialists , rests upon a deficient knowledge of men . <end>
<start> He lacked the historical science of moral emotions , the insight into the origin of the good and useful characteristics of the human soul . <end>
<start> He believed , like all antiquity , in good and evil as in black and white that is to say , in a radical difference between good and bad men and good and bad qualities . <end>
<start> In order that property may henceforth inspire more confidence and become more moral , we should keep open all the paths of work for small fortunes , but should prevent the effortless and sudden acquisition of wealth . <end>
<start> Accordingly , we should take all the branches of transport and trade which favour the accumulation of large fortunes especially , therefore , the money market out of the hands of private persons or private companies , and look upon those who own too much , just as upon those who own nothing , as types fraught with danger to the community . <end>
<start> THE VALUE OF LABOUR . <end>
<start> If we try to determine the value of labour by the amount of time , industry , good or bad will , constraint , inventiveness or laziness , honesty or make believe bestowed upon it , the valuation can never be a just one . <end>
<start> For the whole personality would have to be thrown into the scale , and this is impossible . <end>
<start> Here the motto is , Judge not ! <end>
<start> But after all the cry for justice is the cry we now hear from those who are dissatisfied with the present valuation of labour . <end>
<start> If we reflect further we find every person non responsible for his product , the labour hence merit can never be derived therefrom , and every labour is as good or as bad as it must be through this or that necessary concatenation of forces and weaknesses , abilities and desires . <end>
<start> The worker is not at liberty to say whether he shall work or not , or to decide how he shall work . <end>
<start> Only the standpoints of usefulness , wider and narrower , have created the valuation of labour . <end>
<start> What we at present call justice does very well in this sphere as a highly refined utility , which does not only consider the moment and exploit the immediate opportunity , but looks to the permanence of all conditions , and thus also keeps in view the well being of the worker , his physical and spiritual contentment : in order that he and his posterity may work well for our posterity and become trustworthy for longer periods than the individual span of human life . <end>
<start> The exploitation of the worker was , as we now understand , a piece of folly , a robbery at the expense of the future , a of society . <end>
<start> We almost have the war now , and in any case the expense of maintaining peace , of concluding treaties and winning confidence , will henceforth be very great , because the folly of the exploiters was very great and long lasting . <end>
<start> Of the Study of the Social Body . <end>
<start> The worst drawback for the modern student of economics and political science in Europe , and especially in Germany , is that the actual conditions , instead of exemplifying rules , illustrate exceptions or stages of transition and extinction . <end>
<start> We must therefore learn to look beyond actually existing conditions and , for example , turn our eyes to distant North America , where we can still contemplate and investigate , if we will , the initial and normal movement of the socialHUMAN , ALL TOO HUMAN . <end>
<start> body . <end>
<start> In Germany such a study requires arduous and historical research , or , as I have suggested , a telescope . <end>
<start> How far Machinery humiliated . <end>
<start> Machinery is impersonal it robs the piece of work of its pride , of the individual merits and defects that cling to all work that is not machine made in other words , of its bit of humanity . <end>

<start> Destiny and the Stomach . <end>
<start> A piece more or less of bread and butter in the jockey s body is occasionally the decisive factor in races and bets , and thus in the good and bad luck of thousands . <end>
<start> So long as the destiny of nations depends upon diplomats , the stomachs of diplomats will always be the object of patriotic misgivings . <end>
<start> Quousque tandem . <end>
<start> The Victory of Democracy . <end>
<start> All political powers nowadays attempt to exploit the fear of Socialism for their own strengthening . <end>
<start> Yet in the long run democracy alone gains the advantage , for parties are now compelled to flatter the masses and grant them facilities and liberties of all kinds , with the result that the masses finally become omnipotent . <end>
<start> The masses are as far as possible removed from Socialism as a doctrine of altering the acquisition of property . <end>
<start> I f once they get the steering wheel into their hands , through great majorities in their Parliaments , they will attack with progressive taxation the whole dominant system of capitalists , merchants , and financiers , and will in fact slowly create a middle class which may forget Socialism like a disease that has been overcome . <end>
<start> The practical result of this increasing democratisation will next be a European league of nations , in which each individual nation , delimited by the proper geographical frontiers , has the position of a canton with its separate rights . <end>
<start> Small account will be taken of prehistoric memories of previously existing nations because the pious affection for these memories will be gradually uprooted under the democratic regime , with all its craze for novelty and experiment The corrections of frontiers that will prove necessary will be so carried out as to serve the interests of the great cantons and at the same time that of the whole federation , but not that of any venerable memories . <end>
<start> To find the standpoints for these corrections will be the task of future diplomats , who will have to be at the same time students of civilisation , agriculturists , and commercial experts , with no armies but motives and utilities at their back . <end>
<start> Then only will foreign and home politics be inseparably connected , whereas to day the latter follows its haughty dictator , and leans in sorry baskets the stubble that is left over from the harvest of the former . <end>
<start> Goal and Means of Democracy . <end>
<start> Democracy tries to create and guarantee independence for as many as possible in their opinions , way of life , and occupation . <end>
<start> For this purpose democracy must withhold the political suffrage both from those who have nothing and from those who are really rich , as being the two intolerable classes of men . <end>
<start> At the removal of these classes it must always work , because they are continually calling its task in question . <end>
<start> In the same way democracy must prevent all measures that seem to aim at party organisation . <end>
<start> For the three great foes of independence , in that threefold sense , are the have nots , the rich , and the : i parties . <end>
<start> I speak of democracy as of a thing to come . <end>
<start> What at present goes by that name is distinguished from older forms of government only by the fact that it drives with new horses the roads and the wheels are the same as of yore . <end>
<start> Has the danger really become less with these conveyances of the commonwealth ? <end>
<start> Discretion and Success . <end>
<start> That great quality of discretion , which is fundamentally the virtue of virtues , their ancestress and queen , has in common life by no means always success on its side . <end>
<start> The wooed would find himself deceived if he had wooed that virtue only for the sake of success . <end>
<start> For it is rated by practical people as suspicious , and is confused with cunning and hypocrisy : he who obviously lacks discretion , the man who quickly grasps and sometimes misses his grasp , has prejudice on his side he is an honest , trustworthy fellow . <end>
<start> Practical people , accordingly , do not like the prudent man , thinking he is to them a danger . <end>
<start> Moreover , we often assume the prudent man to be anxious , preoccupied , pedantic unpractical , butterfly people find him uncomfortable , because he does not live in their happy go lucky way , without thinking of actions and duties he appears among them as their embodied conscience , and the bright day is dimmed to their eyes before his gaze . <end>
<start> Thus when success and popularity fail him , he may often say by way of private consolation , So high are the taxes you have to pay for the possession of the most precious of human commodities still it is worth the price ! <end>
<start> Et in Arcadia Ego . <end>
<start> I looked down , over waves of hills , to a milky green lake , through firs and pines austere with age rocky crags of all shapes about me , the soil gay with flowers and grasses . <end>
<start> A herd of cattle moved , stretched , and expanded itself before me single cows and groups in the distance , in the clearest evening light , hard by the forest of pines others nearer and darker all in calm and eventide contentment . <end>
<start> My watch pointed to half past six . <end>
<start> The bull of the herd had stepped into the white foaming brook , and went forward slowly , now striving against , now giving way to his tempestuous course thus , no doubt , he took his sort of fierce pleasure . <end>
<start> Two dark brown beings , of Bergamasque origin , tended the herd , the girl dressed almost like a boy . <end>
<start> On the left , overhanging cliffs and fields of snow above broad belts of woodland to the right , two enormous ice covered peaks , high above me , shimmering in the veil of the sunny haze all large , silent , and bright . <end>
<start> The beauty of the whole was awe inspiring and induced to a mute worship of the moment and its revelation . <end>
<start> Unconsciously , as if nothing could be more natural , you peopled this pure , clear world of light which had no trace of yearning , of expectancy , of looking forward or backward with Greek heroes . <end>
<start> You felt it all as Poussin and his school felt at once heroic and idyllic . <end>
<start> So individual men too have lived , constantly feeling themselves in the world and the world in themselves , and among them one of the greatest men , the inventor of a heroic idyllic form of philosophy Epicurus . <end>
<start> Counting and Measuring . <end>
<start> The art of seeing many things , of weighing one with another , of reckoning one thing with another and constructing from them a rapid conclusion , a fairly correct sum that goes to make a great politician or general or merchant . <end>
<start> This quality is , in fact , a power of speedy mental calculation . <end>
<start> The art of seeing one thing alone , of finding therein the sole motive for action , the guiding principle of all other action , goes to make the hero and also the fanatic . <end>
<start> This quality means a dexterity in measuring with one scale . <end>
<start> Not to See too Soon . <end>

<start> At Noontide . <end>
<start> He to whom an active and . <end>
<start> stormy morning of life is allotted , at the of life feels his soul overcome by a strange longing for a rest that may last for months and years . <end>
<start> All grows . <end>
<start> silent around him , voices sound farther and farther in the distance , the sun shines straight down upon him . <end>
<start> On a hidden woodland sward he sees the great God Pan sleeping , and with Pan Nature seems to him to have gone to sleep with an expression of eternity on their faces . <end>
<start> He wants nothing , he troubles about nothing his heart stands still , only his eye lives . <end>
<start> Jt is a death with waking eyes . <end>
<start> Then man . <end>
<start> sees much that he never saw before , and , so far as his eye can reach , all is woven into and as it were buried in a net of right . <end>
<start> He feels happy , but it is a heavy , very heavy kind of happiness . <end>
<start> Then at last the wind stirs in the trees , is over , life carries him away again , life with its blind eyes , and its tempestuous retinue behind it desire , illusion , oblivion , enjoyment , destruction , decay . <end>
<start> And so comes . <end>
<start> evening , more stormy and more active than was even the morning . <end>
<start> To the really active man these prolonged phases of cognition seem almost uncanny and morbid , but not unpleasant . <end>

<start> At Once Richer and Poorer . <end>
<start> I know a man who accustomed himself even in childhood to think w r ell of the intellectuality of mankind in other words , of their real devotion as regards things of the intellect , their unselfish preference for that which is recognised as true but who had at the same time a modest or even deprecatory view of his own brain judgment , memory , presence of mind , imagination . <end>
<start> He set no value on himself when he compared himself with others . <end>

<start> Such , in fact , was to some extent the case , but , as he once said , Yet a bitterness of the deepest dye is mingled with my feeling , such as I did not know in earlier life for since I learnt to value men and myself more correctly , my intellect seems to me of less use . <end>

<start> I now always see before me the frightful gulf between those who could give help and those who need help . <end>

<start> Being very Clever . <end>
<start> Being very clever keeps men young , but they must put up with being considered , for that very reason , older than they are . <end>
<start> For men read the handwriting of the intellect as signs of experience that is , of having lived much and evilly , of suffering , error , and repentance . <end>
<start> Hence , if we are very clever and show it , we appear to them older and wicked than we are . <end>
<start> How we must Conquer . <end>
<start> We ought not to desire victory if we only have the prospect of overcoming our opponent by a hair s breadth . <end>
<start> A good victory makes the vanquished rejoice , and must have about it something divine which spares humiliation . <end>
<start> An Illusion of Superior Minds . <end>
<start> Superior minds find it difficult to free themselves from an illusion for they imagine that they excite envy among the mediocre and are looked upon as ex captions . <end>
<start> As a matter of fact , however , they are looked upon as superfluous , as something that would not be missed if it did not exist . <end>
<start> Demanded by Cleanliness . <end>
<start> Changing opinions is in some natures as much demanded by cleanliness as changing clothes . <end>
<start> In the case of other natures it is only demanded by vanity . <end>
<start> Also Worthy of a Hero . <end>
<start> Here is a hero who did nothing but shake the tree as soon as the fruits were ripe . <end>
<start> Do you think that too small a thing ? <end>
<start> Well , just look at the tree that he shook . <end>
<start> A Gauge for Wisdom . <end>
<start> The growth of wisdom may be gauged exactly by the diminution of ill-temper . <end>
<start> Expressing an Error disagreeably . <end>
<start> It is not to every one s taste to hear truth pleasantly expressed . <end>
<start> But let no one at least believe that error will become truth if it is disagreeably expressed . <end>
<start> The Golden Maxim . <end>
<start> Man has been bound with many chains , in order that he may . <end>
<start> forget to comfort himself like an animal . <end>
<start> And indeed he has become more gentle , more intellectual , more joyous , more meditative than any animal . <end>
<start> But now he still suffers from having carried his chains so long , from having been so long without pure air and free movement these chains , however , are , as I repeat again and again , the ponderous and significant errors of moral , religious , and metaphysical ideas . <end>
<start> Only when the disease of chains is overcome is the first great goal reached the separation of man from the brute . <end>
<start> At present we stand in the midst of our work of removing the chains , and in doing so we need the strictest precautions . <end>
<start> Only the ennobled man may be granted freedom of spirit to him alone comes the alleviation of life and heals his wounds he is the first who can say that he lives for the sake of joy , with no other aim in any other mouth , his motto of Peace around me and goodwill towards all the most familiar things , would be dangerous . <end>
<start> In this motto for single individuals he is thinking of an ancient saying , magnificent and pathetic , which applied to all , and has remained standing above all mankind , as a motto and a beacon whereby shall perish all who adorn their banner too early the rock on which Christianity foundered . <end>
<start> It is not even yet time , it seems , for all men to have the lot of those shepherds who saw the heavens lit up above them and heard the words : Peace on earth and goodwill to one another among The Shadow . <end>
<start> Of all that you have enunciated , nothing pleased me more than one promise : Ye want again to be good neighbours to the most familiar things . <end>
<start> This will be to the advantage of us poor shadows too . <end>
<start> For do but confess that you have hitherto been only too fond of reviling us . <end>
<start> The Wanderer . <end>
<start> Reviling ? <end>
<start> But why did you never defend yourselves ? <end>
<start> After all , you were very close to our ears . <end>
<start> The Shadow : It seemed to us that we were too near you to have a right to talk of ourselves . <end>
<start> The Wanderer . <end>
<start> What delicacy ! <end>
<start> Ah , you shadows are better men than we , I can see that . <end>
<start> The Shadow : And yet you called us importunate us , who know one thing at least extremely well : how to be silent and to wait no Englishman knows it better . <end>
<start> It is true we are very , very often in the retinue of men , but never as their bondsmen . <end>
<start> When man shuns light , we shun man so far , at least , we are free . <end>
<start> The Wanderer : Ah , light shuns man far oftener , and then also you abandon him . <end>
<start> The Shadow : It has often pained me to leave you . <end>
<start> I am eager for knowledge , and much in man has remained obscure to me , because I cannon always be in his company . <end>
<start> At the price of complete knowledge of man I would gladly be your slave . <end>
<start> The Wanderer : Do you know , do I know , whether you would not then unwittingly become master in An allusion to the poem Der Wilde The Savage by same , which ends with the line , sheet , wir wilden sind loch lesser genscher Behold , after all , we savages are better men . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> stead of slave ? <end>
<start> Or would remain a slave indeed , but would lead a life of humiliation and disgust because you despised your master ? <end>
<start> Let us both be content with freedom such as you have enjoyed up to now you and I ! <end>
<start> For the sight of a being not free would emitter my greatest joys all that is best would be repugnant to me if any one had to share it with me I will not hear of any slaves about me . <end>
<start> That is why I do not care for the dog , that lazy , parasite , who first became dogfish as the slave of man , and of whom they still say that he is loyal to his master and follows him like The Shadow : Like his shadow , they say . <end>
<start> Perhaps I have already followed you too long to day ? <end>
<start> It has been the longest day , but we are nearing the end be patient a little more ! <end>
<start> The grass is damp I am feeling chilly . <end>
<start> The Wanderer : Oh , is it already time to part ? <end>
<start> And I had to hurt you in the end I saw you became darker . <end>
<start> The shadow : I blushed the only colour I have at command . <end>
<start> I remembered that I had often lain at your feet like a dog , and that you then The Wanderer : Can I not with all speed do something to please you ? <end>
<start> Have you no wish ? <end>
<start> The Shadow : None , except perhaps the wish that the philosophic dog expressed to Alexander the Great just move a little out of my light I feel cold . <end>
